## פתח דבר

## Foreword

In connection with the 19th of Kislev, the Rosh Hashanah of Chassidus, which marks two hundred years since the first printing of the Tanya (20 Kislev 5557)—and noting that this year also marks two hundred and fifty years (Rosh Hashanah 5507) since the Baal Shem Tov’s ascent to the celestial chamber of Mashiach, and his question, "When will the Master come?" to which he was answered, "When your wellsprings spread outward"—and in the days of Chanukah, from which we draw inspiration, as the miracle is linked to the triumph and victory of the wisdom of Torah (and more specifically, the "oil" of Torah—the inner secrets of the Torah)—we hereby publish this volume, in continuation of the Likkutei Sichos volumes already released: Likkutei Sichos Volume 36, on Sefer Shemos (the second volume of the ninth book of Likkutei Sichos).

***

This volume includes:

a) The Likkutei Sichos on the parshiyos of the Torah (and the Yomim Tovim) of the time of reading Sefer Shemos, which were printed in individual kuntresim on a weekly basis throughout the years 5750–5752.

b) Letters whose content relates to the weekly parshiyos (and Yomim Tovim) included in this book. It is worth noting that the section for 24 Teves includes a collection of letters and explanations on the Tanya; 10 Shevat includes the customs of the yahrtzeit; and Terumah includes matters concerning the beis medrash, etc.

c) At the end of the book, a collection of letters regarding medical matters—an addition to Parshas Mishpatim. (Due to the volume of material, these were printed separately at the end of the book and divided into sections.)

Let it be known: some of the letters in this volume have been printed in the "Igros Kodesh" series from the Rebbe Melech HaMashiach, which is currently being published in chronological order (to date, 23 volumes have been released). At the request of many, we have collected here those letters whose content pertains to the weekly parshiyos (and Yomim Tovim) of Sefer Shemos.

d) For the benefit of the reader, we have added at the end of the book (as in volumes 30–35) an index of subjects, pesukim, Chazal, and seforim.

***

May it be the will of Hashem that we merit soon to publish further sichos and maamarim of the Rebbe Melech HaMashiach, and may the merit of studying and printing his holy Torah increase the strengthening of our hiskashrus with the Rebbe Melech HaMashiach. And in this month of Teves—the month in which "the body derives pleasure from the body," where the "body" from above, the Essence and Being of the Almighty, derives pleasure (so to speak) from the "body" (from below) of every single Yid, which refers (primarily) to the state and situation of the future to come, when the neshamah will be nourished by the body, and even more so, the pleasure of the Essence (so to speak) from the body—may this come to fruition in actual, revealed form with the Geulah Amitis V'Sheleimah, the beginning of the Geulah and its completion, through Mashiach Tzidkeinu—"a king from the House of Dovid will arise, etc.," until he "will rectify the entire world to serve Hashem together, as it is said: 'For then I will turn to the peoples... to serve Him with one accord.'"

And the Rebbe Melech HaMashiach will arise and redeem us and lead us upright to our Holy Land, and we will learn Torah from the mouth of Mashiach Tzidkeinu, "a new Torah will go forth from Me", and "your eyes shall behold the King in His beauty" in the revelation of his kingship to the eyes of all, immediately and literally, "and Hashem shall be King over all the earth". Amen, may it be His will.

In anticipation of the spreading of the sichos,
5 Teves, 5757
Brooklyn, N.Y.

## תוכן ומפתח

### Shemos

Sicha 1 (5751) . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . [1](@apppath/admur/lkus/36/1/1/1.aspx)
The necessity for the two interpretations in Rashi on the verse (2:14) "And Moshe feared"; the words of the Midrash (Bereishis Rabbah, beginning of ch. 70) "Two people were promised by Hashem, yet they were afraid," and the debate among the commentators whether this is to their credit or if "it would have been appropriate not to fear"; a discussion regarding the definition of the attribute of bitachon and the foundation of certainty for a person's bitachon, even though it is possible that sin may have caused [a negative outcome]; an explanation of the Tzemach Tzedek’s adage, "Tracht gut, vet zein gut" (Think good, and it will be good).

Sicha 2 (5752) . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . [7](@apppath/admur/lkus/36/1/2/7.aspx)
"What merit do they possess that I should be able to take them out? ... By the merit of the Torah that they are destined to receive, etc." (Shemos Rabbah 3:4) — How does the merit of their future acceptance of the Torah enable their exodus from Egypt? An explanation of the Aggadah cited in the Ran (end of Pesachim) that "at the time Moshe told them... 'you shall serve [Hashem],' each one counted for himself," and the version of the Shibbolei HaLeket that Moshe told them "you shall serve" while they were still in Egypt; the essence of the longing of Yisrael for the acceptance of the Torah according to pnimiyus ha'inyanim.

### 20–24 Teves (5750)

. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . [16](@apppath/admur/lkus/36/2/1/16.aspx)
An explanation of the Alter Rebbe’s language in his Hilchos Talmud Torah (ch. 2, halacha 11) regarding the obligation to study the halachos of the mitzvos that are not practiced in our time "in the compilation of the Rambam"; several approaches to the definition of the obligation of Talmud Torah and the difference in this regard between the approach of the Rambam and the approach of the Alter Rebbe, and their respective interpretations of "one-third in Talmud"; the Alter Rebbe’s approach that there is a preference for studying the definitive halachos of the entire Torah over studying a few halachos with their reasons in a brief manner — and an encouragement to study a set daily portion in the Rambam’s Mishneh Torah.

### Va'eira

Sicha 1 (5752) . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . [26](@apppath/admur/lkus/36/3/1/26.aspx)
Rashi on the verse (9:8) s.v. "And Moshe threw"—an analysis of Rashi’s language "many miracles," even though "he only counts two"; a discussion on whether the actual formation of the boils was miraculous; the unique innovation in the purpose of the plague of boils, which is why "many miracles" occurred specifically in this plague.

Sicha 2 (5751) . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . [33](@apppath/admur/lkus/36/3/2/33.aspx)
The connection of the conclusion of the Haftorah—"And I will give you an opening of the mouth, etc." (Yechezkel 29:21)—to the content of the parsha; several explanations regarding the intent of the plagues and the matter of Pharaoh’s hardened heart, and the mediation between them; an explanation of the principle that the entire world is "for the sake of Yisrael and for the sake of the Torah" (Rashi, beginning of Bereishis).

### Rosh Chodesh Shevat (5750)

. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . [38](@apppath/admur/lkus/36/4/1/38.aspx)
"In the eleventh month, on the first of the month... Moshe began to explain this Torah, etc." (beginning of Devarim)—"He explained it to them in seventy languages" (Rashi on the verse); why was it necessary to translate the Torah for Bnei Yisrael into seventy languages? The application of the name "Torah" to Torah matters studied and written in other languages; the connection to the definition of "Mishneh Torah" that "Moshe said it from his own mouth" (Megillah 31b; Zohar III 261a) and the day of the Hillula of the Rayatz (on the tenth of Shevat).

### Bo

Sicha 1 (5752) . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . [45](@apppath/admur/lkus/36/5/1/45.aspx)
Rashi (12:12) s.v. "And I will pass through" (and the difference from the language of the Mechilta there); the difference between "I will go out into the midst of Egypt" (11:4) and "And I will pass through, etc."; the miracle of "And I will skip over you" according to the literal meaning of the verse; the two aspects of Hashem’s "going out" into Egypt according to pnimiyus ha'inyanim and the practical lesson in the avodah of each person in drawing the hearts of Yisrael closer to their Father in Heaven.

Sicha 2 (5750) . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . [53](@apppath/admur/lkus/36/5/2/53.aspx)
The title "Tzevaos Hashem" given to Yisrael when they left Egypt; the debate in the Bavli (Shevuos 35b) and Maseches Sofrim (4:1; Yerushalmi Megillah 1:7) regarding the opinion of R' Yosi that "Tzevaos" is not among the names that may not be erased—according to halacha and according to drush.

Sicha 3 (5751) . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . [59](@apppath/admur/lkus/36/5/3/59.aspx)
Rashi on the verse (13:13) s.v. "And you shall break its neck"—the connection between the two points in Rashi: (1) breaking its neck with a hatchet, etc., (2) "he caused the Kohen to lose his money, etc."; an analysis of Rashi’s language "he will cause his money to be lost" (and not "will be lost," as in the Gemara (Bechoros 10b) and the practical halachic difference; the definition of the redemption of the firstborn donkey according to the simple meaning.

### Beshalach

Sicha 1 (5750) . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . [65](@apppath/admur/lkus/36/6/1/65.aspx)
Rashi on the verse (15:16) s.v. "Terror shall fall upon them" and s.v. "And dread"—terror upon those who are far and dread upon those who are near, according to the simple meaning and in the avodah of man.

Sicha 2 (5752) . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . [70](@apppath/admur/lkus/36/6/2/70.aspx)
The Alter Rebbe’s language in Shulchan Aruch Orach Chayim (363) that the Melaveh Malkah meal "is not so much an obligation, but rather a mitzvah min hamuvchar only"; a discussion on the source and reason for the Melaveh Malkah meal; the two definitions of "sanctified with the Manna" and "blessed with the Manna" (Bereishis Rabbah 11:2); an explanation of the words of the Beis Yosef (Tur Orach Chayim 300) that there is a limb in a person that "derives no pleasure from eating except on Motzaei Shabbos."

Sicha 3 (5751) . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . [77](@apppath/admur/lkus/36/6/3/77.aspx)
The difference between the approach of Rashi on the Torah and the Mechilta (16:26) regarding the reason the Manna did not descend on Yom Tov; a discussion on the obligation of Lechem Mishneh, and an explanation of the Rambam’s language in Hilchos Berachos (7:4) and Hilchos Shabbos (30:7); the definition of the obligation of Lechem Mishneh on Yom Tov.

### 15 Shevat (5751)

. . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . [82](@apppath/admur/lkus/36/7/1/82.aspx)
"There are four Rosh Hashanahs: on the first of Nissan is the Rosh Hashanah for kings and festivals... on the first of Shevat is the Rosh Hashanah for trees..."—the connection between the Rosh Hashanah for "kings" and "festivals" (which occur on the same day), and their special relevance to the Rosh Hashanah for trees; the reason the festivals were set according to the times of the year's produce and the superiority of trees over seeds.

### Yisro

Sicha 1 (5751) . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . [86](@apppath/admur/lkus/36/8/1/86.aspx)
Rashi on the verse (19:6) s.v. "These are the words"; a discussion on the interpretation of the verse (19:3) "Thus shall you say to the House of Yaakov and tell the Bnei Yisrael" according to Rashi on the Torah, and the difference between Rashi’s language and the Mechilta there.

Sicha 2 (5752) . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . [90](@apppath/admur/lkus/36/8/2/90.aspx)
An explanation of the Ramban’s words (20:13) that honoring father and mother is among the five commandments "regarding the honor of the Creator... for He commanded the honor of the Creator to honor the father who participates in creation"; the words of the Chida (Pischei Einayim to Kiddushin 31a) that the mitzvah of honoring father and mother refuted the opinion of the nations of the world that "a person demands his own honor"; a discussion on why there is no definition of (a form of) partnership in honoring father and mother.

Sicha 3 (5750) . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . [96](@apppath/admur/lkus/36/8/3/96.aspx)
The reason the first commandment after Matan Torah is "You shall not make with Me, etc." (20:20); the innovation of Matan Torah in the manner of bitul to Hashem; an explanation in the teachings of the Alter Rebbe (cited in Sefer HaSichos 5699, p. 337 and onwards) regarding the negative commandment "You shall not make with Me" and the positive commandment "An earthen altar you shall make for Me, etc."—that the negative commandment here precedes the positive one because "absence precedes existence," and the reason the Alter Rebbe did not explain (like the Abarbanel and others) that the matter of bitul is hinted at in "an earthen altar."

### Mishpatim

Sicha 1 (5752) . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . [102](@apppath/admur/lkus/36/9/1/102.aspx)
The opinion of the Rambam on the law of "a secondary domain" (Hilchos Nizkei Mamon 4:5 and 6:6) and the changes in the Rambam’s language (in the two aforementioned halachos); the debate between Rashi and the Meiri (Bava Kamma 39a) on the definition of "a secondary domain"; the law of "a secondary domain" in pnimiyus ha'inyanim.

Sicha 2 (5751) . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . [109](@apppath/admur/lkus/36/9/2/109.aspx)
Rashi (23:15) s.v. "The month of spring" and the changes in Rashi at the end of Parshas Va'eira and later in Tisa (34:18), and Rashi’s silence in Parshas Bo (13:4); a discussion on Rashi in Parshas Re'eh (16:1) regarding when the year is intercalated.

Sicha 3 (5750) . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . [115](@apppath/admur/lkus/36/9/3/115.aspx)
Rashi (24:6) s.v. "In basins"—a discussion on the manner of bringing Bnei Yisrael into the covenant according to Rashi’s approach to the simple meaning of the verse.

### Terumah

Sicha 1 (5752) . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . [123](@apppath/admur/lkus/36/10/1/123.aspx)
The innovation in the explanation of the Tzemach Tzedek (Ohr HaTorah on our parsha, p. 1442) that the matter of "it never moves" said regarding the Sanctuary (Vayikra Rabbah 2:2)—is because it is written "a minor sanctuary... these are the synagogues" (Megillah 29a)—regarding the Rambam’s explanation in Sefer HaMitzvos (positive mitzvah 176) and other explanations in this maamar; the approach of the Zohar (II 59b, III 26a) that the positive mitzvah of "and they shall make Me a sanctuary" also includes building a synagogue; the matter of "and I will dwell among them... within each and every one of Yisrael" in halacha.

Sicha 2 (5751) . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . [131](@apppath/admur/lkus/36/10/2/131.aspx)
Rashi (26:5) on the manner of placing the curtains on the Sanctuary—the reason Rashi brought the opinion of the Beraisa of 47 measures, even though "the text in this parsha" supports the opinion of the Shas (Shabbos 98b), and later (26:12) s.v. "And the excess that remains" he cited only the opinion of the Beraisa.

Sicha 3 (5750) . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . [136](@apppath/admur/lkus/36/10/3/136.aspx)
The definition of the "Tarskin cubit" according to the Rambam (Hilchos Beis HaBechirah 4:2) that in the Second Temple "they left an extra cubit between the Holy and the Holy of Holies and did not build a wall... but made two curtains, etc."; a discussion on his language there "but in the First Temple there was only one curtain"; the words of the Zohar (II 4a) that counted the "Dvir" as one of the parts of the Sanctuary and the practical halachic difference from the explanation of this in Likkutei Levi Yitzchak on the Zohar there.

### Tetzaveh

Sicha 1 (5751) . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . [146](@apppath/admur/lkus/36/11/1/146.aspx)
Rashi (28:10) s.v. "According to their generations"—the innovation in Rashi "in the order they were born"; the Rambam’s approach (Hilchos Kelei HaMikdash 9:7) to "according to their generations"—according to the order of their birth from the mothers; the difference between the two aforementioned approaches in pnimiyus ha'inyanim.

Sicha 3 (5752) . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . [153](@apppath/admur/lkus/36/11/2/153.aspx)
The debate between the Rambam and the Ra'avad (end of Hilchos Kilayim) whether kilayim was permitted in the priestly garments in the Sanctuary only during the time of avodah and whether kilayim existed only in the sash or also in the other garments of the Kohen Gadol, and the uniqueness of the sash—according to halacha and in pnimiyus ha'inyanim; an explanation of the language of the Bachya (Kedoshim 19:19) on the reason for the prohibition of kilayim.

### Purim

Sicha 1 (5750) . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . [161](@apppath/admur/lkus/36/12/1/161.aspx)
Achashverosh’s doubt "What to do with Queen Vashti, for she did not do the king’s command, etc."; Rashi (1:8) s.v. "He established" and s.v. "Over all the great ones of his house"; the unique innovation in this feast according to Rashi (the simple meaning of the verse).

Sicha 2 (5751) . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . [167](@apppath/admur/lkus/36/12/2/167.aspx)
The three reasons why we do not say Hallel on Purim (Megillah 14a)—(1) the miracle of Purim happened outside the Land, (2) the reading of the Megillah is in place of Hallel, (3) we are still servants of Achashverosh—and the practical difference in the avodah of man.

### Tisa

Sicha 1 (5750) . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . [172](@apppath/admur/lkus/36/13/1/172.aspx)
"A person should never deviate from custom, for Moshe went up to the heavens and did not eat bread" (Bava Metzia 86b)—the changes in this between the language of the Shas, Shemos Rabbah (47:5), and Bereishis Rabbah (48:14); three approaches to the absence of eating by Moshe Rabbeinu on the mountain, and the three interpretations (Shemos Rabbah 47:7) of what Moshe’s food was on the mountain—and the mediation between them.

Sicha 2 (5752) . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . [180](@apppath/admur/lkus/36/13/2/180.aspx)
An explanation in Likkutei Levi Yitzchak on the Zohar of our parsha (II, p. 140) that the obligation to say a statement in the name of its author is only "when the one saying the statement told it to him," unlike if he did not hear it from him—for seemingly the prohibition of "do not steal the poor" still exists (Magen Avraham Orach Chayim 52:2); the reason we find many statements of our Sages that were not said in the name of their author; the connection between saying a statement in the name of its author and "acquiring Torah."

### Vayakhel

Vayakhel (5752) . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . [187](@apppath/admur/lkus/36/14/1/187.aspx)
One who ignites any amount is liable, provided he needs the ashes (Rambam, Hilchos Shabbos 12:1); the reason the Rambam began with the condition of "needs the ashes"—even though the primary liability for igniting "is not because of the burning and destruction of the wood, but because of the increase of the fire" (Alter Rebbe in Shulchan Aruch, Hilchos Yom Tov 505, Kuntres Acharon 2)—in pnimiyus ha'inyanim.

### Vayakhel — Pekudei

Vayakhel — Pekudei (5751) . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . [192](@apppath/admur/lkus/36/15/1/192.aspx)
The debate between the Ramban (beginning of Vayakhel) and the Rambam (beginning of Hilchos Beis HaBechirah) whether the primary intent of the Sanctuary is that "His presence should be among them" or for the service of the sacrifices; the connection to their debate whether the resting of the Presence in the Sanctuary was in a way of passing through or in a way of enclothement; an explanation of the words of the Zohar (III 73a) regarding the two connections between Yisrael and the Holy One, blessed be He—revealed and hidden.

### Parshas HaChodesh

Vayakhel-Pekudei — Parshas HaChodesh (5750) . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . [199](@apppath/admur/lkus/36/16/1/199.aspx)
"I did not choose a city from all the tribes of Yisrael to build a house, etc., but I chose David, etc." (I Kings 8:15-16—in the Haftorah of Parshas Pekudei)—the connection between the choice of Yerushalayim and the choice of David (according to Zohar Vayakhel 198a); the change in the Rambam’s language regarding the eternity of Yerushalayim (Commentary on the Mishnah, Zevachim 14:8; Hilchos Beis HaBechirah 1:3); an explanation of the Midrash’s language (Shemos Rabbah 15:11) "When the Holy One, blessed be He, chose His world, etc., and when He chose Yaakov and his sons, He established in it the Rosh Chodesh of redemption."

Parshas HaChodesh (5751) . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . . [206](@apppath/admur/lkus/36/17/1/206.aspx)
"One should not interrupt between Parah and HaChodesh... their signs... between these cups [the four cups of the Seder night]... between the third and the fourth one should not drink" (Yerushalmi Megillah 3:5)—the innovation in halacha learned from this sign, and the connection between them according to pnimiyus ha'inyanim; a discussion on the words of the Rebbe Maharash (Sefer HaMaamarim 5629) that even tzaddikim need to do teshuvah.


## שמות

## 7

A. "And Moses said to G-d, 'Who am I that I should go to Pharaoh, and that I should bring the children of Israel out of Egypt?'" Our Sages in the Midrash, and similarly Rashi in his commentary, explain that Moses’ argument, "and that I should bring the children of Israel out of Egypt," meant: "What merit do they possess that I should be able to take them out?" (In Rashi’s words: "What merit do the Israelites have that You should perform a miracle for them and I should take them out of Egypt?")

To this, the Holy One, Blessed be He, answered him: "When you bring the people out of Egypt, you shall serve G-d upon this mountain"—meaning: "Regarding what you said, 'By what merit shall I take them out of Egypt,' know that it is by the merit of the Torah that they are destined to receive through you on this mountain that they will go out from there."

We must understand the meaning of this answer, "by the merit of the Torah that they are destined to receive." How can their future reception of the Torah, which occurs after their exodus from Egypt, serve as a merit for which they are taken out of Egypt?

Now, in his second commentary on the verse here, "And He said, 'For I will be with you, and this shall be the sign for you that I have sent you: When you bring the people out of Egypt, you shall serve G-d upon this mountain,'" Rashi writes: "Another explanation: 'For I will be with you'—and the fact that you will succeed in your mission will be a sign for you regarding another promise that I am making to you: that when you take them out of Egypt, you will serve Me upon this mountain, for you will receive the Torah upon it, and it is the merit that stands for Israel. We find a similar phrasing: 'And this shall be the sign for you: eat this year what grows of itself...'—the downfall of Sennacherib will be a sign for you regarding another promise, that your land is ruined of fruits, yet I will bless the growth.'" The commentators note that Rashi’s citation of "a similar phrasing" is to bring proof that the Holy One, Blessed be He, hangs one promise upon another, even though that very promise has not yet been fulfilled. So too in our case: even though the promise of this mission itself has not yet been fulfilled, and if so, how can the promise of the mission be a proof and sign for the promise of receiving the Torah, which is a different promise? To this, he brings an example from what we find in Isaiah, where the promise of the downfall of Sennacherib, before its fulfillment, was a sign for another promise, which is the blessing of the growth.

We see from this very Rashi that it is a novel concept that a future event (which has not yet been fulfilled) can be a sign and indication. According to this, the difficulty regarding the aforementioned matter is even greater: how can it be said that the future reception of the Torah is a cause for a present matter—namely, the merit they possess for which they are taken out of Egypt?

B. Seemingly, one could say that for this reason,

## 8

Rashi changed the wording of the Midrash. In the Midrash it says, "What you said, 'By what merit shall I take them out of Egypt,' know that it is by the merit of the Torah that they are destined to receive through you on this mountain that they will go out from there." Whereas in Rashi it says, "And as for your question, 'What merit do the Israelites have that they should go out of Egypt?'—I have a 'great matter' regarding this exodus, for they are destined to receive the Torah on this mountain at the end of three months after they go out of Egypt." The fact that Rashi did not write "by the merit of the Torah" is because his intent is to explain that the exodus of the children of Israel is not because of a merit they possess now, but rather that "I have a great matter regarding this exodus." That is to say, the Holy One, Blessed be He, has a "great matter"—an important and lofty goal—for which He is taking them out of Egypt.

[And based on this, it can be said that in the Midrash, where it says "by the merit of the Torah that they are destined to receive through you on this mountain they will go out from there," the intention is not that they currently possess the merit of the future reception of the Torah, for which they are worthy of being redeemed, but rather "by the merit of the Torah" in essence, that the Torah is what provides the merit (the reason and cause) for their exodus, so that they will be able to receive the Torah (and therefore the Midrash does not say "by the merit that they are destined to receive the Torah" or the like).]

However, this requires clarification, as it is not so clear in his wording here, "And as for your question, 'What merit do the Israelites have that they should go out of Egypt?'—I have a 'great matter' regarding this exodus," for the core emphasis in answering Moses' question ("What merit...") is missing—that this is not a merit they possess now, but rather the purpose, intent, and goal of the exodus. Furthermore, in his second commentary, Rashi explicitly writes, "and it is the merit that stands for Israel."

C. It could also be said that since the Holy One, Blessed be He, knows the future, it is possible for Him to grant reward for a mitzvah even before its fulfillment. Likewise in our case, since

## 9

the Holy One, Blessed be He, knew that they were destined to receive the Torah, He took them out from the beginning for the sake of the future merit.

But this explanation also requires study, because when the future act is to be performed by the person himself, it is possible for it to be considered a merit. But in our case, the Giving of the Torah was not due to the choice of the children of Israel, as the Sages say, "The Holy One, Blessed be He, suspended the mountain over them like a barrel and said to them, 'If you accept the Torah, it is well, and if not, there shall be your burial,'" and therefore "from here there is a great protest against the Torah." Thus, the Giving of the Torah is not something that depended on their will and choice, and if so, there is no room for reward for this act.

Furthermore, since the Holy One, Blessed be He, said, "When you bring the people out of Egypt, you shall serve G-d upon this mountain," it turns out that the children of Israel were compelled in this by the very word of the Holy One, Blessed be He.

[And this is not similar to the fact that the Holy One, Blessed be He, knows the future, yet this knowledge does not compel human choice (as explained in many places)—for it is known that this applies to the knowledge of the Holy One, Blessed be He, whereas the Divine speech, which is a revealed matter, does compel choice (as explained regarding the concept of "a righteous person and a wicked person He does not say," that even though the Holy One, Blessed be He, knows if one will be righteous or wicked, since this knowledge does not come via speech ("saying"), it does not compel human choice). And based on this, since in our case the Holy One, Blessed be He, said, "You shall serve G-d upon this mountain," the children of Israel were compelled in this, and it is impossible to give them reward for this act.]

And this is not a contradiction to the principle of free choice [that "it is a great principle and a pillar of the Torah and the mitzvos... that the authority is in your hands, and whatever a person desires to do of the deeds of men, he does, whether good or evil... for the Creator does not force people nor decree

## 10

upon them to do good or evil, but everything is handed over to them"]—because this principle is stated only in relation to the conduct of an individual, but not regarding the Giving of the Torah (to the entire Jewish people), as we find explicitly, as mentioned above, that the entire essence of the Giving of the Torah was not due to their choice, but through the compulsion of the Holy One, Blessed be He.

If so, the difficulty remains: seemingly, how can it be considered a merit for the children of Israel that they received the Torah, since it was not by their choice but they were compelled to accept it at the time of the Giving of the Torah? And furthermore, they were compelled in this from the moment of the word of G-d, "When you bring the people out of Egypt, you shall serve G-d upon this mountain"—and if so, how was the exodus from Egypt a reward for the Giving of the Torah (and the entire concept of reward and punishment is based on the principle of free choice, as explained by Maimonides)?

Therefore, it seems to me that in the words of our Sages here, there is an allusion to an additional matter beyond the mere reception of the Torah by Israel, a matter that is within their choice, and for which they received the reward of the exodus from Egypt.

D. This will be understood by prefacing the words of the "Haggadah" brought by several early authorities regarding the content of the mitzvah of counting the Omer between Pesach and Atzeres (in our times): "At the time that Moses our teacher, peace be upon him, said to them, 'You shall serve G-d upon this mountain,' the Israelites said to him, 'Moses our teacher, when is this service?' He said to them, 'At the end of fifty days,' and each and every one of them counted for himself (from here the Sages established to count the Omer, etc.)."

We must examine when Moses said to the Israelites, "You shall serve G-d upon this mountain." From the simple reading of the aforementioned Aggadah, that Moses said to them "at the end of fifty days," it implies that this was upon their exodus from Egypt. However, in *Shibolei HaLeket*, this "Midrash Aggadah" is brought in different language: "Because when the Israelites were informed that they would go out of Egypt, they were informed that they were destined to receive the Torah at the end of fifty days from their exodus, as it is said, 'When you bring the people out of Egypt, you shall serve G-d upon this mountain.' The letter *nun* in *ta'avdun* (you shall serve) is extra, to tell you that at the end of fifty days you shall serve G-d (you shall receive) the Torah. And Israel, out of great affection, would count every single day and say, 'Today is one day,' 'Today is the second day,' and so on for all of them, because it seemed to them like a long time due to their great affection for the matter (therefore the counting was established for generations)."

It is explicit that Moses said to them, "You shall serve G-d upon this mountain," "when the Israelites were informed that they would go out of Egypt," which was while they were still in Egypt, as recounted in our *parsha*, that this was the first mission of Moses our teacher, to gather the elders of Israel and say to them, "I have surely remembered you, etc., I will bring you up, etc." According to the version of *Shibolei HaLeket*, Moses then informed them that they were destined to receive the Torah ("You shall serve G-d, etc."). And he also emphasizes there in his language that Moses said

## 11

to them, "that they are destined to receive the Torah at the end of fifty days from their exodus," which implies that this speech was before the exodus from Egypt.

And this is also logical, for even though Moses was not explicitly commanded by the Holy One, Blessed be He, to inform the children of Israel of this, since Moses our teacher was a lover of Israel, why would Moses our teacher refrain from informing Israel of such good news for such a long time [at least ten months, for the duration of each plague was approximately a month, as Rashi says, "The plague serves for a quarter of a month, and for three parts he would warn and testify to them," and even according to the opinion that the last three plagues did not last a month, there was a certain amount of time between the delivery of the first mission to the elders of Israel (in our *parsha*) and the beginning of the plagues, as is understood from what is recounted at the end of our *parsha*].

E. Now, based on this, that while they were still in Egypt the Israelites were informed that they were destined to receive the Torah—and this news aroused in the children of Israel a great desire to receive the Torah, out of great affection—it is understood that this desire began immediately, while they were still in Egypt.

Even though the counting of the days began only after the exodus from Egypt (and according to the "Haggadah" in the other aforementioned early authorities, only then did they ask Moses, "When is this service?"), and not before that while they were in Egypt—this is because Moses said to them explicitly, "When you bring... you shall serve." If so, it is understood and simple that before they went out of Egypt there was no place (to ask "When is this service" and) to count the days, but the desire itself (due to the greatness of the affection) was aroused in Israel immediately when they were informed of this good news.

One should also emphasize this from the parable of the Midrash (brought in the *poskim*), "A man who was imprisoned in a dungeon, etc., the king set a time for him to give him his daughter, and he would count until the time arrived," and in another place, the matter is brought in more detail, that the king said to "a man thrown into a pit and imprisoned there," "I will release you and take you out of this pit, and at such-and-such a time I will give you my daughter (as a wife), and that man rejoiced with great joy. He said, 'It is not enough that he is taking me out of this pit, but he even wants to give me his daughter.' And the king did so; he took him out of the pit and dressed him in fine garments, etc. When that man saw that the king had already fulfilled part of his words, he began to count immediately how much time remained for what the king had set for him to give him his daughter... So were the Israelites in Egypt like in a pit, etc." It is clear that the counting of the days began only after the king fulfilled part of his words and took him out of the pit, even though the joy of the prisoner began immediately when he was informed of the good news. From this, it is understood in our case that the joy of the children of Israel began while they were still in Egypt when they were informed about their exodus from Egypt and the reception of the Torah, but the counting of the days began only after the actual exodus.

Based on all the above, we can explain the words of (our Sages and) Rashi: "And as for your question, 'What merit do the Israelites have?'—I have a 'great matter' regarding this exodus, for they are destined to receive the Torah on this mountain at the end of three months after they go out of Egypt."

By the fact that the Holy One, Blessed be He, informed them that they would receive the Torah after they went out of Egypt, there was aroused in them a great longing and yearning for the reception of the Torah, and this desire is certainly considered a great merit for Israel [for this matter (the desire of Israel to receive the Torah) did not come explicitly in the word of Hashem, who only said to Moses, "You shall serve G-d upon this mountain"].

Based on this, one can say even more: because of this, the actual reception of the Torah at Mount Sinai is also considered a merit (as we find in many places that this is the praise and virtue of Israel, that they received the Torah at Mount Sinai)—even though He suspended the mountain over them like a barrel, and furthermore, the very word of Hashem, "You shall serve G-d upon this mountain," already compelled the matter of the reception of the Torah (as mentioned above)—because the matter of the reception of the Torah "began" from the longing and yearning of Israel to receive the Torah while they were still in Egypt, a matter that came from their own will and choice. This itself revealed that even their act of reception at Mount Sinai was not (only) a matter of compulsion and force, but there is in it

## 13

also an act of reception from their own will and choice.

And this is the meaning of "What merit do the Israelites have... they are destined to receive the Torah," that this is a merit that preceded the exodus from Egypt and is the cause and merit for which they went out, since the beginning of the reception of the Torah is from the longing for it while they were in Egypt, as mentioned above.

F. Now, it is known that every cause and effect has an inner and essential connection between them. In our case, since the longing of Israel for the Giving of the Torah is the cause and merit for their exodus from Egypt, there must be a connection between them, and it is akin to the concept of the exodus from Egypt.

This can be explained (in the inner dimension of the matters):
It is known that the meaning of the exodus from Egypt is not only the physical departure from the land of Egypt in the literal sense, but the departure from all spiritual *mitzrayim* (limitations) and boundaries. That is, not only from the limitation of the *yetzer hara*, and the body and animal soul of the person (*mitzrayim* of *kelipah*), which conceal and hide the light of his soul and prevent the person from the service of Hashem, but also from the *mitzrayim* and boundaries that exist within *kedushah* itself (*mitzrayim* of *kedushah*). For when the service of Hashem is based solely on the person's understanding and comprehension or on the emotion of his attributes (love and fear of Hashem), he is still in a state of "limitation," because his service is in measurement and limitation, according to the measurements of his understanding and comprehension or the emotion of his attributes, as our Rebbe the Alter Rebbe writes in *Tanya* that "even... a completely righteous person who serves Hashem with fear and love in delights... is not nullified... completely... but is a thing unto itself," for his service is limited according to the mode of his "existence."

The true meaning of the exodus from Egypt, the departure from all limitations and boundaries (even of *kedushah*), is when the person's service is from a state of complete *bitul*, where the person has no existence for himself at all, but is nullified to the Holy One, Blessed be He, as it is written, "For to Me the children of Israel are servants; they are My servants whom I brought out from the land of Egypt." By the fact that the Holy One, Blessed be He, took the children of Israel out of the land of Egypt, by that very fact the Israelites became servants of Hashem, for a servant has no existence for himself but is nullified to his master. This is the true exodus from all limitations and boundaries, when the person is at the level of a servant, nullified in the ultimate sense to the Holy One, Blessed be He.

Based on this, the connection is understood between the merit of Israel—that while they were still in Egypt they longed to receive the Torah—and the concept of the exodus from Egypt. For in the Holy One, Blessed be He's notification to Moses, it is explicit, "When you bring... you shall serve G-d upon this mountain," meaning not just a reception of Torah and mitzvos, but the main emphasis is on the matter of servitude, that Israel would become servants of Hashem. For this servitude, Israel longed while they were still in Egypt, from which it is understood that this longing indicates the greatness of the *bitul* of the

## 14

Israelites to the Holy One, Blessed be He, the opposite of the concept of "existence." For even though they were then in a state of exile in the literal sense, in the land of Egypt and in a state of servitude to Pharaoh, nevertheless they longed not only to be free men, but to "serve G-d," that they would be enslaved to the Holy One, Blessed be He, in great *bitul* and servitude, the opposite of the concept of Egypt—limitations and boundaries (even of *kedushah*).

G. Based on this, one can also explain the connection of the two answers to the two questions of Moses: 1) "Who am I" — "What importance do I have to speak with kings," 2) "And that I should bring the children of Israel" — "And even if I am important, what merit do the Israelites have that You should perform a miracle for them and I should take them out of Egypt?" — to which the Holy One, Blessed be He, answered, "Regarding what you said, 'Who am I that I should go to Pharaoh,' it is not yours, but rather Mine, for I will be with you, etc. And regarding your question, 'What merit do the Israelites have that they should go out of Egypt,' I have a 'great matter' regarding this exodus, for they are destined to receive the Torah on this mountain."

Based on the above, it can be said that there is an inner connection and bond between them, for both indicate the matter of *bitul* and the absence of existence, which is the "merit" and cause for the exodus from Egypt, as mentioned above.

Explanation of the matter:
The inner content in the answer of the Holy One, Blessed be He, to Moses, "It is not yours but Mine," is that it refers to what Moses said, "Who am I, what importance do I have to speak with kings." To this, the Holy One, Blessed be He, said to him that indeed it is not a matter of his importance for which he was chosen to be the messenger of Hashem to redeem Israel; "It is not yours but Mine, for I will be with you," for the whole thing does not relate to Moses at all, but is entirely Mine. Consequently, there is no discussion at all regarding the virtue and importance of Moses, for the redemption is of the Holy One, Blessed be He, and when Moses goes to Pharaoh, it is the going of the Holy One, Blessed be He, as it were, for "I will be with you."

And similarly regarding the second matter, "What merit do the Israelites have that they should go out of Egypt," to which the Holy One, Blessed be He, said, "I have a 'great matter' regarding this exodus," for the discussion here is not about the merit and virtue of Israel, but that "I have a 'great matter' regarding this exodus," for the entire matter is nothing but the affair of the Holy One, Blessed be He.

The merit required from the children of Israel to be worthy of this "great matter" which is "Mine" (of the Holy One, Blessed be He) is the matter of *bitul*, which is the content of the longing of Israel while they were still in Egypt to "serve G-d," which indicates the ultimate *bitul* of theirs, as explained at length above.

H. Now, since the Giving of the Torah is the purpose and goal of the exodus from Egypt, it must be that at the Giving of the Torah they ascended to a higher level in the aforementioned *bitul*. But seemingly we find the opposite, that not only did they not ascend regarding the *bitul* that was already in them in Egypt (in their longing to "serve, etc."), and especially after the exodus from Egypt when they became "servants of Hashem," but on the contrary, at the time of the Giving of the Torah, it was necessary for Him to "suspend the mountain over them like a barrel," to compel them to accept the Torah.

One must say that not only is this not a contradiction to the above, but it is the very reason, for the suspension of the mountain over them like a barrel is a continuation of their longing and *bitul* to the Holy One, Blessed be He, as a preparation for the Giving of the Torah, which at the Giving of the Torah came to its perfection.

For in Rashi on the verse, "I am Hashem your G-d who brought you out of the land of Egypt," it says, "The exodus is sufficient that you should be enslaved to Me."

## 15

And it is implied that the main servitude and *bitul* occurred at the time of the Giving of the Torah. It must be said that at the time of the Giving of the Torah, the children of Israel ascended, for even though immediately upon their exodus from Egypt they became "servants of Hashem," through the Giving of the Torah they ascended to a higher level.

One can say the explanation for this:
Regarding the matter of the *bitul* of a servant to the king, that he is not an existence for himself, but his entire existence is the existence of the king, there are several modes: when the *bitul* and acceptance of the yoke are from the side of the person, the servant, who accepted upon himself to be the servant of the king, in this *bitul* there is subtly also a mixture of the person's existence, for the cause of his being a servant and the cause of his *bitul* is from his side (the yoke he accepted of his own will). The true matter of *bitul* is when it comes not from the side of the servant, that he accepted upon himself the mastery of the king, but when it comes entirely from the side of the king himself, that the king makes him a servant.

This is the inner meaning of "He suspended the mountain over them like a barrel," for then the matter of being servants of Hashem was perfected in the ultimate sense, for their *bitul* to the Holy One, Blessed be He, did not depend on their existence (for they preceded "we will do" to "we will hear"), but from the side of the Holy One, Blessed be He. Then, it was added that the *bitul* is in their very existence, and it is also more true and internal, because they are compelled to be servants not from the side of their existence, but from the side of the Holy One, Blessed be He.

This matter was possible only after there was in the children of Israel the *bitul* of the great longing to receive the Torah, through which they reached a complete *bitul* from the side of the person to be servants of Hashem, to the point that they preceded "we will do" to "we will hear." Then, when He suspended the mountain over them like a barrel, the *bitul* from the side of the Holy One, Blessed be He, which is above their existence, was drawn down and acted upon them, and penetrated into their inner being.

I. And just as it is in the exodus from Egypt, that the merit that Israel had for the Holy One, Blessed be He, to perform a miracle for them and take them out of Egypt is their longing for the Giving of the Torah, "When you bring the people out of Egypt, you shall serve G-d upon this mountain," so too it is in the future redemption that is coming soon, that the merit of Israel is the merit of the hope and anticipation for the redemption, as it is in the Midrashim,

in which is also included the yearning and desire for the Torah of Moshiach, for "because of this all of Israel yearned... for the days of Moshiach... because in those days knowledge, wisdom, and truth will increase, as it is said, 'For the earth shall be full of the knowledge of Hashem... because that King who will arise from the seed of David will be a man of wisdom, even greater than Solomon, and a great prophet, close to Moses our teacher, and therefore he will teach all the people,"

which will be speedily in our days, immediately and truly.


## כ'—כ"ד טבת

## 16

### 20–24 Teves

a. In the Laws of Talmud Torah, Chapter Two, our Rebbe [the Alter Rebbe] elaborates on the obligation to study the entire Torah and the prohibition against forgetting words of Torah (which is why there is an obligation to review one’s studies many times, etc., so as not to forget). He writes that one whose mind is not sharp enough to learn and remember the entire Torah "must ensure that all his study is in the type of learning that leads to action—namely, the laws that every person needs to know in order to fulfill the mitzvos according to their laws and to be careful not to stumble into prohibitions, Heaven forbid."

He then continues: "If, after he is well-versed in all his study that leads to action, he is able to set aside a significant hour for other study, and the rest of the day suffices for him to review his study that leads to action, then he is obligated to add, little by little, to learn during this hour other laws—which are the explanation of the 613 mitzvos, even those that are not currently applicable, such as *Kodshim* and the like—and to review them properly, etc., as explained above, for it is said, 'All the mitzvah, etc.,' and 'It is not upon you to complete the work.' Therefore, even if this hour is not sufficient to learn these laws from the Talmud and review them thoroughly, at the very least he should learn them from the Mishnayos with their commentaries and review them properly until he remembers them, etc. And he should occupy himself first with *Seder Kodshim*, as the Sages said, 'Whoever occupies himself with the Torah of a *olah* (burnt-offering) is as if he had offered a *olah*.' Therefore, it is proper, if it is possible for him, to learn the laws of all the offerings in their entirety, and all the services in the Beis Hamikdash, and the laws of the Beis Hamikdash and all its vessels, as explained well in the work of the Rambam, of blessed memory, in *Sefer Avodah* and *Sefer Korbanos*. And afterward, in the course of time, if he is able to add more to this hour to learn and review properly, he should occupy himself with *Seder Taharos* and *Zeraim*, etc."

This requires clarification: At the beginning of the chapter, the Alter Rebbe explained that studying "fixed laws without their reasons" is not sufficient, "because if one does not know the reasons for the laws, he does not understand the essence of the laws correctly and thoroughly, and he is called a 'boor' (ignorant person). Therefore, there are those who forbid ruling [on halachic matters], even for oneself, based on fixed laws without the reasons one has learned." (And therefore, even at the beginning of his studies, a person is obligated to learn "Talmud, which explains the reasons for the laws in the Mishnayos, the *Braisos*, and the statements of the *Amora'im*, and in this time, also in the books of the early *Poskim* who explain the reasons for the fixed laws that the *Tur* and *Shulchan Aruch* have ruled, such as the *Rosh* and the *Beis Yosef*.")

## 17

[This is in accordance with the view of Rashi and the *Rosh*, and not like the view of the Rambam, "who compiled the book *Mishneh Torah* without providing the reasons for the laws at all, and made it to be used for ruling from it alone, as he wrote in his introduction there, that a person would not need any other work in the world, etc."]

If so, it is difficult to understand: Since "if one does not know the reasons for the laws, he does not understand the essence of the laws correctly and thoroughly, and he is called a 'boor,'" why does he write that "if he is able to set aside a significant hour for other study... he should learn... from the Mishnayos... [and] the work of the Rambam, of blessed memory, in *Sefer Avodah* and *Sefer Korbanos*"? Seemingly, it would be better and more correct for him to learn the laws "with their reasons in a concise manner" (from the Talmud and the *Poskim*), for then he understands the laws "correctly and thoroughly."

[Regarding the study of Mishnayos, one could say that for this reason the Alter Rebbe adds "(Mishnayos) with their commentaries," because the commentaries on the Mishnayos also include the reasons (as explained in the introduction of the *Tiferes Yisrael* to the Mishnayos). But it is still difficult to understand the continuation of the Alter Rebbe's words, that in addition to studying Mishnayos, one must also learn "the laws of all the offerings in their entirety [and not only those in the Mishnah]... in the work of the Rambam, of blessed memory"—and the work of the Rambam is "without providing the reasons for the laws at all" (as mentioned above).]

And even though if he were to also learn the reasons for the laws in a concise manner, he would lack in the quantity of study—after all, it is already a case of someone who cannot complete the entire Torah (even just the fixed laws), and it is possible that he will not be able to finish more than *Seder Kodshim* (and only if, in the course of time, "he is able to add more to this hour... he should occupy himself with *Seder Taharos*, etc."), and he already wrote there not to worry about the fact that he cannot finish the entire Torah, because "it is not upon you to complete the work" (just as he brought earlier that "there were students in their days who were not well-versed and did not know by heart more than one tractate, and some of them two tractates"). Since, in any case, "it is not upon you to complete the work," seemingly it would be better to have a study of lesser quantity but higher quality (i.e., laws with their reasons in a concise manner) than a study of fixed laws without reasons from the Mishnayos and the work of the Rambam?

b. This can be understood by first clarifying the general definition of the obligation of Torah study according to the Alter Rebbe's view in the Laws of Talmud Torah.

## 18

The Alter Rebbe writes at the beginning of Chapter 2 there: "One whose father did not teach him Torah is obligated to teach himself when he becomes aware, as it is said, 'And you shall teach them to your children and you shall observe to do them'—this is the positive commandment of Talmud Torah for every person to learn the entire Written Torah and the entire Oral Torah." He cites his source as the "Rambam from the Gemara, *Kiddushin* 29." However, in the Rambam, after he brings the teaching from "And you shall teach them... and you shall observe to do them," he adds, "And thus you find everywhere that study precedes action, because study leads to action, and action does not lead to study." The Alter Rebbe, however, omitted this conclusion.

Seemingly, the reason the Alter Rebbe omitted this addition is that, according to this logic, the definition of the obligation of Talmud Torah is that it leads to action, as the *Kesef Mishneh* explains: "Since he is obligated to perform, he is obligated to learn." This implies that the foundation of the obligation of study is because it leads to action.

Based on this, one could say that the reason the Alter Rebbe omitted this proof is because he holds (as his words continue) that the obligation is "to learn the entire Written Torah and the entire Oral Torah," and this study is not merely study that leads to action, but includes knowing the entire Torah. The definition of this mitzvah is the obligation to know the Torah for the sake of knowledge (not for the sake of action).

c. However, one cannot interpret the Rambam's view as saying that the obligation of study is only because it leads to action, for even according to the Rambam, the obligation of Torah study includes not only learning the necessary laws. As he explicitly rules, "A person is obligated to divide his study time into three: one-third in the Written Torah, one-third in the Oral Torah, and one-third to understand and comprehend the end of a matter from its beginning, and to derive one thing from another, and to compare one thing to another, etc.... which is called *Gemara*." Thus, even at the beginning of his studies, a person is obligated to learn the *pilpul* (dialectical analysis) of Torah [and not only *halacha l'ma'aseh*, because according to the Rambam, learning "Mishnah," which is fixed laws without reasons (and in the Rambam's words above, "one-third in the Oral Torah"), is sufficient to know the action they should perform, as mentioned in section a].

On the contrary: specifically according to the Alter Rebbe's view, the fact that a person is obligated to learn "one-third in Talmud" means only the reasons for the laws in a concise manner, which is necessary for understanding "the essence of the laws correctly and thoroughly" (and only then is it permitted to rule). This is unlike the *pilpul* of Torah, which is "the deep analysis of the reasons for the laws and understanding one thing from another and comparing one thing to another, etc."—"he should not engage in this at the beginning of his studies, but only after he finishes learning all the fixed laws with their reasons in a concise manner without much analysis and *pilpul*."

Furthermore: If the Rambam's intent were only that the foundation of the obligation of study is because it leads to action, it would be unclear what the practical difference is in his concluding words, "(because study leads to action) and action does not lead to study."

Even more puzzling: Later in the Laws of Talmud Torah, when the Rambam details the laws associated with this principle that "study precedes action everywhere" (the postponement of a mitzvah due to the mitzvah of Talmud Torah), he writes, "Talmud Torah is equal to all the mitzvos combined, because study leads to action; therefore, study precedes action everywhere"—and he does not add this detail, "and action does not lead to study." If the Rambam did not bring it in the place where this law is discussed,

## 19

why did he bring it here, when the matter was only brought incidentally as a proof for another matter?

d. Therefore, it seems to me that the *Kesef Mishneh*'s intent in what he wrote (explaining the Rambam's words), "And since he is obligated to perform, he is obligated to learn," is not merely that he is obligated to learn so that he knows how to perform (because study leads to action)—rather, this is also a proof by way of *kal v'chomer* (a fortiori argument): Since we find everywhere that study precedes (and is more important than) the matter of action ["because study leads to action, and action does not lead to study"], then, one who is obligated in action is *a fortiori* obligated in study (which is more important and precedes action everywhere).

Based on this, it emerges that the Rambam's intent is, on the contrary, to increase the importance of study over action. Beyond the obligation of study because it leads to action, the very obligation of study is more important and precedes the matter of action. This superiority is due to the very knowledge of the Torah. Therefore, the Rambam ruled that even at the beginning of a person's studies, he is obligated to learn one-third of his time in the *pilpul* of Torah, "which is called *Gemara*."

Therefore, it can be said that the reason the Alter Rebbe omitted the conclusion of the Rambam's words, "And thus you find, etc.," is because he follows his own view regarding the obligation "to divide his study time into three," which he interprets as "one-third in Talmud" (at the beginning of his studies)—meaning "to know the reasons in a concise manner from the Talmud and the *Poskim* without much analysis and *pilpul*," as will be explained below.

e. The explanation for this is as follows:
When the Alter Rebbe explains the obligation "to know the entire Written Torah and the entire Oral Torah," he interprets and specifies, "meaning *Tanach* and all the fixed laws of the entire Torah with their reasons, for they are the explanation of the 613 mitzvos in the Torah." Similarly, he emphasizes several more times in the Laws of Talmud Torah, regarding the definition of knowing the laws, etc., that they are "the explanation of the 613 mitzvos in the Torah."

The explanation of this language is understood from the words of the *Sefer Mitzvos Gadol* (the *Smag*)—which is the source for the Alter Rebbe—that there is an obligation to learn even the laws of the mitzvos that are not applicable in this time. He writes: "For the mitzvos that the Master of the World commanded, one must know their foundations even if they are not needed now, for we were commanded regarding all the mitzvos: 'And you shall teach them,' and it is said, 'Observe all the mitzvah that I command you today,' and it is said, 'And you shall observe My mitzvos and perform them.' You have learned that 'observance' and 'performance' are two different things, for there is no observance except in the heart... And so our Sages said, 'And you shall observe'—this is the Mishnah; 'and you shall perform'—this is the action."

From his words, it is understood that regarding the 613 mitzvos, there are two obligations: 1) the performance of the 613 mitzvos, 2) the observance of the 613 mitzvos in the heart, which is the knowledge of the mitzvos.

Based on this, it can be said that this is the basis of the difference between the view of the Rambam and the view of the Alter Rebbe regarding the definition of the mitzvah of knowing the Torah: According to the Rambam, it is a separate obligation of knowing the Torah, whereas for the Alter Rebbe, its foundation is the knowledge of the 613 mitzvos

## 20

in the Torah, and the definition of the study is analogous to the fulfillment of the 613 mitzvos, except that one is through action and the other is through observance in the heart [and this matter is also emphasized in the language of the Alter Rebbe in the Laws of Talmud Torah in the name of the "Masters of Truth" regarding the obligation to learn the entire Torah, "that every soul of Israel needs... to fulfill all 613 mitzvos in action, speech, and thought, and speech and thought is the study of their laws"].

Based on this, it can be said that the Rambam and the Alter Rebbe disagree on the interpretation of the verse "And you shall teach them to your children and you shall observe to do them" (which is the source of the obligation to teach oneself):
From the words of the Rambam, who, after bringing the verse, continues, "And thus you find everywhere that study precedes action, etc.," it is understood that he interprets the words "and you shall observe to do them" as referring to the performance of the mitzvos [and the verse comes to emphasize that study precedes action, in that "you shall teach them" comes first, and afterward "you shall observe to do them"]. However, the Alter Rebbe, who did not bring the continuation of the Rambam's words, interprets that the continuation of the verse's language, "and you shall observe to do them," also refers to the obligation of study, as the *Smag* says, "for there is no observance except in the heart... 'And you shall observe'—this is the Mishnah," that one must observe the 613 mitzvos in the heart through the study and knowledge of the 613 mitzvos. This is the continuation of the Alter Rebbe's language that "this is the positive commandment of Talmud Torah for every person to learn the entire Written Torah and the entire Oral Torah, as explained above, for it is said, 'All the mitzvah, etc.'" (where he brought "above" the verse "But if you shall observe to keep all this mitzvah" and the words of the *Smag*). This obligation is hinted at in what is written, "and you shall observe to do them," for which one must know all 613 mitzvos.

Based on this, the difference between the view of the Rambam and the view of the Alter Rebbe in interpreting "one-third in Talmud" is even more understandable—that for the Rambam, this is the *pilpul* of Torah, while for the Alter Rebbe, it is only the reasons for the laws in a concise manner, as mentioned above:
According to the view of the Rambam, where the mitzvah is the knowledge of the Torah, what they said—that a person is obligated to divide his study time into one-third in *Mikra*, one-third in Mishnah, and one-third in Talmud—means that one must divide the study time between all parts of the Torah: *Mikra*, Mishnah, and Talmud.
However, according to the view of the Alter Rebbe, they are not three different parts, but rather *Mikra*, Mishnah, and Talmud constitute a single entity of knowing the 613 mitzvos of the Torah. For this knowledge, one needs the study of

## 21

*Mikra*—in which the 613 mitzvos were stated; and the study of Mishnah and Talmud, which are the explanation of the 613 mitzvos: Mishnah—the fixed laws; and Talmud—the knowledge of the reasons for the laws in a concise manner, for only then does one "understand the essence of the laws correctly and thoroughly" [unlike the *pilpul* of Torah, which does not pertain to the knowledge of the 613 mitzvos of the Torah; this comes only after the person has finished learning the entire Torah, "let him finish and then let him analyze"].

f. This can be proven (that according to the Alter Rebbe, the primary obligation of knowing the Torah is the knowledge of the 613 mitzvos) from the words of the Alter Rebbe himself, later in the Laws of Talmud Torah, beginning of Chapter 3. He writes: "And anyone whose mind and power of memory are sharp enough to be able to learn and remember the entire Oral Torah, he should not take a wife until he first learns the entire Oral Torah, which are all the laws with their reasons in a concise manner, which are the explanation of all 613 mitzvos in their conditions and details and the details of the Scribes, and afterward he should take a wife, etc., for if he takes a wife first, etc., he will not be able to occupy himself with Torah properly, to learn and remember all the laws with their reasons, which are the explanation of the 613 mitzvos and the essence of the Oral Torah."

One must examine why he mentioned here only the study of "all the laws with their reasons," when earlier in the first chapter, when he speaks about the obligation "to know the entire Written Torah and the entire Oral Torah," he writes that this also includes the study of all "the words of the Sages which they supported with the *midrash* of the verses, which are the *Aggados*." Similarly, later, when he explains the obligation to divide his study time, he writes that "the *derashos* of the *Aggados* are considered part of the one-third in Mishnah," and if so, one is obligated to learn them—so why did he omit

## 22

at the beginning of Chapter 3 the obligation to learn *Aggados* [and similarly, several times later in the continuation of the chapter, he brings the matter of learning the entire Oral Torah, which is the study of laws, etc., and does not mention the study of *Aggados*]?

Based on the above, it is understood that the primary obligation of knowing the Torah is the knowledge of the 613 mitzvos, which includes only the study of the laws of the mitzvos. This is also the precision of the Alter Rebbe's language several times in Chapter 3, that the laws of the Torah, which are "the explanation of the 613 mitzvos," are the "essence of the Oral Torah." By this, he comes to resolve the precision mentioned above: that even though the *Aggados* in the Torah are included in the Oral Torah, they are nevertheless not part of the "essence of the Oral Torah," which is only the explanation of the 613 mitzvos [it can be said that the source of the Alter Rebbe's words is in the Rambam's introduction to *Sefer HaYad*, "All the mitzvos, etc., were given with their explanation... and this mitzvah, its explanation... and this mitzvah is what is called the Oral Torah"], and only the obligation to know the "essence of the Oral Torah" is so severe that "this study overrides the great mitzvah of procreation."

g. Now, even though according to the Alter Rebbe, the "essence of the Oral Torah," which is the explanation of the 613 mitzvos, is specifically the laws with their reasons (at least in a concise manner), we nevertheless find that the Alter Rebbe also calls fixed laws without reasons "the explanation of the 613 mitzvos in the Torah."

Furthermore: "Mishnah," which is fixed laws without reasons, is considered a separate part of Torah study, for the obligation to divide one's study time is—one-third in *Mikra*, one-third in Mishnah, and one-third in Talmud, meaning that one must learn Mishnah in a separate time. Seemingly, this is puzzling: Since for the Alter Rebbe, "if one does not know the reasons for the laws, he does not understand the essence of the laws correctly and thoroughly," and he does not fulfill the obligation of knowing the Torah until he also knows the reasons for the laws in a concise manner—if so, why is "Mishnah" considered a separate part of Torah study, to the extent that it is in a separate time?

It must be said that what the Alter Rebbe wrote, that without knowing the reasons for the laws at least in a concise manner it is impossible to understand "the essence of the laws correctly and thoroughly," does not mean to exclude the study of Mishnayos from the fulfillment of the mitzvah of knowing the "explanation of

## 23

the 613 mitzvos," but rather his intent is that there is a lack in the completeness of the understanding, "correctly and thoroughly."

It can be said that this is the reason he continues there, "Therefore, there are those who forbid ruling, even for oneself, based on fixed laws without the reasons one has learned"—for seemingly, the prohibition to rule (even for oneself) does not belong in the Laws of Talmud Torah but in the Laws of *Hora'ah* (Halachic Ruling). Rather, by this, the Alter Rebbe emphasizes that the lack of understanding in studying fixed laws without reasons pertains primarily to the matter of ruling, that "there are those who forbid ruling... based on fixed laws," but not (as much) regarding the study itself, because the definition of knowing the "explanation of the 613 mitzvos in the Torah" also applies to the study of fixed laws, and in this there is a fulfillment of the mitzvah of knowing the Torah. Only—since the completeness of knowledge and understanding ("correctly and thoroughly") also requires knowing the reasons for the laws at least in a concise manner—therefore one must also learn "one-third in Talmud."

This can be proven (that even according to the Alter Rebbe, the definition of knowing the "explanation of the 613 mitzvos in the Torah" and the fulfillment of the mitzvah of knowing the Torah applies to the study of fixed laws without reasons) from the words of the Alter Rebbe himself later in the Laws of Talmud Torah, where he wrote that "anyone who has not reached this level where he can learn the reasons for the laws and their sources, and even in study that leads to action alone, only fixed laws alone, even if he makes his Torah study permanent due to his limited intellect... he is not obligated to live a life of suffering and make his work temporary in order to increase study that he does not understand thoroughly, which is the study of laws without reasons... unless he does so as a measure of piety and love of Torah" [for only one "whose mind is sharp and succeeds in his study and can be a Torah scholar to learn the entire Oral Torah from the Talmud and the *Poskim*" is obligated to make his work temporary, etc.].

It is proven that even though there is no obligation to live a life of suffering for the sake of studying fixed laws without reasons, nevertheless, because of "a measure of piety and love of Torah," there is room for such conduct—to live a life of suffering in order to study the fixed laws of the entire Oral Torah. This is because the definition of fulfilling the mitzvah of knowing the 613 mitzvos of the Torah applies to this as well, and only because it is "study that he does not understand thoroughly" should one not obligate a person to live a life of suffering for it.

h. Based on all the above, the words of the Alter Rebbe mentioned above (section a) are understood: that there is a preference to learn fixed laws from Mishnayos and the work of the Rambam over learning a few laws with their reasons in a concise manner (where one understands them "correctly and thoroughly"):

Since the obligation of study is not merely for the sake of knowing the Torah (in which case it would be understood that there is a preference

## 24

for knowledge that is more complete, knowledge "correctly and thoroughly"), but for the sake of knowing the explanation of the 613 mitzvos, because a person is obligated to observe all 613 mitzvos "in the heart" [and in this time, only the fulfillment of the 613 mitzvos in action is nullified, not the observance in the heart, which is the knowledge of the laws of the mitzvos], it is understood that it is better to learn fixed laws from Mishnayos and the work of the Rambam, where there is a possibility that he will finish learning the laws of all 613 mitzvos, than to learn a few laws (even if the study would be of higher quality, with their reasons in a concise manner).

That is to say: the discussion here is not about study that pertains to action, but about learning laws that are not applicable in this time; and since this is not study that pertains to action, but only for the sake of the obligation of knowing the 613 mitzvos, therefore, it is better to have knowledge of all 613 mitzvos in the Torah than to have more complete knowledge, but only of a few of them.

i. Now, from all the above, one can learn an additional encouragement regarding the proposal to study a fixed daily portion in the Rambam's *Mishneh Torah* (which has been accepted and is spreading throughout the Diaspora of Israel)—for the content of studying this book is the study of the entire Oral Torah, as the Rambam says in his introduction to *Sefer HaYad*: "This work compiles the entire Oral Torah... so that a person reads the Written Torah first, and afterward reads this, and knows from it the entire Oral Torah, and does not need to read any other book between them."

At first glance, there is room to say that this superiority is only according to the view of the Rambam, who holds that it is possible to rule even without knowing the reasons; but according to the ruling of the Alter Rebbe (like the view of Rashi and the *Rosh*), that it is forbidden to rule from fixed laws and the study of laws must be with their reasons (at least in a concise manner)—and as the Alter Rebbe compiled his *Shulchan Aruch*, specifically the laws with their reasons—seemingly, there is no superiority of knowing the entire Oral Torah in the study of the Rambam.

However, based on the above, it is understood that this is not so, because even according to the ruling of the Alter Rebbe, a person who has not reached the level where he can learn and remember all the laws of the entire Torah with their reasons in a concise manner (whether because he has no time or because his mind is not sharp enough to succeed in his study to that extent)—like most people in our generation—should learn the explanation of all 613 mitzvos (according to his ability), which is fixed laws from Mishnayos and the work of the Rambam as mentioned above, because this too is in the category of knowing the 613 mitzvos of the Torah

## 25

(which is the essence of the entire Oral Torah), except that there is a lack in the completeness of the knowledge (which is only when one also knows the reasons for the laws)—which, in any case, he has not reached, as mentioned above.

It is found that in addition to all the superiorities in the fact that all of Israel is united in one study, and in this itself—in words of *halacha*, and in this itself—in the book of the Rambam, in which the fixed laws of all 613 mitzvos of the Torah are included,

it is understood based on the above that in this study there is also the fulfillment of the mitzvah of knowing the essence of the entire Oral Torah (for one who cannot learn the laws of the entire Torah with their reasons).

May it be the will that we merit "that time" when Israel will be "free for Torah and its wisdom," and there will be the study of the entire Torah in its completeness, "for the earth will be full of the knowledge of Hashem as the waters cover the sea."

(From a *sicha* of *Shabbos Parshas Chukas* 5744)


## וארא

## Sicha 1

## 26

A. "And Hashem said to Moshe and to Aharon, 'Take for yourselves handfuls of furnace soot, and let Moshe throw it heavenward... and it shall become dust over all the land of Egypt, and it shall become... boils breaking out in blisters throughout all the land of Egypt'".

Rashi explains: "'And let Moshe throw it'—and anything thrown with force is thrown only with one hand; therefore, there were many miracles: one, that Moshe’s palm held the handfuls of both his own and Aharon’s; and two, that the dust traveled over all the land of Egypt."

Rashi’s intent is simple: since the throwing of the furnace soot (both that of Moshe and Aharon) was performed solely by Moshe, and a forceful throw can only be executed with one hand, it must be that "Moshe’s palm held the handfuls of both his own and Aharon’s." Rashi continues by explaining the reason for this miracle, as we find in this plague in general that there were "many miracles."

Commentators have already addressed Rashi’s phrasing "many miracles," noting that Rashi "only counts two; why call it 'many'?".

It is even more puzzling that the Midrash (Tanchuma and Shemos Rabbah on this verse) lists three miracles without emphasizing "there were many miracles." The Midrash states: "Rabbi Yehuda bar Ilai said: A great miracle occurred with the boils. If a person throws an arrow upward, it does not travel a hundred cubits, but Moshe threw furnace soot—a handful of what is impossible to hold, as it has no substance ('for soot is something that cannot be held in the hand because it has no substance')—and Moshe threw it heavenward up to the Throne of Glory. Furthermore, a great miracle occurred with the boils, in that Moshe’s palm held his own handful and that of Aharon. And another miracle occurred with the boils: if a person scatters one *kav* of earth, he can only scatter it four cubits, but Moshe took a handful and scattered it over all the land of Egypt, etc." Yet Rashi, who omits the first miracle mentioned in the Midrash ("that Moshe threw it heavenward up to the Throne of Glory") and counts only two miracles, adds and emphasizes, "there were many miracles!".

## 27

B.
