Some commentators explain that the travel of the dust over all the land of Egypt contains two miracles : first, the very fact of its traveling such a great distance, and second, that a small amount of ash sufficed for the entire land of Egypt. This is what Rashi means by "there were many miracles," because this miracle of the ash traveling over all the land of Egypt included two miracles.

One could add another detail to the miracle—in the words of the Midrash, "Moshe threw furnace soot—a handful of what is impossible to hold, as it has no substance"—that the very act of holding the soot (a substance without substance) in the hand is a miracle, all the more so to throw it, and to such a great distance.

However, Rashi rejects this interpretation through the precision of his language, writing, "there were many miracles: one... and one..." It is clear that he is speaking of only two miracles ("one... and one").

[Especially since this emphasis of "one... and one" would be superfluous; he could have simply listed the miracles ("there were many miracles, that he held... and that the dust traveled..."). Furthermore, the Midrash uses the language "a great miracle... and another miracle... and another miracle," whereas Rashi changes this to "one... and one." This proves that in Rashi’s view, the interpretation is not that each miracle was a general category containing several specific miracles, but rather that each was a single, distinct miracle.]

Furthermore: if Rashi’s intent were to refer to the multiplicity of miracles within these miracles themselves, he should have been more specific, such as "that the dust traveled and scattered over all the land of Egypt" (and the like) [and similarly—that soot has no substance], which would have highlighted that there are two details here.

C. It seems to me, simply, that it is obvious that in all the plagues, the essence of the plague itself was a miraculous thing. So too in our case: the very plague of boils is a miracle, for the fact that the reality of boils was created from furnace soot is itself a manifest miracle. Therefore, Rashi wrote "there were many miracles," meaning the two miracles he details, together with the essence of the miracle of the plague itself.

## 28

The Ramban writes here that the dust which traveled over all the land of Egypt, when it descended upon people, etc., "caused boils and blisters to rise upon them, for it was hot and burning." According to this, the essence of the plague (the boils and blisters) was born in a natural way (from the hot, burning dust), and the miracle was only in the traveling and scattering of the dust over all the land of Egypt.

Seemingly, one could infer this also from Rashi’s words, where he explains "for boils breaking out in blisters"—"As the Targum says, *lishchin sagi abua'in* (a boil with many blisters), for through it blisters grow." "Boils (*shchin*), a term for heat (*chmimut*), and there are many instances in the language of the Mishnah of *shuna* (a year of drought/heat)." As explained by the commentators, the fact that the boil is the cause of the blisters ("a boil with many blisters") is because *shchin* means heat, and heat causes blisters. This implies that Rashi also holds like the Ramban that the dust from the furnace soot was "hot and burning."

But it is difficult to interpret Rashi this way, as he explains "furnace soot"—"something blown from the dim coals." Even if we were to say that the soot remained hot, it is extremely difficult to say it was "hot and burning" to such an extent that even after traveling over all the land of Egypt, it still possessed enough heat to cause boils and blisters in man and beast. Moreover, the very fact that Moshe and Aharon held this soot in their hands proves it was not "hot and burning" enough to cause blisters at the time they held it, and certainly not after it traveled over all the land of Egypt.

[And although one could say that this too was a miracle—that they were able to hold hot, burning soot in their hands, and that the soot remained in its heat even after traveling over all the land of Egypt—Rashi does not mention these miracles at all (even though he details the miracles that occurred in this plague).]

Furthermore, it is difficult because later in *Parshas Negaim*, Rashi explicitly states that "*Shchin* is a term for heating, that the flesh was heated in a lesion that came from a blow not caused by fire." That is, *shchin* is a name for the heating of the flesh in a lesion not caused by fire. How, then, can we say that the *shchin* here was from dust that was "hot and burning"?

Therefore, it is more reasonable to say that the birth of the boils (the heat, through which blisters grew) in man and beast occurred miraculously, that from the dust, heat (*shchin*) was created in a miraculous way (similar to the birth of lice from the earth in the plague of lice).

This is Rashi’s intent when he says "there were many miracles," even though he details only two, because

## 29

Rashi did not need to detail the miracle in the essence of the plague itself, which is explicitly stated in the verse, as mentioned above.

D. This is further clarified if we say that Rashi’s explanation in the subsequent phrase ("for boils breaking out in blisters"), where he explains the manner of the plague’s creation, "a boil with many blisters," is (also) a continuation of this Rashi. After Rashi detailed the two miracles that occurred with the furnace soot (the dust), he continues by detailing the miracle in the essence of the plague, that from this dust it became "a boil with many blisters." If so, the third miracle is also detailed in Rashi.

Based on this, one could say further that the content of this Rashi indicates several miracles that occurred in the essence of the plague itself. In addition to the miracle that the dust caused boils in man and beast—meaning a lesion in the flesh (which is the definition of *shchin*, as mentioned above from Rashi, "that the flesh was heated in a lesion") —there is also the fact that the *shchin* caused and grew blisters, which is another detail of the miracle of this plague (for it is possible to have a *shchin* without blisters).

One should add that the detail of "breaking out" (*porei'ach*) is also a miracle in itself. Although in this verse it means that through the *shchin*, blisters grew, in the subsequent verse it says, "and there were boils, blisters breaking out in man and beast." Here, it does not refer to the growth of blisters from the *shchin*, but is a description of both the *shchin* and the blisters, that they were "breaking out in man and beast." And here too, Onkelos translates it as "a boil with many blisters."

One can say simply that this is an additional miracle : that the *shchin* and the blisters were not limited to the place where the dust touched the man or beast, but grew, expanded, and multiplied until the entire body of the man and beast became afflicted with *shchin* and blisters.

According to this, there were not just three miracles, but "many miracles" in the literal sense. For even though the first two miracles are only two ("one... and one"), the essence of the plague included several miracles.

E. However, after all this, the language of Rashi, "(many miracles) one... and one," still requires explanation. Seemingly, one should multiply the miracles of the Ribono shel Olam in this plague even more, and explain that these miracles included several miraculous details (as in section B).

Also, what is the practical difference in interpreting the plain meaning of the verses to emphasize that in this plague there were "many miracles"? Seemingly, in other plagues as well, we find additional miraculous details beyond the miracle of the plague in general, yet Rashi does not emphasize in his commentary that "many miracles" occurred (except regarding the hail, which was a "miracle within a miracle").

It can be said that Rashi’s intent here is not only

## 30

to explain the reason for the miracle that occurred in the manner of holding and throwing the soot, but also to explain the reason why Moshe was required to throw the soot with force, as if this soot would reach "heavenward" through the force of his throw, and likewise to every place where the *shchin* needed to reach. Yet, regarding "heavenward" and the distance of all the land of Egypt, there is no difference at all between a simple throw and a throw with all his might.

To resolve this wonder, Rashi explains "there were many miracles: one... and one..." (and in the subsequent phrase he explains the manner of the appearance of the *shchin* and blisters itself). His intent is not merely to list all the miracles that occurred in this plague, but primarily to emphasize the nature of the miracles, according to which we understand the reason Moshe needed to throw the soot with force specifically.

F. This will be understood by prefacing what we find in the plague of *shchin*, which is different from all other plagues:

1) In all the plagues that came through the action of Moshe or Aharon, there was only a small action on their part, and immediately afterward the plague was in effect throughout all the land of Egypt. Like in blood, where Aharon struck the Nile before Pharaoh and immediately all the waters of Egypt turned to blood ; in frogs, where "Aharon stretched out his hand over the waters of Egypt and the frog came up and covered the land of Egypt" ; in lice—"Aharon stretched out his hand with his staff and struck the dust of the earth and the lice were... in all the land of Egypt". And likewise in the plagues after *shchin*—like in hail, when "Moshe stretched out his staff toward the heavens," hail fell on (all) the land of Egypt ; in locusts—"Moshe stretched out his staff over the land of Egypt and Hashem led an east wind... and the locusts came up over all the land of Egypt" ; and likewise in darkness—"Moshe stretched out his hand toward the heavens and there was thick darkness in all the land of Egypt".

This is not the case with *shchin*. Seemingly, one could have said that immediately after throwing the furnace soot heavenward, all man and beast would be struck with *shchin*. But in practice, this was not the case; rather, Moshe’s action only caused the dust to travel over all the land of Egypt, and the dust that traveled over all the land of Egypt (and rested "upon man and upon beast") is what struck them with *shchin*. It requires study: why was this plague different from all other plagues?

2) After the plague of *shchin*, it is said, "And the magicians could not stand before Moshe because of the *shchin*, for the *shchin* was on the magicians and on all Egypt." Seemingly, it requires study: why specifically after this plague were they unable to stand before Moshe?

The Ramban writes there that the magicians "were ashamed

## 31

and humiliated and covered their heads, being full of *shchin*, and they could not save themselves; therefore they did not come to the king’s palace and were not seen before Moshe, etc." But seemingly, it requires study: how is this plague different from the plague of frogs and lice, in which, simply put, the magicians were also struck ? As is explicitly stated in the verse that the frogs were "in your house... and in you" (Pharaoh’s house and Pharaoh himself). If so, why were the magicians not ashamed to stand before Moshe when frogs were "in their intestines... and croaking", or their bodies full of lice ?

Therefore, it seems to me that the verse does not mean only a physical standing, that they could not stand before Moshe because they were struck with *shchin* (especially since according to that, there is no reason for the end of the verse, "for the *shchin* was... and on all Egypt"—what does that have to do with this?). Rather, it refers to a spiritual standing: the nature of this plague brought them to a state where they could not find their hands or feet. That is, after the plague of lice, when they could not produce lice, they said, "It is the finger of Elokus". However, here, after the plague of *shchin*, they were completely nullified to the point that they could not find any "standing" for themselves at all.

Therefore, after this plague, even in the plagues that came through the action of Moshe and Aharon, they did not even attempt to do like them.

G. The explanation for this can be said as follows:

One of the goals of the plagues, besides punishing the Egyptians, was so that "the Egyptians shall know that I am Hashem". This knowledge and recognition came through the plagues, such that even the magicians of Egypt were forced to say, "It is the finger of Elokus." The order of the plagues was that at first, the Ribono shel Olam brought plagues in which it was not so clearly evident that they were from Hashem, for the magicians could also do so with their secret arts. Only afterward came the plague of lice, which the magicians could not replicate with their secret arts, and they were forced to agree that "it is the finger of Elokus." But nevertheless, even though the plague was "the finger of Elokus," it still came into effect through Aharon’s action. This was the innovation of the plagues that followed—*Arov* and *Dever*—which came without the mediation of any action by Moshe or Aharon, but directly from Hashem. Based on this, it is understood simply why the verse does not mention at all the magicians' relationship to these plagues, for in them, there was no room from the outset for the assumption that this was an act of sorcery by Moshe and Aharon, since they performed no action at all to bring the plague.

That is to say, the more one minimizes natural action on the part of Moshe or Aharon, the more it is evident that this is a plague from the Ribono shel Olam.

This is the innovation of the plague of *shchin*, in which there were two opposites: the plague came in a way that gave room for the error that it came through natural actions, but on the other hand, it was entirely

## 32

and only a miraculous act (which has no place in nature). These two opposites at once were what caused the nullification of the magicians. For they too were forced to admit that it was "the finger of Elokus," but according to their perception and grasp, this is only when it concerns a matter that natural forces have no access to (for they do not have power over a creature smaller than a grain of mustard, and certainly not to act without any action). This is not the case with the plague of *shchin*, where even in matters that are seemingly natural actions (which the magicians also have the power to do something similar to), the Ribono shel Olam performed manifest miracles, to the point that not only did they admit "it is the finger of Elokus," but "they could not... stand before Moshe."

We find this in the plague of *shchin* in all its stages:

At the very beginning, they had to take "furnace soot" specifically, whose heat is greatest, and in that itself—handfuls of Moshe and Aharon (a small amount, one handful, etc., was not enough). Moshe had to throw the furnace soot with force, as if this soot would reach every place where the *shchin* needed to be through the force of his throw. And even afterward, the *shchin* was not born in a natural way throughout all the land of Egypt; rather, the dust itself traveled and scattered throughout all the land of Egypt. All these details indicate as if the plague were through a natural action, as if the dust were the progenitor of the *shchin*.

At the same time, in truth, all these actions did not cause the appearance of the *shchin* in a natural way, for the soot was no longer hot, and Moshe’s throw (even with force) could not reach "heavenward," and certainly not throughout all the land of Egypt, in such a way that the dust is not in the category of a natural action at all to bring the *shchin*. Rather, the *shchin* was a new creation (from the dust).

Similarly, regarding the way the *shchin* broke out on man, the verse emphasizes "for boils breaking out in blisters," and as Rashi (at length, above) says, *shchin* is a term for heat, and the meaning of "for boils breaking out in blisters" is that the heat caused blisters to grow. Thus, the plague was born as if in a natural order, where first heat came into the flesh, and from this heat blisters grew, even though in truth, this entire plague was only a miracle from the Ribono shel Olam, which is not within the power of the magicians at all.

Based on this, the reason Rashi did not simply list all the details of the miracles that occurred here is also understood, for the main emphasis here is not on the quantity of miracles, but on the quality of these miracles—a thing and its opposite, nature and miracle at once.

This is why Rashi begins his commentary with the fact that Moshe threw with all his might. Rashi was troubled by what the point was of throwing with all his might when it concerns a miraculous matter, and Rashi explains that "there were many miracles," and details the nature of the miracles, that together with a natural action, there was a miraculous matter here that is above nature.

(From a Sicha of Shabbos Parshas Va'eira, 5743)

## Sicha 2

## 33

### Sicha 2

[quotation]Missing[/quotation]


## ר"ח שבט

## 38

### Rosh Chodesh Shevat

A. "It was in the fortieth year, in the eleventh month, on the first of the month... Moses began to explain this Torah, saying", and our Sages said — as cited in Rashi — "He explained it to them in seventy languages."

This requires explanation: Why was it necessary for Moshe Rabbeinu to explain the Torah to the Jewish people in seventy languages?

The *Levush* writes: "He was concerned that perhaps not all of them were masters of Lashon HaKodesh and did not understand it at all, for surely in such a great nation, numbering more than six hundred thousand, there were those who spoke many languages. Therefore, Rashi says 'in seventy languages,' meaning that since he was concerned that there might be those among them who spoke other languages... he certainly had to explain it to them in all seventy languages, for who knows which one you might exclude—perhaps there are those among them who speak that very language which you leave out, and they will not understand it."

But on the face of it, this explanation is difficult to understand. If Moshe Rabbeinu was concerned about this, why did he wait until now, at the end of forty years, to explain the Torah in seventy languages, and not do so immediately after Matan Torah? Furthermore, just because of a "concern that perhaps they are not all masters of Lashon HaKodesh," was it necessary for Moshe to trouble the entire Jewish people to hear an explanation of the entire Torah in all seventy languages?

Some have written that "because Moshe Rabbeinu saw that the Jewish people would go into exile among the seventy nations, he therefore explained it in seventy languages, so that wherever the Jewish people would come, they would learn in that language."

This answer also requires explanation. Since this matter is not practically relevant until the Jewish people go into exile among the seventy nations, why was it necessary for Moshe Rabbeinu to teach all of this—the entire Torah seventy times, each time in a different language—when the vast majority of the Jewish people did not understand them at all? As our Sages said, even in the Sanhedrin, only a few understood them. And why did he not rely on the Sages of Israel in future generations to do so when the need arose?

## 39

B. The explanation for this is as follows:

It is known what the Ramban writes regarding Lashon HaKodesh: "That the words of the Torah, the prophecies, and all words of holiness were all spoken in that language... It is the language that the Ribono shel Olam... speaks." Based on this, since the essence of Torah is the Torah of Hashem, "who gave us His Torah", it would seemingly follow that the study of Torah should be specifically in Lashon HaKodesh, the language "that the Ribono shel Olam... speaks."

And this is not only regarding the Written Torah, whose definition is—as its name implies—the writing, the precision of the letters, lacking nothing and adding nothing, as it was given from the "mouth of the Almighty" (to the extent that reading the Written Torah, even if one does not understand it, is considered the study of Torah). It is certainly reasonable to say that the reading of the Written Torah must be specifically in Lashon HaKodesh (the language in which it was given from the "mouth of the Almighty").

Rather, even regarding the explanation of the Torah and the Oral Torah in its entirety: even though, on the face of it, understanding is relevant (which is why if one does not understand what he is learning, "it is not considered learning at all"), nevertheless, "thought is not equivalent to speech". There are several laws that apply to speech in words of Torah (even in the Oral Torah), such as the prohibition against speaking words of Torah while naked (whereas thought is permitted). The Alter Rebbe rules that one does not recite the blessings on the Torah for thoughts of Torah, but only for the speech of Torah study, and so on.

Since the Oral Torah is also "from Hashem", the Torah of Hashem, there is room to say that the name and definition of "words of Torah" apply only to speech that is in the language that the Ribono shel Olam speaks, namely Lashon HaKodesh.

This was the action of Moshe Rabbeinu: by explaining the Torah in seventy languages, the name "Torah" became applicable to words of Torah studied by the Jewish people in other languages. Even though it is not

## 40

the language that the Ribono shel Olam speaks, the definition of "words of Torah" nevertheless applies to it. When a person brings forth from his mouth matters of Torah in the languages of the nations, he is bringing forth "words of Torah" (and it is forbidden to speak them before the blessing on the Torah, etc.).

[It may be said that we find something similar within the Torah itself, which contains words from the languages of the nations, such as: "Yegar Sahaduta," "Totafot," and others. The reason these words were included in the Torah (which is entirely "from the mouth of the Almighty") is so that the holiness of the Torah would apply to these words, thereby making the languages of the nations fit to "convey" words of Torah through them. Something similar is extended to all languages when one learns Torah in them.]

The reason this was done specifically by Moshe Rabbeinu is that all matters of Torah were transmitted to the Jewish people specifically through Moshe: "Moshe received the Torah from Sinai", to the point that our Sages said that "everything that a veteran student is destined to innovate was already told to Moshe at Sinai." So too in this matter: the application of the name "Torah" to words of Torah spoken in seventy languages could only come about through Moshe.

C. Based on this, we can explain another matter we find regarding the translation of the Torah into seventy languages. After Moshe Rabbeinu explained the Torah in seventy languages, "Moshe and the elders of Israel commanded the people... on the day that you cross the Jordan... and you shall set up for yourself large stones... and you shall write upon the stones all the words of this Torah, clearly explained." Our Sages said—as cited in Rashi—"in seventy languages." This means that in addition to the fact that he explained the Torah to them in seventy languages orally, he commanded that they write the Torah in seventy languages.

According to what we have explained, the reason both were necessary is understood. The Rambam explains that the writing was done "in the script of each of these nations." Thus, the difference between "he explained it to them in seventy languages" and the writing of the Torah in seventy languages is not only whether it was oral or written, but also when it concerns only the language of the nations or

## 41

only the script of the nations. These are two separate matters: "he explained it to them" and the writing. They are two actions: the first is that the name and definition of Torah should apply to the Torah learned in the languages of the nations; the second is that the name "Torah" should apply to the script of the nations, so that books on Torah matters written in the script of the nations are holy books (which one is forbidden to treat with disrespect, etc.).

D. According to the literal meaning, this verse ("Moshe began to explain this Torah") speaks of the recitation of Mishneh Torah. From this, it is understood that the explanation of the Torah by Moshe Rabbeinu "in seventy languages" is connected to the innovation of Mishneh Torah.

Based on the above, we can explain the connection between them. We begin with the words of our Sages: "The curses in Mishneh Torah... Moshe said from his own mouth" (unlike those in Torat Kohanim (Parshat Bechukotai), which "Moshe said from the mouth of the Almighty"). It has already been explained in many places that this rule applies not only to the "curses in Mishneh Torah," but that Moshe said the entire book of Mishneh Torah "from his own mouth."

The commentators have elaborated on the meaning of this. Seemingly, how can it be said of a verse from the Five Books of Torah that Moshe said it "from his own mouth" (and not "from the mouth of the Almighty")? After all, it is a clear halacha that "one who says that the Torah is not from Hashem... even one word, if he says that Moshe said it from his own mouth, he is a denier of the Torah." And it is explicitly stated in the Rambam regarding the "eight verses at the end of the Torah"—"that it is all Torah, and Moshe said them from the mouth of the Almighty."

This is explained according to the words of the Tosafot: "Moshe said them from his own mouth—but through Ruach HaKodesh." That is to say, even (the curses in) Mishneh Torah were said through Ruach HaKodesh and are included in the Torah that was said "from the mouth of the Almighty." However, there are various levels within "the mouth of the Almighty," and relative to the "mouth of the Almighty" in the first four books, the "Ruach HaKodesh" in Mishneh Torah is called "from his own mouth" (as will be explained in section E).

This requires explanation: Since the definition of the Written Torah (the Five Books of Torah) is that it is entirely "from the mouth of the Almighty" (and in this it differs from the Prophets and Writings, which were also said and written through prophecy or Ruach HaKodesh, but are not in the same category of "from the mouth of the Almighty" as the Torah of Moshe), why were the words of Moshe that were said (only) "through Ruach HaKodesh" included in the Written Torah, to the point that regarding the first four books they are in the category of "from his own mouth"?

The explanation for this is as follows:

## 42

It is known what our Sages said: "Is there anything written in the Writings that is not alluded to in the Torah?" Rashi explains: "And Moshe did not allude to it in the Torah, for the Chumash is the foundation of the Prophets and Writings, and in all of them there is a basis to be found in the Torah." Based on this, it can be said that just as the content of the matters in the Prophets and Writings "has a basis to be found in the Torah," so too regarding the manner of the revelation of the matters in the Prophets and Writings: in order for the name "Torah" to apply to them, there must be a "foundation" for such a revelation in the Torah of Moshe itself.

That is to say: the matters in the books of the Prophets and Writings (which were revealed through prophecy and Ruach HaKodesh) are not merely in the category of "words of a prophet" (and the like), but are "Torah." Therefore, we are obligated to fulfill the commandments within them (not [only] out of the obligation to listen to the words of the prophet, but) out of the obligation of the Torah to fulfill "words of Kabbalah" (though they do not have the same gravity as words of Torah, the Five Books of Torah). It can be said that the foundation for this (that the definition of "Torah" applies to matters revealed through prophecy or Ruach HaKodesh) within the Torah of Moshe itself is Mishneh Torah, which was said (only) "through Ruach HaKodesh," yet it is part of the Torah of Moshe, "for it is all Torah, and Moshe said them from the mouth of the Almighty."

E. In this way, we can also explain the words of the Zohar that Mishneh Torah is called "Oral Torah" and is the source of the Oral Torah [as explained in the book *Megaleh Amukot* that "the matter of Mishneh Torah is the secret of the Oral Torah that the Jewish people received at the end of forty years upon their entry into the land," and this is also the hint in the fact that there are "forty generations from Moshe Rabbeinu until Rabbi who arranged the Mishnayot"—similar to the "forty years that [the Jewish people] waited to receive the Oral Torah"].

The connection of Mishneh Torah to the Oral Torah is understood according to what is explained in several places regarding the difference between the "mouth of the Almighty" in the first four books and the "mouth of the Almighty" in Mishneh Torah. The first books were revealed by the Ribono shel Olam, but through the agency of Moshe—the Ribono shel Olam placed the words into his mouth, and Moshe transcribed them in writing, in a way that Moshe's own intellect and comprehension did not intervene in the transmission of the words. This is not the case in Mishneh Torah; although the matters in Mishneh Torah were also said "from the mouth of the Almighty," the revelation of the matters was through Moshe, in that Moshe spoke from his own self the words of Hashem that he had grasped in his intellect. That is, the words of the Ribono shel Olam were clothed in the intellect of Moshe (and therefore Moshe transmitted them to the Jewish people as if he were saying them "from his own mouth").

[Based on this, we can explain the words of the Ravan, who explains what our Sages said that "even one who does not interpret the juxtaposition of verses generally, does so in Mishneh Torah"—"The reason is that the entire Torah was said from the mouth of the Almighty, and there is no 'earlier' or 'later' in the Torah, but Moshe, who arranged Mishneh Torah, section after section, arranged it only to be interpreted." It can be said that his intent is that in the first four books,

## 43

where the Ribono shel Olam placed His words into the mouth of Moshe (without the intervention of Moshe's intellect and comprehension), there is no necessity that there be a reason in Moshe's intellect for the juxtaposition of the matters, and therefore one does not interpret the juxtapositions. This is not the case in Mishneh Torah, where Moshe grasped the matters and their order in his intellect, and he spoke from his own self the words of Hashem that he had grasped; therefore, the order of the matters is necessarily understood and comprehended, and one can interpret the juxtapositions—"he arranged it only to be interpreted."]

Based on the above (section D), it can be said that the reason there had to be such a matter in the Torah of Moshe (that the revelation of the matters should be in a way that they were first clothed in the intellect and comprehension of Moshe Rabbeinu) is that this is the "foundation" for the definition of the Oral Torah.

Regarding the Oral Torah, we find a paradox: on one hand, its revelation (aside from matters that are *Halacha L'Moshe MiSinai* and received through tradition) is through the Jewish people, who reveal the matters of the Oral Torah through their study. In this, the intellect and comprehension of the Torah student is essential. On the other hand, the entire content of Torah study is to reveal the word of Hashem. That is: the halacha depends (not on human understanding, but) on the fact that this is His will; for the essence of Torah is His wisdom, but the Ribono shel Olam transmitted it to the Jewish people so that they would reveal it through their understanding.

Since all definitions in the Torah must have a foundation in the Torah of Moshe, this matter—that the name "Torah" ("Torah of Hashem") should apply to a matter of wisdom revealed through the intellect and comprehension of a created human being—exists in the Torah of Moshe in Mishneh Torah, which also contains these two extremes: it is the word of Hashem clothed in the intellect and comprehension of Moshe. Through the fact that this definition exists in the Torah of Moshe, it is extended to the Oral Torah in its entirety.

This is also the reason that together with the recitation of Mishneh Torah, "he explained it to them in seventy languages": just as through the recitation of Mishneh Torah, Moshe caused the comprehension of Torah by a human being (who learns according to the rules of the Torah) to become part of the Torah of Hashem (and before one learns it, one is obligated to recite the blessing on the Torah, "who gave us His Torah")—so too regarding the language in which one learns Torah: "he explained it to them in seventy languages" so that the definition of Torah would also apply to a language (which is not a language that the Ribono shel Olam "speaks," but) the language of each nation, the languages of human beings, as explained above at length.

F. The matter mentioned above is especially relevant to the Rebbe, our master and teacher, whose *yahrzeit* falls on the tenth of the "eleventh month" (the tenth of Shevat). One of his innovations regarding the revelation of the inner dimensions of the Torah (which in recent generations has been revealed in the teachings of Chassidus in general, and in the teachings of Chabad Chassidus in particular) was that he strove greatly to translate books and *maamarim* of the inner dimensions of the Torah into seventy languages, so that the springs of the inner dimensions of the Torah would reach the very "outside," even to those who are not only unacquainted with Lashon HaKodesh, but not even with Yiddish.

This was a bestowal of strength from him, that even when the matters of the inner dimensions of the Torah are translated into the letters of the language of each nation, they remain holy, and they contain the qualities of the "hidden aspects of the Torah," which awaken the "hidden aspects of the soul" and connect the person with the "hidden aspects of the Ribono shel Olam" (as explained in the Zohar).

## 44

From here we learn a lesson for action, as we stand at the beginning of the month of Shevat (in which "Moshe began to explain this Torah" in seventy languages), and close to the *yahrzeit* of the Rebbe, our master and teacher, who strove for the translation of the matters of the inner dimensions of the Torah into seventy languages—

And that each and every one of us must walk "in the straight path that he taught us, and we shall walk in his ways, *naseh v'nishma*"—

Especially since this year is forty years since his passing, and the Torah promises that in forty years Hashem gives you "a heart to know, and eyes to see, and ears to hear," and as our Sages said that then a person stands upon the mind of his teacher—

To engage in the dissemination of Torah in general, and in the dissemination of the inner dimensions of the Torah in particular, even to those who are on the "outside," to the point that one must explain the Torah to them in seventy languages.

And through "your springs shall spread to the outside," the Master, King Moshiach, will come, and he will lead us upright to our land—the entry into the land, in the true and complete Redemption, speedily in our days, truly.


## בא

## Sicha 1

### 45

1. "And I will pass through the land of Egypt on this night". Rashi explains: "And I will pass" — "Like a king who passes from place to place, and in one passing and in one moment, they are all struck." The source for this interpretation is in the Mechilta, but there it only states, "Like a king who passes from place to place," while Rashi adds (and explains) — "and in one passing and in one moment, they are all struck."

This requires explanation: a) What difficulty in the literal meaning of the verse is Rashi coming to resolve? b) What is the meaning of the analogy, "Like a king who passes from place to place"? c) What is the meaning of his words, "and in one passing, etc."?

The Ra'am explains that Rashi’s intent is to resolve the redundancy of the word "ve'avarti" (and I will pass). The intent of the analogy to a king is that "it is his custom to walk on a straight path and not turn here or there in the alleys of the city; so too, Hashem passed through the land of Egypt in only one alley, and in that same passing, the firstborn of Egypt were struck, whether they were in that same alley through which He passed, or whether they were in other alleys."

This interpretation is difficult, for in the preceding verse, Rashi explains: "The offering is called Pesach on account of the skipping and passing over, that Hashem skips over the houses of Israel from among the houses of Egypt, and jumps from Egyptian to Egyptian, and the Israelite in the middle is spared." Similarly, in the following verse, he explains: "He would skip from the houses of Israel to the houses of Egypt that were interspersed among them." This implies that Hashem passed from house to house, whereas according to the Ra'am, the entire content of this Rashi is the opposite—that Hashem went only "in one alley alone," and not in other alleys, and certainly not from house to house.

Some commentators suggest that Rashi is troubled by why it says "in the land of Egypt" rather than "in Egypt," which implies that Hashem passed through the entire land of Egypt to strike "every firstborn in the land of Egypt." This is a matter that, so to speak, would require much time and great effort, yet Moshe said, "About midnight I will go out", which implies that the plague of the firstborn occurred at that very moment (throughout the entire land of Egypt). Rashi therefore answers: "Like a king who passes from place to place," meaning he passes without delay, for he does not fear the danger of the roads in fields and forests. Thus, Hashem, in one passing and one moment, struck every firstborn in the land of Egypt and did not need to linger or pass the moment of midnight. Therefore,

## 46

he chose the expression "ve'avarti," an expression of "passing" (avarah) which denotes speed.

But this interpretation is also seemingly difficult. What was the initial assumption that Hashem’s walking "through the entire land of Egypt" is something that "requires much time and great effort"? It is obvious that Hashem is above the limitations of space and time and can pass through the entire land of Egypt in the one moment of midnight. Furthermore, what is added by the analogy of "a king who passes from place to place"? First, the analogy does not match the subject: after all, the walking of a king of flesh and blood, even when passing from place to place with great speed, is not at all similar to "I will pass, etc.," which occurred in the one moment of midnight throughout the entire land of Egypt. Moreover, what does the analogy add?

Furthermore: even though "passing" (avarah) denotes the speed of movement without delays, the concept of "in one moment they are all struck" is impossible for a king of flesh and blood, as it is obvious that he would require time to strike the multitude of people and animals, etc., throughout the entire land. If so, the interpretation in Rashi is seemingly inappropriate.

2. Seemingly, one can explain that Rashi comes in his commentary to resolve a difficulty that arises in the entire parsha: In this verse, it says, "And I will pass through the land of Egypt, etc., and I will strike, etc.," meaning Hashem Himself, as is explicitly stated at the end of the verse, "I am Hashem," and in Rashi, "I Myself, and not through a messenger." The same is implied by the following verse, "And I will pass over (ufasachti) you," that Hashem skips over the houses of Israel, etc. Similarly, in Moshe’s words to Israel, it is said, "And Hashem will pass through to strike Egypt." However, regarding the verse, "And it was at midnight, and Hashem struck every firstborn," Rashi explains, "And Hashem, He and His Beis Din." Likewise, it is said many times in these scriptures regarding "the destroyer," "and He will not permit the destroyer to come into your houses to strike", and the like. This indicates that the plague came through a destroyer. This is also explicit in Rashi on the verse, "And you shall not go out," "This tells us that once permission is given to the destroyer to cause harm, he does not distinguish between the righteous and the wicked, etc."

This is what Rashi explains with the word "ve'avarti" — "Like a king who passes from place to place." When a king passes from place to place, he does not go alone, but with his entourage, his ministers, his servants, etc. Even though they do everything to facilitate the king’s movement from place to place, everything is attributed to the king and not to his ministers, soldiers, and the like. So too in our case: even though Hashem was with His Beis Din and the heavenly host, etc., nevertheless, it is all included in "I will pass, etc., and I will strike, etc."

For when Hashem sends a messenger and the like, even though it is not a matter that depends on the will and choice of the angel or the messenger, they are still called by the name

## 47

"messengers of Hashem." This is not the case when they go and act together with the "King," Hashem Himself; when He, may He be blessed, "passes from place to place," everything is attributed to Hashem.

However, although this interpretation fits well with the language of the Mechilta, "Like a king who passes from place to place", it is difficult to explain Rashi’s words this way, as he adds and explains, "and in one passing and in one moment they are all struck." According to the above, the main point is missing in Rashi, for he should have said that He goes with His ministers, servants, and soldiers (and the like), whereas Rashi emphasizes a different matter: that "in one passing and in one moment they are all struck".

3. It can be said that the explanation for this is as follows:

The difficulty with the expression "ve'avarti" here is that above, in Moshe’s words to Pharaoh regarding the plague of the firstborn, it is said, "So says Hashem: About midnight I will go out (ani yotzei) into the midst of Egypt, and every firstborn in the land of Egypt will die, from the firstborn of Pharaoh, etc.," whereas here (in Moshe’s words to Israel) it says, "And I will pass through the land of Egypt."

We must understand the intent of what is said there, "I will go out into the midst of Egypt," for seemingly, Hashem’s actions do not require a "going out" of Hashem. Just as it was in the other plagues, "Behold, the hand of Hashem is upon your livestock, etc.," "And Hashem will distinguish between the livestock of Israel, etc.", "And only you and your people with the plague, etc." — and in all of these it is not said that there was a "going out" of Hashem into Egypt to strike them. It is simple that Hashem’s action is in the manner of "He spoke and it was". Why then is it said here, "I will go out, etc."?

Rather, the matter of "going out" here means a special action for the sake of the salvation and redemption of Israel, as it is written, "And I have descended to save them from the hand of Egypt." This is also the purpose of Hashem’s "going out" "into the midst of Egypt," in order to save them and redeem them.

[And even though the redemption and the going out of Bnei Yisrael from Egypt was "on this very day," the day of the 15th of Nissan, nevertheless, the time of the redemption from Above

## 48

began from the night, as is explicit in the scripture: "It is a night of watching for Hashem to bring them out from the land of Egypt; this is that night for Hashem," "This is the night that He said to Avraham, 'On this night I will redeem your children.'"]

This is also what is meant by what is said later, "(I will go out, etc.) and every firstborn in the land of Egypt will die, etc.," and not "And I will strike every firstborn" and the like, because the fact that "I will go out into the midst of Egypt" was not for the sake of striking the firstborn of Egypt, but for the sake of saving Israel, except that by the way, "every firstborn in the land of Egypt died."

But it is not understood what is meant by "And I will pass through the land of Egypt, etc., and I will strike every firstborn," which implies that Hashem passes through the land of Egypt to strike every firstborn, specifically for the sake of the plague of the firstborn. Furthermore, He also passed from house to house (in a manner that required "skipping" over the houses of Israel), as mentioned in section 1.

Therefore, Rashi explains, "Like a king who passes from place to place," that this is not a special walk for the sake of the plague of the firstborn, but "like a king who passes from place to place" for another matter. So too in our case, Hashem’s walking through the land of Egypt was for the sake of the salvation and redemption of Israel, as mentioned above.

These are the two expressions in the scripture — "I will go out into the midst of Egypt," "And I will pass through the land of Egypt" — because at midnight, the time of the plague of the firstborn, there were, so to speak, two matters from Hashem’s side: a category of "going out" for the sake of the salvation of Israel, and a category of "passing" in relation to the striking of the firstborn of Egypt.

4. However, it is still difficult: the verse here continues, "And I will strike every firstborn, etc." (and not "and will die," as is said regarding "I will go out"). This seemingly implies that the death of the firstborn of Egypt was not by the way, but in the manner of an action, and what is said, "And I will pass, etc., and I will strike, etc.," means a special walk for the sake of this act of striking.

Therefore, Rashi continues, "(Like a king who passes from place to place) and in one passing and in one moment they are all struck." With this, Rashi emphasizes that the expression "ve'avarti" here teaches us not only the manner of Hashem’s "walking" in Egypt (which, regarding the striking of the firstborn of Egypt, was "like a king who passes from place to place"), but also the manner of the striking of the Egyptians. Since the striking of the Egyptians occurs when Hashem "passes from place to place," therefore the striking itself is also in the way of "passing," in the manner of "in one passing and in one moment they are all struck." It was not a striking in a private manner, each Egyptian individually with special providence, but all were struck as one.

## 49

This is also the content of the analogy of "a king who passes from place to place": when the king’s walking is for the purpose of punishing, then the striking of each one is considered a separate matter occurring at a separate time. But when the walking is in the way of passing from place to place, and the striking occurs incidentally to the passing, then the passing is one passing, and consequently, the fact that they are struck is also considered as being struck in one moment. So it is in our case: since Hashem’s walking through the land of Egypt was for the sake of the salvation and redemption of Israel, and in relation to the striking of the firstborn of Egypt it is "like a king who passes from place to place," therefore the striking of the Egyptians is also merely by way of passing, in which this passing — which is "one passing and one moment" — all the firstborn were struck (as one).

5. Now, based on this emphasis in Rashi, that the striking of the firstborn of Egypt was "in one passing and in one moment," there is a new depth in the entire matter of "And I will pass over (ufasachti) you":

The meaning is not (as it appears superficially) that Hashem descended and passed between the houses of Egypt in order to strike them, and because of this, another matter was created, that Hashem needed to skip over the houses of Israel. Rather, from the outset, this entire descent and going out into Egypt was only for the sake of the salvation of Israel, while the striking of the Egyptians was merely by way of passing.

This means that in the plague of the firstborn itself, there were two categories: "going out" and "passing." The striking of the firstborn of Egypt was in the manner of "passing," "in one passing... they are all struck." Together with this, in order to save Israel from the destroyer, Hashem "went out" "into the midst of Egypt," meaning He descended and watched over the houses of Israel in order to save them, as Rashi explains on the verse, "And the blood will be for you as a sign, etc., and I will see the blood" — "Everything is revealed before Him, but Hashem said: I am setting My eyes to see that you are occupied with My mitzvos, and I am passing over you."

This is also the greatness of the matter of "It is a Pesach offering to Hashem," for Rashi (as mentioned above) says, "The offering is called Pesach on account of the skipping and passing over, that Hashem skips over the houses of Israel from among the houses of Egypt, and jumps from Egyptian to Egyptian, and the Israelite in the middle is spared." Seemingly, one must understand why this is considered such a primary matter, to the point that the offering is named after this skipping, as it is written, "And you shall say: It is a Pesach sacrifice to Hashem, who passed over the houses of the children of Israel in Egypt when He struck Egypt, and He saved our houses."

The explanation is that this expresses the greatness of the virtue and affection of Israel in the eyes of Hashem. At the very moment that Hashem struck the firstborn of Egypt in the manner of "one passing... they are all struck," a conduct in the manner of mere "passing," in this very matter, regarding Israel, there was a category of "going out" into Egypt, where Hashem "went out" "into the midst of Egypt," into all the alleys, in order to save each and every one of Israel, even those located among the houses of the Egyptians. This was the beginning of the redemption of Israel, that Hashem in His glory and essence went out to save and redeem each and every one of Bnei Yisrael from Egypt.

6. The explanation of the difference between the two aforementioned conducts, "I will go out" and "I will pass," according to Chassidus:

The entire matter of the redemption from Egypt came about through "Hashem in His glory and essence", His being and essence, may He be blessed, and as the scripture says, "I will go out," I literally, His essence and being, may He be blessed, which is above the entire Seder Hishtalshelus.

This is also the inner reason why the striking of the Egyptians was by the way, "and every firstborn... died," similar to what is explained regarding the matter of "He commanded and they were created", the passive voice, because from the level of the *makif* (encompassing light), everything comes not by way of action ("He created") but as if by the way ("they were created"). So too in our case, since the plague of the firstborn was from Hashem in His glory and essence, it was in the manner of "and every firstborn died."

For this reason — "in one passing... they are all struck," by way of "passing" without division (as explained at length above), because Hashem in His glory and essence makes small and great equal.

However, all this is regarding the nations of the world. This is not the case regarding Bnei Yisrael, for it is known that even from the level of the *makif*, the affection is for Bnei Yisrael. On the contrary, precisely in this is revealed the true affection of Israel. As is explained regarding the matter of "Was not Esav a brother to Yaakov, etc., yet I loved Yaakov and I hated Esav," that even from the level of the *makif*, where "the darkness is as the light" and consequently "Esav was a brother to Yaakov," nevertheless, "I loved Yaakov and I hated Esav." This is the matter of Hashem’s choosing Bnei Yisrael not because of a reason, for even though seemingly we are speaking of a level where the virtue of Israel is not apparent relative to Esav (because "the darkness is as the light"), nevertheless, "I loved Yaakov" specifically.

And from this affection, "Hashem left the upper ones and the lower ones... He chose Israel His people and brought them out of Egypt, the nakedness of the land, a place of filth and impurity, not through an angel... but Hashem in His glory and essence descended there, as it is written, 'And I have descended to save him, etc.'" This means that this affection of Hashem for Bnei Yisrael caused Hashem to descend and *tzimtzum* Himself into the affairs of the children of Israel, to the point of watching over them with *hashgacha pratis* and saving them.

This is what is meant by "I will go out into the midst of Egypt," which is Hashem’s "going out" from His place ("Hashem left the upper ones") and His descent "into the midst of Egypt," and all this is to save Israel.

7. Now, this matter, that this descent of Hashem to save Israel is because of Hashem’s love for Bnei Yisrael (from the inwardness of the *makif*), is also expressed in the details of the miracle of "And I will pass over you." As is brought in Rashi, that even "when an Israelite was in the house of an Egyptian, I might hear that he would be struck like him, therefore the scripture says, 'And there will be no plague among you.'" Seemingly, one must ask: what was the initial assumption that because an Israelite was in an Egyptian’s house, "he would be struck like him"?

The matter is that from the very fact that this Israelite was in an Egyptian’s house on this night, his low spiritual state is understood. After all that the Egyptians did to them, and after all the plagues that the Egyptians received, this person is still in the house of an Egyptian! And when? On this night when he was commanded to offer the Pesach sacrifice (for his salvation), and was warned, "And you shall not go out, any man from the opening of his house until morning"!

## 51

Therefore, the initial assumption was that "he would be struck like him," since the distinction between him and the Egyptian was not apparent.

Nevertheless, because of the affection for each and every one of Israel, "I loved Yaakov" specifically, and it was not enough that Hashem saved even this Israelite (which could have been done even by an angel), but furthermore, Hashem in His glory and essence descended to save him from the hand of Egypt. As mentioned above regarding the interpretation of "I will go out into the midst of Egypt," which was a descent and *hitlabshut* (clothing) into the midst of Egypt, the nakedness of the land,

[which is one of the reasons that it could not be through an angel, because an angel could not descend into the impurity of Egypt].

8. From here there is a lesson in the *avodah* of each and every one of Israel in his efforts to bring the heart of Israel to our Father in Heaven: There are those who say that they are ready to engage in bringing Bnei Yisrael closer to Torah and mitzvos, but not to descend from their level for this. For example, a person who is engaged in the study of Torah is ready to study Torah even with simple people, and even with an Israelite who is for the time being far from the Torah of Hashem, but this applies when they are ready to come to the house of Hashem, to the beis medrash and the four cubits of Torah to study with him. It is otherwise when he needs to stop his study and descend to engage with those who do not yet belong to entering the beis medrash to study, because they are "infants captured among the gentiles." He claims that this engagement does not belong to him.

To this is the lesson — "It is enough for the servant to be like his master". Just as Hashem in His glory and essence descended into Egypt, the nakedness of the land, a place of filth and impurity, to bring them out from there, and even to such a person who was in an Egyptian’s house, and all this to bring him closer to Him, and in a true closeness and union through Torah and mitzvos — so too must each and every one of Bnei Yisrael "descend" from his level to save Bnei Yisrael, even "infants captured" from Israel, and even if because of this descent he soils his garments (as is understood and simple, in a manner permitted according to halacha).

Moreover, after Hashem said at the giving of the Torah, "I am Hashem your G-d" in the singular, Hashem became the power and vitality of each and every one of Israel in whatever status and situation he may be, and one only needs to reveal his true and inner will.

The redemption of all of Israel — that each and every one of Israel, even your heads and your tribes, and likewise the redemption of the Shechinah, that "the Shechinah is in exile" — depends on the redemption of each and every one, for in this redemption of the future, no one will remain in exile, to the point that even that type who were not redeemed from Egypt will be redeemed in the future redemption that is to come through Mashiach Tzidkeinu,

as discussed many times regarding the interpretation of the answer to the wicked son, "If he had been there, he would not have been redeemed,"

that only there in Egypt he would not have been redeemed, but in our redemption from this exile, he too will be redeemed.

And through the engagement of each and every one of Israel to bring Bnei Yisrael closer to their Father in Heaven, Hashem goes with him to assist him in this work of his.

And through this, Bnei Yisrael are redeemed from the exile, "And you shall be gathered one by one, O children of Israel", through Mashiach Tzidkeinu, who builds the Temple and gathers the dispersed of Israel, immediately and truly.

(From a Sicha of Shabbos Parshas Bo, 5736)

## Sicha 3

### 59

**A.** "And every firstborn donkey you shall redeem with a lamb, and if you do not redeem it, you shall break its neck". Rashi comments on the words "you shall break its neck" (*ve-arafto*): "Break its neck with a cleaver from behind and kill it. He caused the Kohen to lose his money, therefore he shall lose his money."

Seemingly, Rashi is explaining two distinct matters here: (1) The definition of *arifa* (breaking the neck) — "Break its neck with a cleaver from behind and kill it," meaning the killing of the firstborn donkey must be performed in a specific manner, "break its neck with a cleaver, etc." (2) The reason for the mitzvah of *arifa* — "He caused the Kohen to lose his money, therefore he shall lose his money." It is difficult to understand why Rashi combined these two matters into a single comment [unlike his usual practice in most places, where he separates different matters (even when explaining a single word) into two separate entries].

Furthermore: What Rashi writes, "Break its neck with a cleaver from behind and kill it," is derived from the (anonymous) Mishnah ("If he did not wish to redeem it, he breaks its neck with a cleaver from behind and buries it"), which everyone agrees upon. However, the reason for the *arifa*—"He caused the Kohen to lose his money, therefore he shall lose his money"—is the opinion of Levi (in the *beraita* cited in the Gemara), "Levi taught: He caused the Kohen to lose his money, etc."

In the Mechilta here, it states: "'And if you do not redeem it, you shall break its neck'—from here they said that the mitzvah of redemption takes precedence over the mitzvah of *arifa*. Another explanation: 'And if you do not redeem it, you shall break its neck'—if you do not redeem it, break its neck; since you caused the Kohen’s assets to be lost, your assets shall also be lost." The plain reading of the Mechilta implies that these are two different opinions, and this reason for *arifa* ("since you caused, etc.") is not accepted by all.

[As explained by the commentators, according to the first opinion, *arifa* is a mitzvah just like redemption, only that the mitzvah of redemption takes precedence; whereas according to the "another explanation," the *arifa* of a firstborn donkey is not a mitzvah, but rather a punishment and a fine for the donkey's owner for not redeeming it with a lamb (as per the opinion of Levi in the *beraita*)].

Based on this, it requires even greater explanation why Rashi combined these two matters—the definition of *arifa* (which is for everyone) and the reason for *arifa* (which is only according to Levi, and the second opinion in the Mechilta)—into a single entry.

**B.** This can be understood by first asking: Why

## 60

does Rashi provide a reason for *arifa* at all? Rashi’s commentary on the Torah is not a book of *taamei hamitzvos* (reasons for the commandments); its purpose is only to explain the plain meaning of the verse (*peshuto shel mikra*), and in the vast majority of cases, we do not find Rashi explaining the reasons for the mitzvos.

It can be said that the necessity in this case to find a reason (even according to the *peshat*) for the mitzvah of *arifa* is because of the specific manner of killing the firstborn donkey, specifically "you shall break its neck":

Killing a donkey by *arifa*—"breaking its neck with a cleaver from behind"—is a death associated with suffering and pain, because by breaking the neck from behind with a "cleaver" ("a large knife"), the neck bone is broken, etc.. And it is difficult to understand: even if there is a need to kill the firstborn donkey (since "every firstborn is for Hashem", and just as a firstborn of a kosher animal must be slaughtered for Hashem, so too a firstborn donkey that one did not wish to redeem must be killed), nevertheless, why must its death be in such a manner?

The caution against *tzaar baalei chayim* (causing pain to living creatures) is a matter understood by simple logic (even for a "five-year-old beginning Scripture"). Furthermore: It is explicitly stated above in *Parshas Bereishis* that "it was not permitted for Adam and his wife to kill a creature and eat meat" (for Adam and Chava, the killing of a creature was not permitted even for the sake of eating). From this, it is understood that even when it was later permitted (to Noach), there must be caution not to cause pain to the animal, which is the simple reason for the prohibition of *ever min hachai* (a limb from a living creature), and therefore the slaughtering of an animal for eating is done with a knife (as cited in Rashi above in *Parshas Vayeira*).

Therefore, after Rashi explains what *arifa* is—"break its neck with a cleaver from behind and kill it"—he is compelled to continue and explain the reason for this: "He caused the Kohen to lose his money, therefore he shall lose his money."

The explanation of the matter:
The fact that the donkey's owner does not wish to redeem it is merely a "loss" (damage), for he has no pleasure or gain from this, since before its redemption it is forbidden for benefit (as Rashi writes in the entry before this, "He gives a lamb to the Kohen and the firstborn donkey is permitted for benefit," where the simple intent of Rashi is that the redemption via a lamb is what permits the firstborn donkey for benefit). Furthermore, if he does not redeem it, he must kill it—if so, what

## 61

pleasure does he have in the absence of the donkey's redemption? On the contrary, if he had redeemed it with a lamb, he would have profited, because the value of the lamb is less than the value of the donkey. If so, the absence of the donkey's redemption is nothing but a loss of the Kohen's money (preventing him from receiving the lamb), without any benefit or gain for himself, and this is the opposite of kindness and mercy.

Therefore, he is punished *midah keneged midah* (measure for measure): not only is his donkey killed, but furthermore, "he breaks its neck with a cleaver from behind and kills it," for killing in this manner emphasizes that it is an act of pure loss—"he shall lose his money"—and through this, he will take to heart the cruelty in his conduct, etc.

**C.** In this way, we can also explain the precision of Rashi’s language, "He caused the Kohen to lose his money, therefore he shall lose his money." The language of the *beraita* is "therefore his money shall be lost" (*yufsod*), [and similarly in the Mechilta, the language is "your assets shall be lost"], whereas Rashi changed it and wrote "he shall lose his money" (*yafsid*).

Based on the above, it can be said:
The difference in the plain sense between the two phrasings—"his money shall be lost" or "he shall lose his money"—is: "His money shall be lost" refers to the object (*cheftza*) of "his money"; since he caused the Kohen's money to be lost, therefore his own money is lost [and according to this, one could say it is a matter of an obligation of payment, that since he caused a loss in his fellow's money, an obligation of payment (and lien) falls upon his money, and something similar applies here, that since he caused the Kohen's money to be lost, an obligation of "loss" falls upon his money]. In contrast, "he shall lose his money" refers to the person (*gavra*), and in this phrasing, the punishment on the person is more emphasized—that he himself must lose his money.

This is the reason Rashi changed it to "he shall lose his money," because according to his view, the primary injustice in the absence of redeeming the firstborn donkey is not the monetary aspect (that he caused a deficiency in the Kohen's money), but the cruelty of the person, whose intent is only to cause the Kohen to lose money without any benefit or gain for himself, and "therefore he shall lose his money," meaning he must feel the matter of loss in his money.

Based on this, perhaps one can add further that the phrase "he shall lose his money" in Rashi here means not only that he suffers a loss in his money, but that he causes a loss and damage to his money, that he must lose his money with his own hands, for the act of *arifa* (causing his money to be lost) must be done by the donkey's owner himself.

That is to say: Since he acted in a cruel manner regarding the Kohen's money, "he caused the Kohen's money to be lost," therefore his punishment is that he (himself) must perform a cruel act regarding his own money, "he shall lose his money."

**D.** It has been explained many times that even though in his commentary on the Torah, Rashi only comes to explain the plain meaning of the verse, nevertheless, "wondrous matters" are also hinted at within it regarding other parts of the Torah, and also according to *halacha*.

So too it can be said in this case, that according to the above regarding the precision of Rashi's language "he shall lose his money," one can learn the definition of the obligation of *arifa* according to *halacha*.

Regarding the redemption of a firstborn donkey, it is stated in the Gemara, "One who redeems the firstborn donkey of his fellow, its redemption is valid." In the *Minchat Chinuch*, he inquires what the law is regarding the *arifa* of a firstborn donkey, whether it is possible to perform *arifa* on a firstborn donkey belonging to one's fellow.

The *Minchat Chinuch* there approaches it based on the question of how a person can damage his fellow's money, for since the owners can redeem the donkey at any time if they wish, when he performs *arifa*, he is damaging his fellow's money. However, one must examine this based on what Rashi writes in the Gemara: "Therefore his money shall be lost; the *Beis Din* shall force him to perform *arifa* after thirty days." Thus, according to Rashi, after thirty days, the *Beis Din* forces him to perform *arifa*, and since that is the case, seemingly there is room to say that after thirty days, it is permitted to perform *arifa* on the firstborn donkey of one's fellow, and there is no law of damaging a fellow's money in this (since he no longer has the option to redeem it, as the *Beis Din* forces him to perform *arifa* after thirty days).

However, based on what was explained above regarding the intent of Rashi's language "he shall lose his money," it is understood that this is an obligation of the owners, that the donkey's owner must perform the *arifa* (lose his money), and his fellow cannot merit the fulfillment of this obligation.

One should add further: Regarding the redemption of a firstborn donkey of one's fellow, there is a discussion among the commentators as to the reason for the matter. In *Tosafos*, it states that the mitzvah of redemption is not an obligation of the owners (and it is like the redemption of *hekdesh*, where any person can redeem *hekdesh* money, not just the owners who dedicated it). But according to several commentators, the redemption of a firstborn donkey is an obligation of the owners, and redemption by one's fellow is effective due to (acquisition and) agency.

Based on the above regarding Rashi's approach in his commentary on the Torah, it seems to me that all of this is only regarding redemption; however, regarding *arifa*, it would not be effective even if he explicitly appointed his fellow as an agent, because the obligation of *arifa* is a punishment on the person (*gavra*), which must be done with his own hands (as mentioned above), for only through this is his punishment fulfilled.

**E.** There is still a difficulty in Rashi's language, "He caused the Kohen's money to be lost," for seemingly, there is no loss of the Kohen's money here, but only the prevention of a gain—that he should have given a lamb to the Kohen and did not give it, but he did not cause the Kohen to lose any money (that he already possessed).

Seemingly, one could say that even the prevention of a gain is called a loss (at least according to the *peshat*). But

## 63

it is somewhat difficult to interpret the language "he caused the Kohen's money to be lost" in this way, which implies that it refers to money that the Kohen had already acquired. If the intent were only the prevention of a gain, it should have said, "he caused a loss to the Kohen" or something similar (and not "he caused the Kohen's money to be lost").

Therefore, it seems to me that according to Rashi in the *peshuto shel mikra*, the Kohen's right is not only in the redemption of the firstborn donkey, but in the firstborn donkey itself. If so, Rashi's intent in "he caused the Kohen's money to be lost" is not regarding the prevention of the gain of the lamb, but regarding the firstborn donkey itself.

The explanation of the matter:
According to the *peshat*, it implies that the definition of the redemption of a firstborn donkey is that one redeems the donkey from the Kohen, for the firstborn donkey belongs to the Kohen, but the Torah said that one does not give the firstborn donkey itself to the Kohen, rather one must redeem it from his hand by giving a lamb in place of the donkey.

As is proven from the plain meaning of the verses in *Parshas Korach* (where only there is it explicitly stated regarding the giving (of the redemption of the firstborn son and the redemption of the firstborn donkey) to the Kohen): "Every firstborn of the womb of all flesh which they offer to Hashem, in man and in beast, shall be yours; however, you shall surely redeem the firstborn of man, and the firstborn of the unclean beast you shall redeem." It is explicitly stated that "every firstborn of the womb of all flesh... in man and in beast, shall be yours" (to the Kohen), but then the verse excludes the firstborn of man and the firstborn of an unclean beast, that they themselves are not given to the Kohen, but only their redemption.

It turns out that the definition of the redemption of a firstborn donkey is—the redemption of the donkey from the hand of the Kohen, for essentially, immediately upon its birth—"it shall be yours," it belongs to the Kohanim, but the Torah commanded the Jew to redeem it from the Kohen (and also eased it for the Jew, that he can redeem it with a lamb).

This is the interpretation of Rashi's language, "he caused

## 64

the Kohen's money to be lost," for one who does not wish to redeem the donkey is causing the Kohanim's money to be lost, because (according to the *peshat*) the firstborn donkey—"it shall be yours," and even though the Torah commanded the Jew to redeem it, nevertheless, the Kohanim have a right in this donkey equal to the value of the redemption (the lamb).

(From the Sichos of Shabbos Parshas Tetzei and Shabbos Parshas Tavo, 5741)


## בשלח

## Sicha 2

1. Our Rebbe writes in his Shulchan Aruch regarding the laws of the Melaveh Malkah meal: "It is good to cook meat or another dish on Motzaei Shabbos in honor of this meal. In our times, since the third meal (*Seudah Shlishis*) is often delayed to the point that one cannot eat on Motzaei Shabbos, one may fulfill this meal with fruit." He then continues (in parentheses): "One need not advance the third meal in order to eat this meal properly, as this meal is not a strict obligation, but rather a *mitzvah min hamuvchar* (a preferred, optimal way to perform a mitzvah)."

Seemingly, this requires some clarification: why does he state that it is "not a strict obligation, but rather a *mitzvah min hamuvchar*"? It would seemingly have been more appropriate to say that it is merely a "mitzvah" or something similar, rather than a *mitzvah min hamuvchar*, which implies that *lechatchilah* (ideally), the mitzvah should be performed in this specific manner.

2. This can be understood by first explaining the source and definition of the Melaveh Malkah meal. The obligation of the three Shabbos meals is derived in the Gemara from the three times the word "today" (*hayom*) is mentioned regarding the Manna: "And Moses said, eat it today, for today is a Shabbos to Hashem; today you will not find it in the field" (some say this is a Torah-level obligation). In contrast, we do not find a scriptural source for the Melaveh Malkah meal.

The Gemara states: "A person should always set his table on Motzaei Shabbos, even if he only needs a *kezayis*." Rashi explains: "On Motzaei Shabbos, this is also for the honor of Shabbos, to escort it upon its departure in a respectful manner, like a person who escorts a king as he leaves the city." Later in the Gemara, it is related that on Motzaei Shabbos, they would prepare a "three-year-old calf" for Rabbi Avahu (even though he would only eat a small amount, a "kidney"). It is understood simply that this is the reason for the meal: to honor Shabbos upon its departure ("like a person who escorts a king as he leaves the city") through this meal.

It should be noted that this statement does not speak of an obligation to eat a meal on Motzaei Shabbos, but only about setting the table. This is proven by the fact that this statement is brought in the Shas following the statement of Rabbi Elazar: "A person should always set his table on Erev Shabbos, even if he only needs a *kezayis*." It is clear that "setting his table on Erev Shabbos" is not the obligation of the Friday night meal (which is at night), but rather the obligation of setting the table on Erev Shabbos (before Shabbos begins).

[This is a law of *Kavod Shabbos*, as the Rambam writes (after bringing these two statements as law regarding setting the table on Erev Shabbos and Motzaei Shabbos): "in order to honor it upon its entry and departure." He brings this there in the context of other preparations that one is obligated to perform at home on Erev Shabbos for the sake of *Kavod Shabbos*—whereas the law of the Shabbos meals is not a matter of *Kavod Shabbos*, but rather *Oneg Shabbos*.]

From this, it is understood that the statement "a person should set his table on Motzaei Shabbos" also does not speak of an obligation to eat a meal, but rather about the manner of arranging the table for a meal to honor Shabbos upon its departure. In the words of the Alter Rebbe here: "He should make preparations by setting the table, such as spreading a tablecloth and the like" [for even if the person does not intend to eat more than a *kezayis*, it is a matter of *Kavod Shabbos* (even upon its departure) to set the table as if he were going to eat a "complete meal"].

Seemingly, there is room to say that this meal is not an obligation at all, but rather that since it is the way of people to eat on Motzaei Shabbos—even though they have already eaten the third meal, since it is usually nighttime, which is a time for a meal—they eat another meal (at least a small one). [Only regarding one who lives on charity is there a consideration in the Gemara not to give him a meal for Motzaei Shabbos, "for we say to him: what you want to eat at the departure of Shabbos, eat on Shabbos itself."] Thus, the law of Melaveh Malkah is that due to the obligation of *Kavod Shabbos*, one is obligated to prepare the table for this meal (which people eat anyway) "like a complete meal." But one who does not eat a meal every night has no obligation to eat this one.

However, one cannot say this, for it is proven in many places that the meal itself is a *mitzvah*. As the Alter Rebbe says, "one can fulfill this meal with fruit," and he subsequently writes, "that this meal is not a strict obligation, but rather a *mitzvah min hamuvchar*." From this, it is understood that the meal itself is at least a minor obligation (and there are even those who say that this meal must consist of bread).

Thus, we must explain the source and reason for this meal.

3. There is also a difficulty in the aforementioned words of Rashi, that setting the table for Melaveh Malkah is "to escort it upon its departure in a respectful manner, like a person who escorts a king as he leaves the city." This analogy is understood: until that point, the Shabbos has not yet "left" (like in the analogy, where at the time one escorts the king, the king is still among the people escorting him). Seemingly, even though one is obligated to add from the profane to the holy both upon its entry and its departure, the concept of Melaveh Malkah is specifically *after* Havdalah and the departure of Shabbos.

In the Shulchan Aruch of the Alter Rebbe, it is written: "There are those who have the custom... to say *piyutim* and *zemiros* after Havdalah to escort the Shabbos after it has departed, in the manner that one escorts a king after he has left the city." This still requires explanation, as seemingly, in the analogy, the king's departure is only from the city, but the escorts are still with the king; whereas in the subject, seemingly after Havdalah, the Shabbos is no longer "together" with the Jewish people.

It must be that even after the departure of Shabbos (at Havdalah), it has not yet completely left. The commentaries on the Shulchan Aruch cite in the name of the students of the Arizal: "The *neshamah yeseirah* does not leave completely until after the Motzaei Shabbos meal; therefore, it is not proper to engage in work that is not for the sake of food until after the Motzaei Shabbos meal."

We must explain the connection between the departure of the *neshamah yeseirah* (and Shabbos in general) and the Melaveh Malkah meal, such that until this meal, it is like a king leaving the city who is still nearby and with the people of the city, and the people of the city escort him.

4. The explanation for all this is as follows:
