The descent of the Manna was in a manner where "the night follows the day," as is explicit in the verse that its descent was in the morning, and from the *omer* of Manna that descended in the morning, there was food for two meals: the morning meal and the evening meal (as the *Baalei Tosafot* write, "all year round they would make two loaves from the *omer*").

From this, it is also understood regarding the Manna that descended on Erev Shabbos, "two *omers* for one," which was in the manner of "the night follows the day." The first *omer* provided the Friday meal and the Friday night meal, and the second *omer* sufficed not only for the two meals of the day of Shabbos, but also for the meal of the night that followed (Motzaei Shabbos).

[It is forced to say that they did not eat any meal on Motzaei Shabbos because they had already eaten the third meal on the day of Shabbos itself, for it is astonishing to say that during the forty years the Israelites were in the desert, they did not eat any meal on Motzaei Shabbos (because they had nothing to eat). Beyond the fact that a person generally eats a meal (even a small one) on Motzaei Shabbos even when they have eaten the third meal (as mentioned in section 2), there is more (and this is the essence): since, due to *Kavod Shabbos*, one is obligated to escort the Shabbos upon its departure by setting the table for a complete meal, it is obvious that they did this in the desert as well (and it should be noted that it is a great stretch to say that there is a concept of setting a table for a complete meal without eating at all).]

Thus, it turns out that the bread for the Motzaei Shabbos meal was from the *omer* of Manna that was designated for Shabbos.

Based on this, we can add an explanation to the words of our Sages regarding the verse "And He blessed... and He sanctified": "He blessed it with Manna and sanctified it with Manna; He blessed it with Manna... on Erev Shabbos with two *omers*, and sanctified it with Manna in that it did not descend at all." It can be said that the difference between the two matters of "blessed" and "sanctified" is not only whether it is a positive matter (that two *omers* descended on Erev Shabbos) or a negative one (that the Manna did not descend on it), but rather they are two different definitions (and times):

"Sanctified it with Manna" refers to the matter of Manna in connection with the holiness of Shabbos, for the holiness of Shabbos requires that the Manna not descend during this holy time, and this matter is only during the hours of Shabbos itself, during the time of Shabbos holiness (including the time of adding from the profane to the holy, when there is also Shabbos holiness).

In contrast, "blessed it with Manna"—the blessing of the Manna for the day of Shabbos is not limited to the time of Shabbos holiness, but to the *me'at-le'et* (the 24-hour cycle) of Shabbos. And since the time of the *me'at-le'et* regarding the descent of the Manna is in the manner of "the night follows the day," so too regarding the time of the blessing of the Manna of Shabbos, it is such that the blessing of the Manna of the day of Shabbos extends to Motzaei Shabbos.

[On the contrary, the Friday night meal was from the first *omer* that descended for Erev Shabbos, and it was specifically the Motzaei Shabbos meal that was from the second *omer* that descended specifically for the day of Shabbos, in which the miracle occurred that "it did not rot, and there was no worm in it."]

5. It can be said that this is also the source for the fact that the matter of Shabbos extends until the time of the Motzaei Shabbos meal (as mentioned in section 3):
Even though the holiness of Shabbos (proper) is only until after Havdalah, when one separates between the holy and the profane, the blessing of Shabbos ("And G-d blessed the seventh day," "Therefore Hashem blessed the Shabbos day") is in a manner that it extends until the Motzaei Shabbos meal, just as the "blessing with Manna" also included the blessing and provision of food for the Motzaei Shabbos meal.

[It is understood and simple that just as the holiness of Shabbos, even though our Sages explained that "He sanctified it" means "He sanctified it with Manna," is nevertheless the holiness of Shabbos in every time and every generation, so too the matter of the blessing of Shabbos was not only in the time of the desert when the Manna descended, but is a constant matter in every Shabbos in every generation.]

This is the reason why this meal is a "Melaveh Malkah" (Escorting the Queen) meal, for then one escorts the Shabbos upon its departure, because in this meal is the completion of the "blessing" of Shabbos.

Based on the above, perhaps we can say further that the foundation of this meal is a remembrance of the Manna. Since the *omer* of Manna of Shabbos included the meal of Motzaei Shabbos, therefore, just as one is obligated to eat three meals on Shabbos as a remembrance of the Manna, this remembrance itself also obligates the eating of this meal (just as the Israelites in the desert ate this meal from the Manna that descended for the day of Shabbos).

Based on this, we can explain the aforementioned language of the Alter Rebbe, "that this meal is not a strict obligation, but rather a *mitzvah min hamuvchar*." It can be said that his intention is that through the Melaveh Malkah meal, the mitzvah of the three meals becomes a "*mitzvah min hamuvchar*." That is to say, the obligation of the Melaveh Malkah meal is not just a matter unto itself (to escort the Shabbos, which is not a matter for the other Shabbos meals that take place on Shabbos itself), but it is (also) part of the obligation of the three meals, for the fulfillment of the mitzvah of the three meals in the manner of "*mitzvah min hamuvchar*" is when one also eats the Melaveh Malkah meal, for then the remembrance of "He blessed it with Manna" is complete.

6. The explanation of the matter of the Melaveh Malkah meal according to *drush* (Chassidic discourse) can be understood by first noting what is written in the *Beis Yosef* that "there is a limb in a person... and it does not derive pleasure from eating except on Motzaei Shabbos." From this, it is understood that there is a matter in the Melaveh Malkah meal that is not in the Shabbos meals, for this limb—which is the *etzem luz* (the resurrection bone)—does not derive pleasure from any eating (even that of the Shabbos meals) except for that of Motzaei Shabbos alone.

The matter can be explained according to what is written in the *Eliyahu Rabbah* that the *etzem luz* did not derive pleasure from the Tree of Knowledge (for since "it does not derive pleasure from any eating... except when one eats on Motzaei Shabbos," it did not derive pleasure from eating from the Tree of Knowledge on Erev Shabbos), and "therefore it does not decay." For the entire matter of death came about because of the sin of the Tree of Knowledge, for which death was decreed upon the world. But the *etzem luz*, which did not derive pleasure from the Tree of Knowledge, is above the matter of death, and from this bone the body will be rebuilt for the resurrection of the dead.

Seemingly, this requires clarification: since this *etzem luz* is above the matter of death and does not need eating for its existence, why does it derive pleasure from the Melaveh Malkah meal? And on the other hand, if there is an eating that belongs to this bone, why is it not from the eating of Shabbos itself, whose virtue is unique in that the eating itself is a mitzvah, and "it does not say 'your Shabbos-spread'"—but only from the meal of Motzaei Shabbos?

The explanation for this according to *drush* is as follows: The ultimate intent in the creation of the Tree of Knowledge was not only to test the first man so that he would not eat from it, but so that man would transform the Tree of Knowledge and elevate it above the matter of death. As explained in the *sefarim*, if the first man had waited three hours until the day of Shabbos, he would have eaten from the Tree of Knowledge and there would have been no matter of death at all. This is because Shabbos is a foretaste of the life of the World to Come, above the category of death, and if he had waited to eat the fruit of the Tree of Knowledge until Shabbos, he would have been above the matter of death.

Now, after the sin, when the matter of death already exists in the world, even though there is Shabbos, which is a foretaste of the World to Come, nevertheless, since the day of Shabbos is distinct and holy from the other days, the "life" of Shabbos does not extend into the place of death itself—that is, as man lives in the world of the profane (where death was decreed upon him).

It can be said that this is the inner content of the Melaveh Malkah meal: the rectification of the sin of the Tree of Knowledge. For in this meal, there is the connection of Shabbos and the profane. On one hand, it is after the completion of the holiness of Shabbos, but on the other hand, it contains the blessing (and the extension) of the day of Shabbos, through which the power of Shabbos is extended even into the meal of the profane, as discussed at length above.

This is also why the *etzem luz* derives pleasure specifically from the Motzaei Shabbos meal, for the rectification of the sin in practice is the matter of the resurrection of the dead, when "He will swallow up death forever." This comes through the *etzem luz*, which is above the matter of death, as mentioned. That is, the matter of this bone is not only that it remains in existence, but that from it the entire body must be built, so that even the body that had the category of death in it will be refined and rectified until it rises in the resurrection of the dead and lives in eternal life ("He will swallow up death forever").

The power for this is through deriving pleasure from the Motzaei Shabbos meal, for even in a meal at a time of the profane, one extends the blessing of Shabbos, a foretaste of the World to Come.

## Sicha 3

### 77

1. Regarding the verse "Six days you shall gather it, but on the seventh day, which is Shabbos, there will be none," Rashi explains: "And on the seventh day, Shabbos — it is Shabbos, the manna will not be there; and the verse comes only to include Yom Kippur and Yomim Tovim."

Its source is the teaching of Rabbi Elazar HaModa'i in the Mechilta on this verse: "Six days you shall gather it; Rabbi Yehoshua says: We have learned that it does not descend on Shabbos. From where do we know this regarding Yom Tov? The verse teaches, 'Shabbos there will be none.' Rabbi Elazar HaModa'i says: We have learned that it does not descend on Shabbos. From where do we know this regarding Yom Tov? The verse teaches, 'There will be none.' From where do we know this regarding Yom Kippur? The verse teaches, 'Shabbos there will be none.'"

However, Rashi deviates from the teaching of Rabbi Elazar in several details: a) In the Mechilta, there is a separate derivation for Yom Tov and a separate derivation for Yom Kippur, whereas Rashi includes them together. b) In the Mechilta, Yom Tov precedes Yom Kippur, whereas Rashi places Yom Kippur before Yom Tov. c) In the Mechilta, Yom Tov is derived from the words "there will be none," and Yom Kippur from "Shabbos there will be none," while Rashi’s language is "the verse comes only to include," meaning the entire verse comes to include both Yom Kippur and Yom Tov.

Simply put, the basis for these changes is: According to the Mechilta, the cessation of the manna on Yom Tov and Yom Kippur is not defined by the same category as its cessation on Shabbos (and furthermore, their cessation on Yom Tov and Yom Kippur are two different categories of cessation). Conversely, according to Rashi, they are all of the same type. This is also emphasized in Rashi’s heading, "And on the seventh day, Shabbos," upon which he comments, "It is Shabbos, the manna will not be there (and he continues), and the verse comes only to include Yom Kippur and Yomim Tovim" (for since it was already stated, "For today is Shabbos to Hashem, today you will not find it in the field, six days you shall gather it," it must be that the verse comes only to include Yom Kippur and Yom Tov). Thus, Yom Kippur and Yom Tov are learned together from the verse, "And on the seventh day, Shabbos, etc."

The explanation for this: According to the Mechilta, the fact that the manna did not descend on Yom Kippur and Yom Tov is because these days are also special, holy days, distinct from the other days of the week (the weekdays) [and since the holiness of Yom Kippur differs from the holiness of Shabbos, and the holiness of Yom Tov differs from that of Yom Kippur, a special derivation is needed for each]. Conversely, according to Rashi, the fact that it did not descend on these days is due to the category of "Shabbos" inherent in them, as they are also included in the category of Shabbosos of Hashem.

This is also the reason Rashi placed Yom Kippur before Yom Tov, because on Yom Kippur it is more straightforward that it is called "Shabbos".

2. Now, the Tosafos write in several places that there are differing traditions regarding whether the manna descended on Yom Tov. For from what is stated regarding Shabbos (the seventh day), "And He blessed... and He sanctified, He blessed it with manna and sanctified it with manna " (that on Shabbos the manna did not descend), it is implied that only on Shabbos it did not descend, but on Yom Tov it did. Conversely, according to the Mechilta’s teaching, "Shabbos there will be none to include Yom Kippur, there will be none to include Yom Tov," the manna would not descend on them.

However, Rashi in his commentary on the Torah brought both teachings: On the verse "And He blessed... and He sanctified," he explains: "He blessed it with manna, for all the days of the week an omer descended for each person, and on the sixth day a double portion, and He sanctified it with manna, for it did not descend at all on Shabbos." And together with this, he brought in our Parsha (as mentioned above) that the manna did not descend on Yom Kippur and Yomim Tovim.

Based on the above, it is understood that according to Rashi, the fact that the manna did not descend on Yom Kippur and Yom Tov is not due to the holiness of the day inherent in them (which are also distinct from the days before and after them for being *mikra'ei kodesh*), but rather due to the category of "Shabbos" that is in them.

3. It can be said that there is a practical difference (*nafka mina*) in Halacha. To preface:

Regarding the obligation of *lechem mishneh*, the Rif writes that the obligation on Yom Tov is like that of Shabbos: "For Rabbi Abba said: A person is obligated to break bread over two whole loaves on Shabbos. What is the reason? 'They gathered a double portion of bread' is written. And the Sages explained that on Yomim Tovim as well, we require breaking bread over two loaves just like on Shabbos, for the primary obligation on Shabbos is because the manna did not descend on Shabbos, but rather descended on Erev Shabbos in double portions. And on Yomim Tovim as well, it did not descend, but rather descended on Erev Yom Tov in double portions, just as it descended on Erev Shabbos. Therefore, we require breaking bread on Yom Tov over two whole loaves, just as we are obligated on Shabbos."

Now, the Rambam brought the obligation of *lechem mishneh* on Shabbos and Yom Tov twice, in *Hilchos Brachos* and in *Hilchos Shabbos*. In *Hilchos Brachos* he wrote: "On Shabbosos and Yomim Tovim, one is obligated to break bread over two loaves; he takes both in his hand and breaks one of them." And in *Hilchos Shabbos* he wrote briefly: "And one must establish each of the three meals over wine and break bread over two loaves, and likewise on Yomim Tovim."

Although it appears simply that it is the same law and he shortened his language in *Hilchos Shabbos* because he relied on what he wrote previously in *Hilchos Brachos*, it seems to me that these are two separate laws regarding the obligation of *lechem mishneh*.

For this obligation can be explained in two ways:
a) It is a law regarding the Shabbos meal; the definition of a meal is established by bread, and since a Shabbos meal is an important meal, one is obligated to establish it over *lechem mishneh*.
b) The obligation of *lechem mishneh* is a law regarding the breaking of bread. It is a rule of breaking bread that one must recite a blessing and break a whole loaf, but on Shabbos, an additional requirement for "wholeness" was added: because it is an important day, "wholeness" on it means *lechem mishneh*. Therefore, the breaking of the bread must be done over *lechem mishneh*.

There is a simple *nafka mina* between these two approaches: If it is a law regarding the breaking of bread, it only concerns the time of the blessing and the breaking of the bread. Conversely, if it is a definition regarding the importance of the meal, it concerns the entire meal. Therefore, both loaves must be on the table throughout the meal, and it is a mitzvah (the preferred way) to eat from both.

Based on this, it can be said that the reason the Rambam brought this law twice—in *Hilchos Brachos* and *Hilchos Shabbos*—is because he holds that both are present; there are two definitions in the obligation of *lechem mishneh*, both from the law of breaking bread and from the laws of the Shabbos (and Yom Tov) meal.

This is the distinction between the two sets of laws in the Rambam: In *Hilchos Brachos*, he brought the obligation of *lechem mishneh* in continuation of the laws of breaking bread. The beginning of the *halacha* is: "It is a mitzvah from the preferred way to break a whole loaf. If there was a whole loaf of barley and a broken piece of wheat, he places the whole one inside the broken one and breaks from both, so that he breaks from the wheat and from the whole one." And in continuation of this, he brings: "On Shabbosos and Yomim Tovim, one is obligated to break bread over two loaves; he takes both in his hand and breaks one of them." Meaning, from the laws of breaking bread, one is obligated on Shabbos and Yom Tov to break over two loaves.

Conversely, in *Hilchos Shabbos*, he is dealing with the second law of *lechem mishneh*, from the perspective of the importance of the meal, as indicated by his precise language: "A person is obligated to eat three meals on Shabbos, etc., and must establish each of the three meals over wine and break bread over two loaves." From the laws and rules of the three meals on Shabbos, there is an obligation to establish them over wine and break over two loaves.

Therefore, he did not add here (what he wrote in *Hilchos Brachos*) that one breaks only one loaf, because that is only from the law of breaking bread, not from the establishment of the meal (as mentioned above).

According to this, there is another *nafka mina*: In the *Maggid Mishneh* on *Hilchos Shabbos*, he brought regarding the obligation of *lechem mishneh*: "And a Gaon wrote: 'As long as he is eating,'" meaning that this obligation applies to every meal one eats on Shabbos. Seemingly, this is from the law of breaking bread that is in *lechem mishneh*, for then, every time he eats on Shabbos and Yom Tov, he must break over two loaves. But the obligation of *lechem mishneh* from the law of the Shabbos meal could be said to apply only to the obligatory meals, the three Shabbos meals (and not if he adds other optional meals).

4. From here, we must examine the obligation of *lechem mishneh* on Yom Tov, whether it also contains these two laws or not. To preface:

The source for the law of *lechem mishneh* is in *Maseches Shabbos* : "Rabbi Abba said: On Shabbos, a person is obligated to break bread over two loaves, as it is written, 'a double portion of bread.' Rabbi Ashi said: I saw Rav Kahana take two and break one. He said: 'They gathered' is written. Rabbi Zeira would break over the entire loaf."

This *sugya* was also brought in *Maseches Brachos* after the laws of breaking bread regarding whether it is over pieces or over whole loaves, and it says: "A G-d-fearing person fulfills both... he places the broken piece inside the whole one and breaks... Rabbi Papa said: Everyone agrees on Pesach that he places the broken piece inside the whole one and breaks. What is the reason? 'Bread of affliction' is written." And it continues after that, "Rabbi Elazar said, etc."

It can be said simply that this is the source for the Rambam’s words that there are two definitions in the obligation of *lechem mishneh*, from the perspective of the laws of breaking bread and from the perspective of the law of the Shabbos meal. This is the distinction between the two *sugyos* of *Brachos* and *Shabbos*: In *Maseches Brachos*, it is dealing with the laws of breaking bread, as mentioned above, and from this we learn that the obligation of *lechem mishneh* is from the perspective of the laws of breaking bread. And the *sugya* in *Maseches Shabbos* is (primarily) regarding the laws of the Shabbos meals, from which it is understood that the obligation of *lechem mishneh* is a definition regarding the Shabbos meal.

Now, the Tosafos write : "And the essence of the matter of Rabbi Chiya bar Abba in the chapter *Kol Kisvei* is only that he brings it there (in *Brachos*) after Pesach to say that one needs a third matzah there because of *lechem mishneh*, and one is obligated to break over two loaves both on Shabbos and on Yom Tov. For if not so, why did he bring the matter of Rabbi Chiya there? And so ruled Rav Alfas, that one must break over two loaves on Yom Tov, and the reason is because the *lechem mishneh* would descend on Erev Yomim Tovim." Thus, in the *sugya* of *Maseches Brachos*, it was innovated that the obligation of *lechem mishneh* also applies on Yom Tov.

Based on this, it can be said that the fact that the obligation of *lechem mishneh* on Yom Tov is learned in the *sugya* of *Brachos* specifically is because, according to the *Shas*, the fact that we learn the obligation of *lechem mishneh* from Shabbos is only regarding the definition of *lechem mishneh* from the perspective of the laws of breaking bread (which is what is being discussed in *Maseches Brachos* there), but not from the perspective of the definition of a Yom Tov meal (which is what is being discussed in *Maseches Shabbos* there, regarding the laws of the Shabbos meal).

The explanation for this: In the obligation of breaking bread, there is no difference between Shabbos and Yom Tov, and therefore it is logical to say that just as the breaking of bread on Shabbos must be over two loaves, so it is on Yom Tov. Conversely, regarding the matter of the Shabbos and Yom Tov meals, we find several differences between them. Since on Shabbos one is also forbidden from the labor of *ochel nefesh* (food preparation), and there must be extensive preparation for the meal from Erev Shabbos, it follows that the Shabbos meal has more importance and established status than a Yom Tov meal, which can be prepared on the day itself. Therefore, there is room to say that a Yom Tov meal does not mandate the establishment of the meal over two loaves.

Based on all this, it can be said that this depends on the difference between the Mechilta and Rashi on the Torah (mentioned above):

According to Rashi, the fact that the manna did not descend on Yom Tov is due to the category of "Shabbos" that is in it. Similarly, regarding the obligation of *lechem mishneh* (which we learn from the absence of the manna descending on Yom Tov), the definition of the obligation of *lechem mishneh* on Yom Tov is exactly like the obligation of Shabbos, which contains two definitions: both from the perspective of the laws of breaking bread and from the perspective of the obligation of the meal. Conversely, according to the Mechilta, which has a separate derivation that the manna did not descend on Yom Tov, the obligation of *lechem mishneh* on Yom Tov that stems from this is not equal to the obligation of *lechem mishneh* of Shabbos in every respect, because the obligation of *lechem mishneh* on Yom Tov is only from the perspective of the laws of breaking bread, and not from the perspective of the obligation of the Yom Tov meal.

(From a Sicha of Parshas Beshalach 5728)


## ט"ו בשבט

## 82

### Tu B'Shvat

1. We learned in the beginning of Maseches Rosh Hashanah: "There are four Rosh Hashanahs: On the first of Nissan is the Rosh Hashanah for kings and for the festivals... On the first of Shevat is the Rosh Hashanah for the tree, according to the words of Beis Shammai; Beis Hillel says on the fifteenth of it."

Now, beyond the general connection between all four Rosh Hashanahs [since they are all taught in one Mishnah and are all called by the name "Rosh Hashanah," it is understood that there is a connection between them in their content], it is understood according to the well-known principle that "their end is embedded in their beginning, and their beginning in their end," that the content of the Rosh Hashanah mentioned at the beginning of the Mishnah—"the first of Nissan is the Rosh Hashanah for kings and for the festivals"—is specifically connected to the Rosh Hashanah for the tree, which is mentioned at the end of the Mishnah.

Furthermore: Since the Rosh Hashanah for kings and the Rosh Hashanah for the festivals occur on the same day—the first of Nissan —it stands to reason that there is a special connection between them.

2. This can be understood by first noting what the Bachya (and other commentators) writes: that the three festivals were established based on the grain harvest: Pesach at the time of the spring; Shavuos, the festival of the harvest—the wheat harvest; and Sukkos at the time of the ingathering.

The reason why the festivals of the year were established based on the growth of the grain is, according to Chassidus :

Bnei Yisrael are called "the harvest of Hashem," as it is written, "Israel is holy to Hashem, the first of His harvest," and it is written, "And I will sow her for Myself in the land." For the descent of the souls of Israel into this world is like sowing in the earth. Now, our Sages have said, "Does a person sow a kor except to harvest many korim?" The purpose of sowing is the multiplication of the growth, where from one wheat kernel many wheat stalks grow. So it is with Bnei Yisrael : the purpose of their "sowing" in the land—which is a tremendous descent, from a high roof to a deep pit —is the "growth" and development of the soul that comes through this descent. As the well-known saying goes: "This descent is for the sake of an ascent." Through the descent of the soul below, it is elevated to a supreme level, higher than the level of the soul as it was before its descent below.

Moreover: From the growth of the grain, one can also learn the manner of the avodah of the souls of Israel:

## 83

a) The growth of the grain does not come from the wheat kernel itself that is sown in the earth, but rather through the rotting of the sown wheat. By the seed rotting and becoming *batel* (nullified) to the power of growth within the earth, this awakens the power of growth in the earth to sprout stalks of wheat. From this, another matter is understood: that the increase produced by the growth is not merely an increase in quantity (that from one kernel a great multitude is made), but it can be said that it is like a new creation, a different quality.

So it is also with the descent and ascent of the soul. The way to reach the "growth" of the soul through the sowing in the land is by the person being in a state of *bitul* (like the matter of rotting), "my soul shall be as dust to all." This is the avodah through *kabbalas ol* (which is "the beginning of the avodah, its essence, and its root," as written in the holy Tanya). Through this, the ascent of the soul and its "growth" is an ascent that is completely incomparable, becoming like a truly new existence.

b) Just as with physical sowing it is said, "Those who sow in tears shall reap in joy; he goes along weeping, carrying the bag of seed, he shall surely come with joy, carrying his sheaves," that specifically because they "sow in tears... and weeping," with crying and *bitul*, therefore "they shall reap in joy... he shall come with joy, carrying his sheaves"—so too is it in the avodah of Bnei Yisrael. Through *bitul* and the sowing in the land, there is created from this the joy at the time of growth and revelation.

It can be said that in spiritual avodah, the joy revealed at the time of the ascent is not merely a side matter (that because he sees the growth, he is therefore happy), but rather it is connected to the ascent itself. For the ascent of the soul that is in the category of "growth"—which is an incomparable ascent (until it becomes like a new existence)—is specifically through joy. As is known regarding the matter of joy breaking through boundaries, that through true joy all the boundaries and limitations of a person are nullified. This is the wondrous ascent effected in the soul through its avodah in the land, that it becomes like a new existence (as above), which is brought about through joy, the breaking of boundaries, etc.

c) Based on this, we can explain the reason why the three festivals were established based on the times of the year's grain harvest:

The two aforementioned matters that cause the "growth" and ascent of the soul—*bitul* (rotting) and joy—are present in the "three festivals":

On one hand, "regalim" (festivals) is from the word "regel" (foot) [which is why "a minor... who cannot hold his father's hand and ascend from Jerusalem to the Temple Mount" is exempt from *re'iyah* (the appearance offering), because it is written "three *regalim*," and as Rashi explains, "the one who is fit to ascend on his feet is obligated by the verse." And so it is in the Gemara that "one who is lame in one foot is exempt from *re'iyah*, as it is said *regalim*"]—and it is known that "regel" indicates the avodah of *kabbalas ol malchus shamayim*, that he does not do the will of Hashem because of his understanding and grasp or the feeling of his heart (love of Hashem and fear of Him), but does so because of *kabbalas ol* (and this is the matter of the rotting and *bitul* in the sowing).

And on the other hand, they are "appointed times for joy," and this is the joy revealed at the time of growth and ascent.

And this is also the common point between the Rosh Hashanah for the festivals and the Rosh Hashanah for kings:

The role of a king of Israel [the intention in the Rosh Hashanah for kings is the kings of Israel] is

## 84

to activate in all of the people of Israel the matter of *bitul* to Hashem ("sowing in tears," *bitul* and rotting), through which he elevates Bnei Yisrael to a very great ascent.

As is known from the explanation of the Rebbe the Tzemach Tzedek, the essence of a king of Israel is to draw down into all Bnei Yisrael the *bitul* to the King of Kings, the Holy One, Blessed be He. For by a king of Israel there must be the ultimate and complete *bitul* to Hashem, as our Sages said, "He has no one above him except Hashem his God" [which is why "a king, once he bows, does not stand up again," for this is not a matter of caution (so that his heart does not become haughty from his elevation as a king), but this itself is the essence of a king, to bring himself to ultimate *bitul*, until he cannot stand himself up while standing before Hashem]. And through the people nullifying themselves to the king, as it is written, "You shall surely set a king over you," and as our Sages expound, "that his awe should be upon you," the *bitul* of the king to the King of Kings, the Holy One, Blessed be He, is drawn into them.

It can be said that through this *bitul* of the people to the king, he elevates and raises them to a higher level. Since the king is in a state of ultimate elevation and loftiness, and as it is written, "from his shoulders and upward he was taller than all the people," therefore it is in his power to elevate and raise Bnei Yisrael to such a great ascent that is above that which is from their own selves —similar to the matter of growth, which is a new existence, as mentioned above.

d) Now, regarding the joy of the festivals, there are differences among the three festivals themselves, as explained in the Yalkut : "You find three joys written regarding the festival... but on Pesach you do not find even one joy written; why? You find that on Pesach the grain is judged, and a person does not know if the year will produce grain or not, therefore joy is not written there... And likewise you find that only one joy is written regarding Atzeres (Shavuos)... And why is one joy written there? Because the grain is brought inside. And what is the reason two joys are not written there? Because the fruits of the tree are judged... But on the festival (Sukkos), because... the grain and the fruits of the tree are inside, therefore three joys are written."

It turns out that the greatness of the joy of Sukkos is because then "the fruits of the tree are inside" (and not only the grain that is brought inside at the time of Atzeres).

It can be said that the (inner) reason for this is (continuing from the above):

The aforementioned qualities in the matter of sowing are present with greater intensity and strength in trees. For the growth of grain is not in the category of a truly new existence in a revealed way, because even the seed that is sown is wheat or barley or the like (which has taste and is fit for eating). This is not so with the planting of trees, for the seed that is sown has no taste, and the fruits that grow from the tree are a completely different quality from the seed that was sown.

For this reason, there is a difference between sowing and planting regarding the time of growth. In sowing, "there is not such a need for a delay and waiting from the time of sowing until the growth," whereas in planting, "there is a great delay and waiting in the time of its growth, etc.," and this is due to the disparity in value between the seed sown and the fruits that grow from it.

On the other hand, there is a great advantage in the growth of the fruits of the tree over the growth of grain, that

## 85

the fruits of the tree are fit for eating immediately, unlike grain, where the wheat that grows is not fit for food as it is, and it requires several labors until it becomes fit for eating.

This is the reason for the greatness of the joy of Sukkos specifically, for since then it is not only the ingathering of the grain, but also of the fruits of the tree, it turns out that then is the time of ultimate perfection in the matter of growth.

e) Based on the above, the connection between the Rosh Hashanah for kings and the festivals (at the beginning of the Mishnah) to the Rosh Hashanah for the tree (at the end of the Mishnah) is understood—that their beginning is embedded in their end. The elevation hinted at in the beginning of the Mishnah (Rosh Hashanah for kings and festivals), the growth and the (incomparable) ascent, is embedded in its end, the Rosh Hashanah for the tree, in which the perfection of the matter of growth is emphasized, which is specifically in trees (as above).

And in the avodah of man, it can be said based on what is written, "For man is the tree of the field," and it is in the Gemara that this refers to Torah scholars. Based on this, the advantage of growth that is in the tree is from the advantage of Torah. And this is because in Torah it is emphasized that success in it depends specifically on the matter of toil, "that you should be toiling in Torah," as the saying goes, "You toiled—and you found." Through the toil and effort in Torah, which are connected with the *bitul* of man, one merits growth and success that is incomparable to the toil, which brings to true joy, "Those who sow in tears shall reap in joy."

(From a sicha of Tu B'Shvat 5735)


## יתרו

## Sicha 1

## 86

### Sicha 1

A. "Thus shall you say to the house of Jacob, etc. You have seen, etc. And now, if you will indeed listen to My voice, etc. And you shall be to Me, etc. These are the words that you shall speak to the children of Israel." Rashi comments on the words "These are the words": "Neither less nor more."

The commentators ask: Why would it even enter our minds that Moshe Rabbeinu would subtract from or add to the words of Hashem, such that a special warning, "These are the words that you shall speak to the children of Israel — neither less nor more," was necessary? And if such a warning were necessary, why do we not find it regarding other commandments?

The Gur Aryeh answers: "It might have entered our minds that Moshe would entice them on his own—not that he would add to the word of Hashem, but that he would add words of persuasion of his own, telling them that these were his own words. This, too, is forbidden, the reason being so that they would not later say that they were overly enticed into accepting the Torah."

However, this explanation only resolves the need for the warning not to add ("nor more"). It remains difficult: why was the warning "neither less" necessary? Is it conceivable that Moshe Rabbeinu would not deliver the words of Hashem to Bnei Yisrael in their entirety?

It seems to me, simply put, that this Rashi comes as a continuation of his commentary at the beginning of the subject: On "Thus shall you say to the house of Jacob and tell the children of Israel," Rashi comments: "'To the house of Jacob'—these are the women; speak to them in a soft language. 'And tell the children of Israel'—explain the punishments and the details to the men; words as hard as sinews." It turns out that in the manner of delivering the words of Hashem, there was a fundamental difference between men and women: for the men, it was necessary to explain the punishments and the details, whereas for the women, he did not explain the punishments, but spoke in a soft language.

Based on this, one might have thought that the words of Hashem here were stated to Moshe only in a general sense, and afterwards, at the time of delivering the words to Bnei Yisrael, there were differences: for the men, he added an explanation of the punishments and the details (so that the words would be "hard"), and for the women, he subtracted from them in order to fulfill the commandment to "speak to them (the women) in a soft language." Therefore, the verse teaches us "These are the words"—"neither less nor more"—meaning that the words of Hashem were stated here in such a way that Moshe did not need to add to them.

B. However, the matter is still not fully resolved, because the concept of "details" (*dikdukin*) belongs only to a detailed commandment, and in these verses, only a general exhortation is stated: "If you will indeed listen to My voice and keep My covenant." Furthermore, no punishments for failing to keep the Torah are specified here, only words of

## 87

persuasion and reward for keeping the Torah: "And you shall be to Me a treasure, etc., a kingdom of priests, etc." (as Rashi explains that "a kingdom of priests" means "princes").

Therefore, we must say that what Rashi says regarding "and tell the children of Israel"—"explain the punishments and the details to the men"—means that Moshe had to specify and explain them even though they were not specified in the words of Hashem here. This is puzzling: how does this fit with Rashi on "These are the words"—"neither less nor more"—which implies that Moshe must not add anything to the words of Hashem (not even as "his own words," as mentioned above)?

Some commentators divide the verses: "To the women he said, 'You have seen what I did to Egypt and I carried you on eagles' wings, etc.,' and all of this is a soft language until 'for all the earth is Mine.' And afterwards he turned his face to the men and said to them, 'And you shall be to Me a kingdom of priests,' meaning like priests serving before Hashem all day, 'and a holy nation,' separated from forbidden relations and from all transgressions." [Based on this, they explain what is meant by "These are the words that you shall speak to the children of Israel," that "these last words, which are 'And you shall be to Me a kingdom of priests and a holy nation,' you shall speak to the children of Israel (the men)."]

But this interpretation is difficult to reconcile with Rashi (the plain meaning of the text). Apart from the fact that according to the plain meaning, "These are the words that you shall speak to the children of Israel" refers to all of Israel, both men and women [and according to Rashi, the intent of the verse "These are the words, etc." is to warn "neither less nor more"—not that these last words are only for the men. Furthermore, according to their interpretation, the warning of "neither less nor more" applies only to the men!], there is also the fact that according to Rashi, the meaning of "kingdom of priests" here is not "servants" but "princes" (as mentioned above). Thus, Rashi's statement, "explain the punishments and the details to the men" (as mentioned above), is still not resolved, for there are no punishments and details in these verses. Since Moshe was warned "neither less nor more," how could he have explained the punishments and the details?

C. It is also a matter of inquiry: After the aforementioned verses, it is related that "Moshe came and called for the elders of the people and placed before them all these words which Hashem commanded him." Seemingly, this is puzzling: Moshe was commanded "Thus shall you say to the house of Jacob and tell the children of Israel," and as Rashi explains, he had to speak to the women first in a soft language, and afterwards to the men "words as hard as sinews." If so, how was the commandment of Hashem fulfilled by calling the elders of Israel and placing the words of Hashem before them?

In the Mechilta there, it says: "This teaches that Moshe showed honor to the elders." Simply put, the intent of the Mechilta is to resolve this difficulty [as explained by the commentators, that according to the Mechilta, the intent is not that Moshe delivered the words of Hashem to them, but that he spoke to the women and to the men in the presence of the elders in order to show them honor. This is the precision of the verse's language, "and he placed before them all the words, etc.," and it does not say that he spoke to them].

But Rashi is silent and explains nothing,

## 88

and it is obvious that one cannot say Rashi relies on the student of Scripture to understand all this on his own. Therefore, we must say that according to Rashi's perspective, the difficulty does not exist from the outset.

D. Regarding the gravity of the matter, it seems to me (even though it is somewhat forced) that "Thus shall you say to the house of Jacob and tell the children of Israel" does not refer to the verses that follow, but is a general instruction to Moshe Rabbeinu that pertains to the entire Torah. That is, the beginning of Hashem's words to Moshe upon his first ascent to the mountain to receive the Torah was a general instruction for all time: that the manner of delivering the words of Torah to Bnei Yisrael is that to women one speaks in a soft language, and to men, "words as hard as sinews."

Now, regarding the entire Torah, there is no need for the warning of "neither less nor more," for surely Moshe explained and taught the words of Hashem to Bnei Yisrael with clarification, etc. In this very matter, there is a difference between men and women: for women, one should not emphasize the punishments and the details, but speak in a soft language; for men, one should explain (even if they were not specified in the words of Hashem) the punishments and the details.

Afterwards, the Holy One, Blessed be He, said the first words that must be delivered to Bnei Yisrael—"You have seen, etc. And now, if you will listen, etc. And you shall be to Me, etc."—and at the end, He added, "These are the words that you shall speak to the children of Israel," to warn that even though regarding the Torah in general Moshe must add explanation and interpretation, and sometimes there is a need to shorten (as mentioned above), here in this case—these three verses—he must deliver the words in a manner of "neither less nor more."

[It may be said that this is also the meaning of the conclusion, "that you shall speak to the children of Israel," which refers to all of Israel, to emphasize that in these words there is no difference between women and men.]

E. One can bring proof that this is Rashi's intent in interpreting these verses:

In the Mechilta on "Thus shall you say," it says: "'Thus'—in the Holy Tongue; 'Thus'—in this order; 'Thus'—in this manner; 'Thus'—that you shall neither subtract nor add." [And at the end of the subject on "These are the words," it explains: "that you shall neither subtract nor add," "'that you shall speak to the children of Israel'—in this order."]

Rashi changes the language of the Mechilta, for on "Thus shall you say" he explains "in this language and in this order," meaning: (1) Rashi did not write "in the Holy Tongue" but "in this language"; (2) He omitted "in this manner... that you shall neither subtract nor add."

Now, regarding the reason for Rashi's omission of "that you shall neither subtract nor add," seemingly the reason is that this matter is learned from the end of the subject, "These are the words," and why do I need both? Therefore, he explains that "Thus shall you say" teaches only "in this language and in this order," and "These are the words" teaches "neither less nor more." But it still requires explanation, for the learning from "Thus shall you say" is from the fact that the word "Thus" (*koh*) indicates precision. If so, how can one say that the precision of "Thus shall you say" does not include the matter of "neither less nor more," such that the verse needed to add "These are the words" once more?

And according to the above (Section D), it works out simply.

## 89

For since "Thus shall you say to the house of Jacob and tell the children of Israel" refers to the entire Torah, there is no room to say that Moshe cannot add explanation and interpretation; on the contrary, that is Moshe's role, to explain the Torah to Bnei Yisrael [and simply put, he would explain the words of Hashem also in other languages (if there was a need for it), not necessarily in the Holy Tongue]—and therefore Rashi explains that the intent of the precision of "Thus shall you say" is regarding the matter explicitly stated in the verse here: the order of delivering the words of Torah to women and men, which must be: (1) "In this language" (a soft language for women and words as hard as sinews for men), (2) "In this order"—women before men.

But afterwards, in the verse "These are the words," which refers to these three verses, the redundancy of the verse teaches us that these words are different from the entire Torah, in that Moshe must deliver these words as they are, "neither less nor more."

(From the sichos of Shabbos Parshas Yisro and Shabbos Parshas Mishpatim 5741)

## Sicha 2

A. The Ramban writes in his commentary on the Torah that "the Ten Commandments consist of five regarding the honor of the Creator and five for the benefit of man. For 'Honor your father and your mother' is [included in] the honor of Hashem, because it is for the honor of the Creator that He commanded us to honor the father who participates in the creation, and thus five remain for the needs and benefit of man." It is understood from his words that the mitzvah of honoring one's father and mother is also included among the matters between man and Hashem. This is why its place in the Ten Commandments is within the first five, all of which are matters between man and Hashem, because this—"the honor of Hashem"—is the primary aspect, unlike the honor of the father and mother themselves.

In the Sefer HaChinuch, in the roots of the mitzvah of honoring one's father and mother, it is written: "It is fitting for a person to recognize and repay kindness to one who has done him a favor, and not to be a base, ungrateful, and unappreciative person, for this is a trait that is utterly evil and repulsive before Hashem and man. He should take to heart that his father and mother are the cause of his existence in the world, and therefore it is truly fitting for him to accord them all the honor and benefit he can, for they brought him into the world and also labored for him with many hardships in his childhood. When he accepts this trait into his soul, he will ascend from it to recognize the goodness of Hashem, blessed be He, who is his cause and the cause of all his ancestors back to Adam HaRishon, who brought him into the air of the world, provided his needs all his days, established him in his proper form and the perfection of his limbs, and placed within him a knowing and intellectual soul—for without the soul that Hashem graced him with, he would be like a horse or a mule that has no understanding. He should contemplate how much he is obligated to be careful in His service, may He be blessed."

It should be noted that according to the Chinuch, it is explained (only) that there is a connection between honoring parents and the honor of Hashem, because through establishing the recognition of the kindness of his father who begot him, he will ascend from it to recognize the kindness of Hashem, blessed be He; however,

From the language of the Ramban, it perhaps implies that the honor of the father itself is primarily the honor of the Ribono shel Olam, "for it is for the honor of the Creator that He commanded us to honor the father who participates in the creation," for which reason the mitzvah of honoring father and mother is in its essence not from the "five for man," but from the first five commandments which speak of "the honor of the Creator."

B. The explanation for this is as follows:
In the Chinuch, the language is that "the father and mother are the cause of his existence in the world... they also labored for him with many hardships in his childhood," and in the Ibn Ezra: "The child does not come into the world except through his parents, and they did him kindness, nurtured him, raised him, gave him to drink, fed him, and clothed him." But the Ramban emphasizes that the father "participates in his creation," and nothing more. Simply put, its source is from our Sages: "There are three partners in a person... when a person honors his father and his mother, the Holy One, blessed be He, says: I consider it as if I dwelt among them and they honored Me."

It can be said that the fact that the matter of honoring father and mother is itself "the honor of Hashem" is only because he "participates (with Hashem) in his creation." The fact that parents do everything for the child—"they did him kindness, nurtured him, etc."—does not fall under the category of "partnership" with Hashem. For even though the truth is that He, may He be blessed, "sustains the entire world," nevertheless, since these actions are matters of nature that a person does (seemingly) through his own power, this is not called "partnership" with the work of Hashem. This is not the case with the creation of the child, for it is not in the power of man alone to bring a child into the world, but only through the addition of the manifest "partnership" of Hashem, who "places within him a spirit and a soul, etc."

Perhaps it can be said that even the part of the father and mother (the body) depends in a manifest way on Hashem, and as is known, the power of procreation in created beings is not in them from themselves, but is the power of the Ein Sof and the boundless nature of Hashem. For created beings, by their own nature, are defined by measure and limit, and it is not possible for them to possess, from themselves, the power of procreation, which is a boundless power—to beget until Ein Sof (and the very act of procreation is a matter of innovation, like *yesh me'ayin*). It is only from the power of the Ein Sof of Hashem that He established in the human body through the power that performs wonders. And this is simply the case with animals as well (and see below, section E).

[It should be noted that there are many people (men and women) who are healthy and complete in the ultimate sense of health, to the point that there is no deficiency at all in their bodily limbs, and yet they do not beget children, because the matter of procreation is not merely a natural matter, but depends on the drawing down of the power of the Ein Sof to enable procreation, just as it is written: "Male and female He created them, and Hashem blessed them, and Hashem said to them, 'Be fruitful and multiply, etc.'" For even when they are complete in the nature of their bodies such that they could beget, they still require a blessing to be fruitful and multiply, and this blessing is the power of the Ein Sof that is drawn into the person so that he can beget.]

Since the entire matter of the creation of a child is impossible except through the manifest partnership of Hashem, therefore, when one honors the father because of this aspect—that he "participates in his creation"—this is also "the honor of Hashem."

This is also the point of the explanation in the words of the Ramban that honoring father and mother is "the honor of Hashem," for his words imply (as mentioned above in section A) that in its essence it is a matter of "between man and Hashem" (which is why it appears in the first five commandments).

C. This will be understood further by prefacing the words of the Gemara: "At the time when the Holy One, blessed be He, said 'I am' and 'You shall have no other gods,' the nations of the world said: 'He is demanding for His own honor.' Once He said 'Honor your father and your mother,' they returned and admitted to the first commandments" (and Rashi explains: "How much more so is a person obligated in His honor, for He is also a partner in his creation like his father and mother, and his life and death are handed over to Him").

In explaining the words of the Gemara, the Chida writes based on what the Rambam writes at the beginning of the Laws of Idolatry: "In the days of Enosh, people made a great error, and the counsel of the wise men of that generation became foolish... And this was their error: they said, 'Since Hashem created these stars and spheres to lead the world, and placed them on high, and apportioned honor to them, and they are servants that serve before Him, it is fitting to praise them, glorify them, and apportion honor to them, etc.' They began to build temples to the stars and offer sacrifices to them, etc., and to bow down before them, and this is the root of idolatry." And similarly in our case, "The nations of the world thought as the Rambam says, that Hashem apportioned honor to the stars and spheres and placed them on high, and this is a deed He did, and from it they learned to honor them and bow down to them. Afterwards, when they heard 'I am' and 'You shall have no other gods,' they said, 'He is demanding for His own honor,' that He became jealous, Heaven forbid, and does not want them to honor anyone else, and the speech contradicts the deed. But when they heard 'Honor your father and your mother,' and He equated their honor to His own honor, as it were, from this they knew that He did not become jealous, Heaven forbid, for He commanded to honor parents with the intensity of honor and equated the honor of parents to His own honor, and it is forced to conclude that He gave the constellations and stars, etc., on high to illuminate the lower world, but not to serve them and bow down to them. They returned and admitted to the commandments precisely, for even though it is speech, it does not contradict the deed, Heaven forbid."

And regarding the explanation above, the Admur the Tzemach Tzedek also writes, and he adds there an explanation of the reason

E. Regarding the power of procreation, our Rebbe the Alter Rebbe explains that since it is a power of the Ein Sof (as mentioned above in section B), therefore it exists only in souls and not in angels, for only in the souls of Israel is there the illumination of the Ein Sof, blessed be He (as they are "a part of Hashem from above, literally," and to the extent that "Israel and the Holy One, blessed be He, are all one"), and therefore they possess the power of the Ein Sof that is in procreation, unlike angels, etc. And the fact that we find the power of procreation also in other created beings, and even in animals, is because "they are nourished by vegetation that grew from the earth," for in the earth "there is also the power of procreation" from the power of the Ein Sof that is in the dust of the earth (for "their beginning is wedged in their end," specifically).

[And perhaps it can be said (regarding the procreative power that is in the nations of the world), based on what is explained in other places, that there are many matters in the human body whose reason is so that it should be in accordance with their soul, which is a part of Hashem from above, literally (like the structure of the body where the head is on top, which is because "you are man," see there: *adamah*—to be like the Supernal One). And even so, we find these matters also in the bodies of the nations of the world, in whom there is no holy soul. It is explained that this is so that the matter of *bechirah* (choice) can exist in Israel, for the matter of choice is only possible between two equal things, and therefore there must be an equality between the bodies of Israel and the bodies of the nations of the world, for only then is the matter of choice in Israel specifically relevant. And similarly, it can be said regarding the power of procreation that exists in the nations of the world.]

There is a fundamental difference between the power of procreation in Israel and this power in other created beings:
In other created beings that do not possess a holy soul, the power of procreation—which is a power of the Ein Sof that is above creation—is something additional to their very existence. For even though Hashem established this power in the nature of their bodies, it is like an encompassing power that is above their existence. This is not the case with Israel, who, by virtue of the greatness of their soul, possess the illumination of the Ein Sof; this power is in a manner that is unified with them, and it is a part of their very existence.

It can be said that this is the depth of the Ramban's intention that "Honor your father and your mother is the honor of Hashem, for it is for the honor of the Creator that He commanded us to honor the father who participates in the creation." For one can interpret his intention that the creation of the child (through the power of procreation) is only through the power of the Ein Sof that is in the parents, which is found in them only because Hashem established a power of the Ein Sof in the nature of man. If so, this is not the honor of the father and mother from themselves, but only the honor of Hashem, the power of the Ein Sof that is in them.

This is also the necessity that, by virtue of this aspect in honoring parents—that they participate with Hashem in his creation—this is primarily a mitzvah between man and Hashem ("the honor of Hashem"), and not a matter of between man and his fellow: If the honor obligated by the creation of the child were for the existence of the father and mother from themselves, because they are masters of choice and through their choice and will they brought him into the air of the world, then this would be in contradiction to the true faith in the unity of Hashem, that there is no other authority besides Hashem in the leadership of creation. Rather, the matter of honor here is only by virtue of the power of the Ein Sof of Hashem that comes and is drawn in a manner of Ein Sof and boundlessness in the procreation that occurs through the parents. Since the power of the Ein Sof illuminates and is unified with them, therefore, it is relevant to say that the honor accorded to the father and mother (by virtue of the power of the Ein Sof that is in them, through which he was created) is "the honor of Hashem," the category of "between man and Hashem."

F. From all of the above, it is found that in the honor of father and mother there are two categories and aspects:
1) A rational mitzvah, which is a matter between man and his fellow (whether from the aspect of *hakaras hatov*—recognizing the good—for everything his father and mother bestowed upon him, his upbringing and education, or the category of repaying a debt, as is found in the Yerushalmi).
2) A category of between man and Hashem, for since the father "participates in his creation," the honor of parents is "the honor of Hashem."

It can be said that this is the very reason why the mitzvah of honoring parents was stated in the Ten Commandments in the first five, which are matters between man and Hashem, because the primary innovation of the mitzvah of honoring parents is only in Israel, and it is this matter of honoring the father because he "participates in his creation." This is not the case with the nations of the world, where only the first aspect of honoring parents is relevant (a rational mitzvah between man and his fellow).

For with the *Bnei Noach*, since the power of procreation—which is the power of the Ein Sof that is above creation—is something additional to their very existence, therefore the part of the parents in the creation of the child and the part of Hashem are like two separate things. Consequently, the honor accorded to them by virtue of the fact that they chose to be "emissaries" through whom Hashem creates this child is not a detail of "the honor of Hashem," and it is also only a category of between man and his fellow.

Only in Israel is it relevant to say that the matter of honoring parents by virtue of this aspect—that they participate in his creation—is primarily a category of between man and Hashem, since the power of the Ein Sof that creates the child is found in the father and mother themselves. If so, that which one honors them is itself "the honor of Hashem."

## Sicha 3

### 96

**A.** At the end of our parsha, the verse relates that after Matan Torah, Hashem said to Moshe: "Thus shall you say to the Bnei Yisrael: You have seen that I have spoken to you from the heavens. You shall not make with Me gods of silver, and gods of gold you shall not make for yourselves."

The Ramban writes in explanation of these words: "After you have seen with your own eyes that I spoke to you from the heavens, and that I am the Master in heaven and on earth, do not associate with Me gods of silver and gods of gold, for you have no need for any other help alongside Me." He explains the content of the two warnings in the verse—"You shall not make with Me gods of silver" and "gods of gold you shall not make for yourselves"—by stating that "He warned against believing in them, and then warned against the act of making them alone, similar to the matter of 'You shall not set up for yourselves a carved image or a pillar'".

Commentators have already raised difficulties with the Ramban, as it is forced to say that the intent here is to warn against believing in another god (since it was already stated in the Ten Commandments, "You shall have no other gods"). They write a different explanation: that here the intent is "to command them that, along with the belief in His Divinity, they should not place intermediaries in their prayers between themselves and their God, nor should they make forms to draw down the influence of the stars to ensure the success of their acquisitions. For they have no need for them, since Hashem is close to them whenever they call upon Him, and He will hear and answer them—not through an angel, nor through a star, nor through any other intermediary that would petition on their behalf... He brings proof from the chosen assembly, where they heard the Ten Commandments from Hashem Yisbarach without any intermediary between Him and them. Therefore, 'You shall not make with Me'—meaning, to be intermediaries between Him and them—'gods of silver and gods of gold,' which are the talismans that the ancients would make to draw down the spirituality of the stars for the success of worldly matters. He said 'You shall not make with Me,' and returned to say 'you shall not make for yourselves,' to indicate that it is inappropriate from both His perspective and theirs: from His perspective, because 'I have spoken to you from the heavens' without an intermediary ; and from their perspective, because they are under His providence and guidance, and not under the governance of the celestial systems."

This requires explanation: seemingly, there is no need to warn them against making intermediaries immediately after Matan Torah, as this matter is also in the category of avodah zarah, as the Rambam elaborates in *Hilchos Avodah Zarah*. At Matan Torah, all of Israel saw "that Hashem is the G-d, there is nothing else besides Him—and they saw that He is One", meaning that there is no other existence besides Him. How, then, could it enter their minds to make an intermediary between themselves and their God? 

It seems we can explain that the command "You shall not make with Me" is not (merely) a specific warning (against avodah zarah, or *shituf*, or intermediaries, and the like), but rather it gives

## 97

expression to the general concept of Matan Torah, as follows.

**B.** The explanation for this:
The first verse stated in the Torah after Matan Torah is : "And all the people saw the thunder and the flames and the sound of the shofar... and the people saw and they trembled and stood from afar." At Matan Torah, a complete *bitul* was effected in Israel [and furthermore, at the time of Matan Torah, *bitul* was effected in all of creation, as our Sages say : "When the Holy One, blessed be He, gave the Torah, no bird chirped, no fowl flew, no ox lowed, the *ofanim* did not fly, the *serafim* did not say 'Kadosh, Kadosh,' the sea did not stir, the creatures did not speak; rather, the world was silent and hushed, and the voice went forth, 'I am Hashem your G-d...' When the Holy One, blessed be He, spoke at Mount Sinai, He silenced the entire world so that the creatures would know that there is nothing outside of Him." This means that Matan Torah effected *bitul* not only in Israel, but in all of creation].

The meaning of this is that at Matan Torah there were two aspects: 1) That Israel received the Torah and the *mitzvos*; 2) The *bitul* that was effected in Israel (and in all of creation). Both come from the two aspects that were present at Matan Torah: 1) The thunder and lightning, etc., and the general revelation of the *Shechinah* and the entire *Merkavah*, which effected *bitul* in Israel (and in all of creation); 2) The word of Hashem in the Ten Commandments, which is the giving of the Torah and its *mitzvos* to Israel.

That is to say: At Matan Torah there was a Divine revelation that caused a recognition and feeling in all created beings (and especially in Israel) that there is no independent existence at all, and they only exist from the truth of His Being. In addition to this, there was the matter of the utterance of the Ten Commandments and the giving of the Torah to Israel. According to the simple meaning, these are two different matters.

This is the innovation in the speech after Matan Torah—"You have seen that I have spoken to you from the heavens, you shall not make with Me"—that these two matters are not two separate things ; rather, the content of Matan Torah is the *bitul* of the person's existence, so that he will feel and recognize that "You shall not make with Me," that there is no existence "with Me."

**C.** The explanation of this:
Regarding the nature of the feeling of *bitul* in a person, there are generally two modes : 1) That the person humbles and lowers himself. In this, it is possible that his submission is only in one detail or several, or that he humbles and nullifies himself completely, like the *bitul* of a servant before his master, who humbles and nullifies himself completely toward his master to listen to his voice in everything he commands. 2) Through a most lofty revelation, the *bitul* occurs automatically, similar to the *bitul* of a simple person before a great sage, which is not a case where the simple person nullifies and humbles himself, but rather his existence is nullified automatically before the greatness of the sage.

The advantage of the second mode over the first is twofold: (a) In the first mode, the *bitul* is from the side of the one being nullified, and it is understood that (b) even after he nullifies himself, even when he is nullified in all his details, he still remains an existence, like a servant nullified before his master. This is not the case in the second mode: (a) Since the *bitul* is not from his side—that he humbles himself—but rather through the revelation, he is nullified automatically; and therefore (b) no existence remains here at all. That is to say, it is not "that he has some existence for himself, only that he is nullified," but rather "that he is not in existence at all."

This is the innovation that occurred in the giving of the Torah to Israel:
The ultimate level of the recognition and feeling of the *bitul* of existence that created beings could reach before Matan Torah is the level of *bitul* of the *Merkavah*, where the *Merkavah* is completely nullified to the Rider, yet there is still an existence of a "Merkavah." Therefore, even though "the Patriarchs are the *Merkavah*" —"that all their limbs were entirely holy and separated from the matters of this world, and they were made a *Merkavah* only for the Higher Will alone all their days" —the definition of a "*Merkavah*" is that it is nullified to the Rider; there are two things here: the Rider and the *Merkavah* that is nullified to Him.

The innovation of Matan Torah is that the *bitul* is not merely a result of the Bnei Yisrael humbling themselves before Hashem, but rather it is through and from the side of the revelation of Hashem. Since the Torah and the Holy One, blessed be He, are one, therefore, when "you have seen that I have spoken to you from the heavens," there is no place for any existence besides Him, not even an existence that is nullified to Him.

**D.** It can be said that this is the content of the teaching of the Alter Rebbe brought by the Rebbe Rayatz, that the first negative commandment after the receiving of the Torah is "You shall not make with Me," etc., and the first positive commandment is "An altar of earth you shall make for Me, and you shall sacrifice upon it your burnt offerings and your peace offerings." He continues that the reason the negative commandment here precedes the positive commandment is because the "absence" (*he'eder*) precedes the "being" (*yesh*), and decay precedes growth; "and according to the measure of the greatness of the decay, so is the measure of the growth in sowing and planting."

That is to say: the content of the first negative commandment is the matter of "absence" (which precedes "being")—the matter of *bitul*. In this, the general content of Matan Torah is expressed: that the purpose of Torah is to bring a person to the state of "You shall not make with Me"—that his feeling of *bitul* is in the manner of "absence," where there is no existence at all, not even in the manner of "with Me"—something secondary and nullified to Hashem. That is to say, not (merely) the *bitul* hinted at in the verse "there is nothing else besides Him" —that besides Him there is no existence, but with Him there is an existence—but rather as it is said "there is nothing else", that there is no existence at all (not even an existence of "else" and secondary).

It can be said that the content of this explanation is also hinted at in the *Midrash Mechilta* (and brought in Rashi) on the verse "You shall not make with Me"—"You shall not make... the likeness of My ministers who minister before Me on high, not the likeness of angels, nor the likeness of *ofanim*, nor the likeness of *keruvim*". In general, this refers to the Higher *Merkavah*. This means that the *avodah* of Bnei Yisrael

## 99

from now on is not "like the likeness of ministers," the *bitul* in the manner of "ministers who minister before Me," the Higher *Merkavah*, but rather higher than that: *bitul* in the ultimate sense, "there is nothing else."

**E.** Now, a semblance of the aforementioned distinction exists even now (after Matan Torah) in the *avodah* of man. This is the difference between the *bitul* when a person fulfills the *mitzvos* of Hashem, and the *bitul* in the study of Torah, as the Alter Rebbe explains : that a person who fulfills *mitzvos* is "like a servant who fulfills the commands of the King and does His words," but in the study of Torah, he is in the "category of a king," as the saying goes, "Who are the kings? The Rabbis". For the words of Torah that he brings forth from his mouth are truly the word of Hashem, as it is written : "And I have placed My words in your mouth"—My words literally, for "the word of Hashem is *halachah*", it is the very thing revealed in him and it is what speaks from his throat.

This means that even though for the study of Torah to be as it should be—and especially in such a manner, that it is truly the word of Hashem—it requires the person to humble himself, nevertheless, the *avodah* of the person in this is only a preparation and a vessel. Through "and let my soul be as dust to all," a "opening of my heart in Your Torah" is made. But the *bitul* in the manner of "and I have placed My words in your mouth" is drawn down from the side of the Torah itself.

This is why the aforementioned matter of negation and *bitul* of existence is stated in the language of a command and a *mitzvah*—"You have seen that I have spoken to you from the heavens... You shall not make with Me"—to teach that the *bitul* is not a separate thing that comes and branches off from the revelation of Matan Torah, but rather it is connected with the Ten Commandments, the Torah that was given at Matan Torah.

**F.** According to this, we can understand why the Alter Rebbe explains that the primary *bitul* is what is written, "You shall not make with Me," and not "An altar of earth you shall make for Me, and you shall sacrifice upon it your burnt offerings and your peace offerings," as many Torah commentators explain that "An altar of earth you shall make for Me" points to submission. For according to their interpretation, the *bitul* is the submission and *bitul* from the side of the person's action, and this matter is a preface and preparation for Torah. Without this, it is opposed to Hashem and His Torah, as our Sages say, "I and he cannot dwell together", "You will not find [the Divine Presence] in the arrogant," etc. (and as they explain, this is the continuation of the verse before it: "You have seen that I have spoken to you from the heavens; for Hashem is high, yet He sees the lowly". "And even though My *Shechinah* is in the heavens, nevertheless, from My dwelling place I have spoken to you, and this is a proof that Hashem has no desire for the haughty. Therefore, 'You shall not make with Me gods of silver,' etc., and this is a command regarding the altar, that he should not make it of silver and gold, but rather 'an altar of earth you shall make for Me,' which points to the level of humility").

But according to the Alter Rebbe's interpretation, the *bitul* is from the side of the Torah, which effects *bitul* in the ultimate sense, the definition of "absence." Therefore, he interprets it in the language of "You shall not make with Me," which emphasizes that there is no existence at all (not even in the manner of "with Me," the "with" being secondary).

**G.** Now, regarding the interpretation of "An altar of earth you shall make for Me," the Alter Rebbe also explains it more deeply, that it is not merely a command regarding submission and humility in order to be worthy of receiving a command and the like, but rather it is deeper, coming as a result of the person's *bitul*

## 100

in the manner of "You shall not make with Me," the definition of "absence." As he puts it: "Decay precedes growth, and according to the measure of the greatness of the decay, so is the measure of the growth in sowing and planting."

Explanation of the matter:
After the explanation of "You shall not make with Me," the Alter Rebbe continues (there): "And the positive commandment is: 'An altar of earth you shall make for Me, and you shall sacrifice upon it your burnt offerings and your peace offerings.' 'An altar of earth you shall make': An altar of *bitul* you shall make for yourself. 'Your burnt offerings'—your ascents; 'your peace offerings'—your wholeness; you shall sacrifice upon this altar to become dust and ashes literally."

Seemingly, this is in contradiction to what was explained earlier: from his language at the beginning, that the negative commandment is "You shall not make with Me" and the positive commandment is "and you shall sacrifice upon it," and that the negative commandment here is before the positive commandment because "absence precedes being and decay precedes growth, and according to the measure of the greatness of the decay, so is the measure of the growth in sowing and planting"—it is understood that the content of the matter of "An altar of earth you shall make for Me and you shall sacrifice upon it, etc." is the growth, meaning the existence and the ascent of the person, not the *bitul* ("absence"). And then he continues in the explanation of the verse that its entire content is the absence and *bitul* of the person, "to make himself an altar of *bitul* to sacrifice your ascents, etc."?

Rather, they are the very same things: When the *bitul* is from the side of the person—submission and humility that the person has effected in himself, or even *bitul* in the manner of "a servant who fulfills the commands of the King and does His words"—then even when he comes to the level of "an altar of earth," a matter of ultimate *bitul*, there is still a matter of ascent and wholeness. And as is simple, when the servant fulfills the commands of the King, it is felt that this is an ascent and wholeness that was effected by him (and similar to "a servant of a king is a king").

This is what is explained there: that the matter of "an altar of earth" is in continuation of the negative commandment of "You shall not make with Me," meaning the *bitul* that is effected from the side of the Torah, in which there is no existence at all besides the Torah that he is studying (not even the existence of the one who is nullified). And in the verse "An altar of earth you shall make for Me, and you shall sacrifice upon it your burnt offerings and your peace offerings," it is explained how this *bitul* (of "You shall not make with Me") penetrates also into the existence of the person, that even as he is in his existence from his own side, not at the time of Torah study, his *bitul* should be in the aforementioned manner, that there is no existence at all.

And to this he continues: "An altar of *bitul* you shall make of yourself; your burnt offerings, your ascents; your peace offerings, your wholeness; you shall sacrifice upon the altar to become dust and ashes literally."
