The meaning is that his *bitul* is not in a manner where he is an independent existence that humbles and nullifies himself to Hashem—for then the *bitul* is an *avodah* and an ascent of his—but rather the *bitul* is in the manner of a sacrifice, where there is no existence here and no "ascent" of *bitul*. And this is specifically when the ascents and wholeness—meaning true ascents and wholeness that are called such according to the true Torah (for the ultimate ascent and wholeness in relation to Hashem and His Torah is in the *bitul* itself)—he sacrifices them upon the altar. Meaning, it is not felt by him that he has acted and done some lofty thing and has ascended to Hashem and become whole in his *avodah*, but rather he is entirely an altar of *bitul*, without any feeling of elevation and wholeness.

**H.** Now, in the teaching of the Alter Rebbe there, he continues: "An altar of *bitul* is not yet anything; rather, one must sacrifice upon it the ascents and wholeness."

Seemingly, this is only a repetition of words. But it can be said that in this, he adds a higher level.


## תרומה

## Sicha 1

### 123

A. We are taught in the Sifri : "Wherever it is said 'to Me' (li), it is enduring for this world and for the world to come... Regarding the Mikdash, it says, 'And they shall make a Mikdash for Me (li).'" Similarly, it is stated in the Midrash : "Wherever it is said 'to Me' (li), it does not depart ever, neither in this world nor in the world to come... Regarding the Mikdash, 'And they shall make a Mikdash for Me (li)'."

His Holiness, the Tzemach Tzedek, explains that the concept of "it does not depart in this world" regarding the mitzvah of "And they shall make a Mikdash for Me" is "because it is written, 'And I have been to them a small Mikdash (mikdash me'at)...' And our Sages of blessed memory said... 'These are the synagogues.' If so, it does not depart even in this world."

Now, several explanations have been offered regarding the aforementioned statement of our Sages. Among them: 1) "Regarding the Mikdash and the Altar, it shall be seen that even though they were removed during the building of Solomon, they are nonetheless enduring forever in the place where they were hidden, and there will be no loss in them." 2) Even though the Mikdash was destroyed, the holiness of the place was not nullified, as the Rambam writes that "the first holiness which Solomon sanctified—he sanctified the Courtyard and Jerusalem for their time and sanctified them for the time to come... for the holiness of the Mikdash and Jerusalem is because of the Shechinah, and the Shechinah is not nullified. And it says, 'And I will make your sanctuaries desolate,' and the Sages said, 'Even though they are desolate, they stand in their holiness.'"

It requires clarification: Why was the Tzemach Tzedek not satisfied with the aforementioned explanations? Seemingly, they are superior, for according to them, the statement refers to the Mikdash itself, whereas according to the Tzemach Tzedek’s explanation, the concept of "it does not depart" is because

### 124

there are synagogues in this time that are a "small Mikdash," a semblance and likeness of the Mikdash, and not the Mikdash itself.

Regarding the first explanation mentioned above, it can be said that it is a sufficient explanation for the language of the Sifri, "enduring for this world and for the world to come," but it does not resolve the language of the Midrash, "it does not depart ever, neither in this world nor in the world to come," for the Mikdash did depart from its place and was hidden. But why was he not satisfied with the explanation that it is due to the holiness of the Shechinah and that "the Shechinah is not nullified," which is a matter pertaining to the site of the Mikdash itself, in this world, as the Rambam writes : "Therefore, we offer all the sacrifices even though there is no built house, and we eat the most holy offerings in the entire Courtyard even though it is desolate and not surrounded by a partition"? Thus, it is found that the holiness of the Mikdash, "And they shall make a Mikdash for Me," does not depart (even) in this world.

B. It can be said based on what the Rambam writes in Sefer HaMitzvos (in interpreting the words of the Sifri mentioned above): "'And they said, wherever it is said 'to Me' (li), it is enduring forever,' meaning that it is a constant matter and not a mitzvah for a temporary time, but it is appropriate and obligatory for all generations." That is to say, the interpretation of "enduring forever" is not (only) that the object (cheftza) is enduring forever, but that the obligation of the person (gavra) is enduring forever; for wherever it is said "to Me" (li), it is a constant commandment for the generations.

So too in our case, regarding the commandment of "And they shall make a Mikdash for Me," since it is said "to Me" (li), it is a commandment for the generations. And this is explicitly stated in the Rambam at the beginning of Hilchos Beis HaBechirah, where he learns the commandment "to build the Beis HaMikdash" from this verse, even though it was said regarding the Mishkan in the desert (but since it says "to Me" (li) regarding it, it must be that it refers not only to the Mishkan in the desert but to all Mikdashes). As written in the Kesef Mishneh, according to the Rambam, "this verse is a general rule for every place, whether for the Mishkan in the desert, or for Shiloh, Nov, and Givon, or for the eternal House." It is found, therefore, that it is a constant mitzvah in every place and at every time (and it is not among the mitzvos that are dependent on the Land, for the Mishkan in the desert is also included in this single commandment).

With this, the language of the Sifri and the Midrash mentioned above is also precise, for they do not cite the end of the verse, "and I will dwell among them," but only the beginning of the verse, "And they shall make a Mikdash for Me," because the primary emphasis here is on the action of man, "And they shall make a Mikdash for Me."

It can be said that for this reason, the Tzemach Tzedek was not satisfied with explaining that "it does not depart ever" is because of the resting of the Shechinah in the place of the Mikdash—for this points only to the eternal existence of the object of the Mikdash (the resting of the Shechinah within it), and not to the eternal existence of the obligation of the person to "make a Mikdash for Me."

C. Now, it has been explained elsewhere that since, according to the Rambam, "And they shall make a Mikdash for Me" is a constant commandment in every place and at every time, it must be that even in this time there is an obligation to engage in the building of the Mikdash. It has been explained that it can be said that this is the basis of the obligation to study the Torah of the House and the form of the House, etc., for since it is impossible to build the House in actuality, this part of the engagement in the building of the House remains—the study of the form of the House.

This is the intent of the Midrash that the Holy One, blessed be He, said to Ezekiel, "Tell them (the Jewish people) to occupy themselves with reading the form of the House in the Torah, and for the reward of their reading... I account it to them as if they were occupied with the building of the House." And furthermore, that the Holy One, blessed be He, said, "And because My children are in exile, shall the building of My House be nullified (entirely)? Tell them..." That is, through the engagement in the form of the House, the "building of My House" is not nullified—and this is because the study of the laws and the form of the House is within the category of the mitzvah of building the Beis HaMikdash, which is a constant commandment. In this time, when it is impossible to build the House literally, the fulfillment of this commandment is through the study of the form of the House.

According to this, the statement of the Sifri that the commandment "And they shall make a Mikdash for Me" is "enduring for this world and for the world to come" is understood simply, because even in this time there is a fulfillment of this commandment through the engagement in the laws and the form of the House.

The fact that the Tzemach Tzedek did not bring this matter is because, as mentioned above, he is referring to the words of the Midrash, where the language is "it does not depart ever, neither in this world, etc." From this language, it is understood that no change or "departure" occurred in the manner of fulfilling the commandment, and even "in this world." Whereas according to the aforementioned explanation, a change and "departure" has occurred in the manner of fulfilling the commandment of "And they shall make a Mikdash for Me," in that we do not fulfill it physically (in a physical building) but only through the matter of study, a spiritual matter.

Therefore, the Tzemach Tzedek explains that the concept of "it does not depart ever, neither in this world" is due to the matter of "a small Mikdash... these are the synagogues," according to which there is a fulfillment of "And they shall make a Mikdash for Me" literally, in a physical sense.

It requires explanation: How is it possible to say that through the matter of synagogues, which are only a "small Mikdash," there is no "departure" or change in the manner of fulfilling the commandment "And they shall make a Mikdash for Me"?

D. This can be explained based on the words of the Zohar : "'And they shall make a Mikdash for Me and I will dwell among them'—'And they shall make a Mikdash' is stated generally, for every synagogue in the world is called a Mikdash." In the Ra'aya Meheimna, he counts this in the enumeration of the mitzvos: "Commandment (19): To build a Mikdash ('a synagogue') below, in the likeness of the Mikdash above, as it is written, 'The place for Your dwelling, which You have made, O Hashem'—that it is necessary to build a Mikdash below and to pray within it every day to serve the Holy One, blessed be He, for prayer is called service (avodah)."

In the later authorities, they have elaborated to bring proof from the words of the Zohar there that the positive commandment of "And they shall make a Mikdash for Me" includes also the mitzvah of building a synagogue, and that this is implied by the words of the Maharik. He writes:

"It is appropriate to compare the donation for a synagogue to the donation for the work of the Mishkan, for in every place our Sages of blessed memory compared the synagogue to the Beis HaMikdash, as we say in the last chapter of Megillah: 'And I have been to them a small Mikdash... these are the synagogues and houses of study.' And from this, the Mordechai writes there that it is forbidden to dismantle anything from a synagogue, just as it is taught in the Sifri : 'From where do we know that one who dismantles a stone from the Heichal, etc., is in violation of a negative commandment, etc.?' And you learn from this that we derive the synagogue from the Mikdash. And it is taught in the Sifri : 'Mikdash—your sanctuaries (mikdasheichem), to include synagogues.' And we also say in the first chapter of Shabbos : 'Every city whose roofs are higher than the synagogue will eventually be destroyed, as it is written, 'to raise the house of our G-d and to restore its ruins.'' And even though that verse, 'to raise the house of our G-d,' is written in Ezra regarding the building of the Beis HaMikdash, we learn from it that one should be compared to the other. And so also wrote Rabbeinu Meir, and the Mordechai brings it... And perhaps it is similar to the upper chambers of the Heichal which were sanctified... 'Our small Mikdash'—we must conduct ourselves in it with a measure of holiness and a semblance of the holiness of the Heichal. This is to say that even according to the one who says that the upper chambers of the Courtyard were not sanctified, nevertheless, our synagogue is more stringent, for it can be compared to the upper chambers of the Heichal which were sanctified."

[It can be said that this is the halachah in the Rambam, for in addition to his ruling that "synagogues and houses of study that were destroyed stand in their holiness, as it is said, 'And I will make your sanctuaries desolate,' even though they are desolate they stand in their holiness" (like what he writes regarding the holiness of the Mikdash), in the enumeration of the mitzvos at the beginning of Sefer HaYad, in the negative commandments, it is explicitly stated: "Not to destroy the Beis HaMikdash or synagogues or houses of study, etc., as it is said, 'You shall destroy them—you shall not do so to Hashem your G-d.'" Thus, he counts the negative commandment (in his enumeration of mitzvos) not to destroy synagogues and houses of study in the same breath and from the same verse from which we derive regarding the Beis HaMikdash].

## 131

### Sicha 2

A. Regarding the manner in which the curtains were placed upon the Mishkan, Rashi brings two opinions in his commentary on the Torah. He writes:
"The width of the curtains was forty cubits when joined... Thirty of these were for the roof of the Mishkan’s interior along its length, one cubit for the thickness of the tops of the planks in the west, and one cubit to cover the thickness of the pillars in the east... Eight cubits remained, hanging over the back of the Mishkan in the west, and two lower cubits were exposed. This is what I found in the Braisa of Maseches Middos. But in Maseches Shabbos, the curtains do not cover the eastern pillars, and nine cubits hang behind the Mishkan. And the verse in this Parsha supports us: 'And you shall place the Paroches beneath the clasps.' If it were as the Braisa says, the Paroches would be pulled one cubit from the clasps toward the west."

The explanation is as follows: The Paroches was at the end of the ten cubits of the Kodesh HaKodashim (which is on the western side of the Mishkan). According to the Braisa, since only eight cubits (out of the twenty cubits of the first set of curtains) covered the eight cubits of the planks on the western side, plus one cubit for the thickness of the tops of the western planks, it turns out that the end of the set of curtains (where the clasps are) is at the end of the eleventh cubit (from the west) of the Mishkan’s roof [8 + 1 + 11 = 20 cubits]. Thus, the Paroches (which is at the end of the tenth cubit) is not beneath the clasps.

However, according to the opinion of the Shas (in Maseches Shabbos), where nine cubits of the western planks were covered, the Paroches was exactly beneath the clasps (9 + 1 for the thickness of the plank tops + 10 = 20).

This requires study: It has been explained many times that Rashi, in his commentary on the Torah, does not set out to bring all the opinions of Chazal on a given matter, but only to explain the *peshuto shel mikra* (the plain meaning of the verse), in the language of Rashi: "I have come only to explain the plain meaning of the verse and the Aggadah that resolves the words of the verse."

We see this in our very case: In Maseches Shabbos, a dispute is brought regarding the thickness of the planks. According to Rabbi Yehuda, they were a cubit thick only at the bottom, but "at the top they tapered until they were a fingerbreadth," whereas according to Rabbi Nechemiah, "just as they were a cubit thick at the bottom, so too were they a cubit thick at the top." This results in a significant difference regarding how the curtains were placed on the Mishkan, both in their length and their width (as explained in detail in the sugya there). For according to Rabbi Nechemiah, the curtains must cover the cubit of the thickness of the plank tops, whereas according to Rabbi Yehuda (where the tops of the planks are only a fingerbreadth), one cubit of the curtains remained that covered another cubit (approximately) of the planks (on the north, south, and west). This opinion is not mentioned

## 132

at all in Rashi, because it is not according to the *peshat* at all.

Based on this, we must examine: Since Rashi wrote explicitly that the verse in our Parsha supports the opinion of the Shas, why did he bring the opinion of the Braisa of Maseches Middos at all?
[Even though the commentators explain the verse "And you shall place the Paroches beneath the clasps" to fit the Braisa as well—nevertheless, Rashi is seemingly clear that the verse supports the Shas. We must say that he holds the explanations of the commentators are forced, and if so, why did he need to bring the opinion of the Braisa at all?]

B. This is even more difficult: Further on, regarding the goat-hair curtains, which were wider than the lower curtains by one curtain (four cubits), it is stated: "And the excess that remains in the tent curtains, half of the excess curtain shall hang over the back of the Mishkan." Rashi comments: "And the excess... the tent curtains are the upper ones, of goat hair... and they were wider than the lower ones by half a curtain toward the west, for the half of the eleventh curtain was folded toward the front of the tent [i.e., toward the entrance on the east side]. Two cubits of the width of the half-curtain remained, exceeding the width of the lower ones. It shall hang over the back of the Mishkan, to cover the two cubits that were exposed on the planks."

Here, Rashi assumes the opinion of the Braisa, that two cubits were exposed. For according to the opinion of the Shas (in Maseches Shabbos), where "nine cubits [of the lower curtains] hang behind the Mishkan," only one cubit of the planks was exposed.

[Also in Rashi at the beginning of that passage, on the verse "And you shall fold the sixth curtain toward the front of the tent," he assumes the opinion of the Braisa of Maseches Middos, writing: "And you shall fold the sixth curtain, the one that is in excess in these upper ones more than the lower ones. Toward the front of the tent, half its width was hanging and folded over the screen in the east opposite the entrance, like a modest bride who is covered with a veil over her face."

This explanation (that two cubits were hanging over the screen in the east) seemingly applies only to the opinion of the Braisa. For according to the opinion of the Shas, that "the [lower] curtains do not cover the eastern pillars," there is no "half-width" of the sixth curtain [two cubits] hanging "over the screen in the east opposite the entrance," since one cubit of it covers the tops of the eastern pillars (which were not covered by the lower curtains), and only one cubit remains that was hanging over the screen].

## 133

It is extremely difficult: After Rashi wrote explicitly that the verse supports the opinion of the Shas, how could he subsequently assume the opinion of the Braisa of Maseches Middos?!

The Re'em writes: "Rashi, of blessed memory, took the Braisa as the primary opinion and continued the explanation of the verses according to the Braisa, even in a place where the verse stands against it." Seemingly, his words are puzzling, for how can it be said that Rashi, who came only to explain the *peshuto shel mikra*, would take the Braisa as the primary opinion "even in a place where the verse stands against it"?

C. Therefore, it appears to me—in accordance with the rules of Rashi that have been explained several times—that the fact that he brought the opinion of the Braisa of Maseches Middos, and even before the opinion of the Shas, is because it is the primary explanation of the *peshuto shel mikra*. On the contrary, simple logic mandates only this explanation (for several reasons, as will be seen in section D). The reason that compelled him to bring the explanation of the Shas is only because of the support from the verse "And you shall place the Paroches beneath the clasps."

This is the very reason Rashi brought support for the Shas's explanation, even though in most places his custom is to explain the verse without proofs and evidence for his interpretation (or for the difficulty he is resolving). For in this case, there is seemingly no room for this interpretation, and therefore he must explain why he brought it.

It can be said that this is also the precision of Rashi's language: "And the verse in this Parsha supports us"—he did not use the word "proves" (and the like), as he does (in many places) when his intent is to bring a (complete) proof for his interpretation—because these verses do not contain a proof to decide in favor of this interpretation. The reasons for the first interpretation are much stronger than this support [especially since it can be said that the Tanna of the Braisa holds that it is appropriate to say the Paroches is "beneath the clasps" even if it is pulled one cubit away from them, for relative to a length of thirty cubits (of the Mishkan), even when it is located beneath the clasps at a distance of one cubit, it is called "beneath them"]. Rather, it is only Rashi's reason for bringing it as a second interpretation.

D. Explanation of the matter:
Regarding the manner of placing the curtains over the width of

## 134

the Mishkan, Rashi writes here: "The curtains were twenty-eight cubits long... The width of the Mishkan from north to south is ten cubits... He places the curtains, their length across the width of the Mishkan; ten middle cubits for the roof of the Mishkan's interior, and one cubit here and one cubit there for the thickness of the tops of the planks, whose thickness is one cubit. Sixteen cubits remained, eight for the north and eight for the south, covering the height of the planks, whose height is ten. It turns out that the two lower cubits were exposed."

Based on this, it is understood why Rashi brought (following this) the opinion of the Braisa of Maseches Middos, that the width of the curtains was also placed on the Mishkan in a way that they covered eight cubits of the planks on the western side "and the two lower cubits were exposed"—according to which it turns out that the lower edge of the curtains was equal on all three sides. This is not so according to the opinion of the Shas, where on the north and south sides the curtains covered eight cubits, while on the western side they covered nine cubits.

[And even though the goat-hair curtains did not cover the planks on all sides equally—for on the north and south sides, where the excess of the goat-hair curtains over the lower curtains was "one cubit here and one cubit there", they covered only one cubit ("of the two cubits that remained exposed"), "and the lower cubit of the plank that the curtain does not cover is the cubit embedded in the socket of the base, for the bases were a cubit high"; whereas on the western side, where there were two cubits of excess (half the width of the extra curtain), "to cover the two cubits that were exposed on the planks" (as above in section B)—one must distinguish: since the Kodesh HaKodashim is on the western side, they needed to cover the entire wall on that side, out of respect for the Shechinah (but this is not a reason to change the measurement of the lower curtains, that they should cover nine cubits on the western side, since in any case they did not cover all ten cubits of the planks, and furthermore, they were not visible to the eye—if so, why should they not be equal to the curtains on the other two sides?).]

E. Based on this, that Rashi took the interpretation of the Braisa as the first and primary interpretation, we must also explain the reason why, in his commentary on the verse "And the excess that remains... half of the excess curtain shall hang over the back of the Mishkan" (regarding the goat-hair curtains), Rashi took only the interpretation of the Braisa (as above in section B)—for from these verses there is another strong proof for the interpretation of the Braisa:

According to the opinion of the Shas, that the lower curtains covered nine cubits of the planks on the western side, it turns out that "half of the excess curtain" (which is two cubits) that must be "over the back of the Mishkan" (to cover what remained exposed from the lower curtains), one cubit of it would cover the tenth cubit of the planks, and the second cubit—would be lying on the ground!

[And as is explicit in Maseches Shabbos there, that this curtain was "dragging on the ground", as he brings there: "The Tanna of the school of Rabbi Yishmael says: To what is the Mishkan compared? To a woman who walks in the marketplace and her skirts (the hems of her garments, Rashi) follow behind her."]

## 135

But according to *peshat*, it is difficult to say that the curtains of the Mishkan were dragging on the ground, as this is the opposite of honor. Especially since Rashi brings as the reason that the excess of the goat-hair curtains (on the north and south sides) "should hang over the sides of the Mishkan on this side and that side to cover it"—"The Torah taught proper conduct, that a person should be sparing of the beautiful". Even though this refers (simply) to the lower curtains, that because they were beautiful he was sparing of them to cover them (with the goat-hair curtains), nevertheless, it is understood that that same behavior of "proper conduct" also mandates being careful with the goat-hair curtains (which are also precious) and not to degrade them by having them drag on the ground!

Therefore, Rashi explains the verse only according to the interpretation of the Braisa, that "half of the excess curtain" covered "the two cubits that were exposed on the planks," according to which it was not dragging on the ground at all.

F. One can explain the opinion of the Shas—that the goat-hair curtains were dragging on the ground—according to the inner dimension of the matters:
The inner difference between the ten lower curtains and the eleven goat-hair curtains is:
Kedushah is divided into ten levels (the ten Sefiros), whereas in the *sitra achra* there are eleven crowns of impurity. Similarly in our case: the lower curtains hint at the light that surrounds all ten levels of Kedushah, and therefore they were ten in number; whereas the upper curtains hint at a higher surrounding light, which has the power to clarify even the *sitra achra*, "so that the *kelipah* will be a guard for the fruit and the *kelipah* will not be an opponent to the fruit", and therefore they are eleven in number.

This is also the hint in the fact that the goat-hair curtains were dragging on the ground, in accordance with their inner matter, to clarify even the earthliness, so that it too will be permeated with the light of Kedushah.

(From a Sicha of Shabbos Parshas Terumah, 5741)

## Sicha 3

## 136

A. The Rambam writes in *Hilchos Beis HaBechirah* : In the First Temple, there was a wall separating the *Kodesh* (Holy) from the *Kodesh HaKodashim* (Holy of Holies), one *amah* in thickness. When they built the Second Temple, they were in doubt as to whether the thickness of the wall belonged to the measurement of the *Kodesh* or to the measurement of the *Kodesh HaKodashim*. Therefore, they made the *Kodesh HaKodashim* twenty full *amos* in thickness, and they made the *Kodesh* forty full *amos*, and they left an extra *amah* between the *Kodesh* and the *Kodesh HaKodashim*. They did not build a wall in the Second Temple, but rather made two *parochos* (curtains), one on the side of the *Kodesh HaKodashim* and one on the side of the *Kodesh*, with an *amah* between them, corresponding to the thickness of the wall that existed in the First Temple.

The *Kesef Mishneh* writes that the source for the Rambam’s statement, "When they built the Second Temple, they were in doubt," is the *Yerushalmi, Kilayim* : "The *amah* of the *tarksin*—is it from within or from without?... Rabbi Yosi said: From that which is written, 'And forty *amos*...'—this implies from within... And it is written, 'And he made the house of the *Kodesh HaKodashim*...'—this implies from without."

Now, the debate regarding the definition of the "*amah tarksin*" exists also in the *Bavli* (in *Maseches Yoma*): "Regarding the *amah tarksin*, the Sages did not determine whether it was considered as being within or as being without. Ravina challenged this: What is the reason? If you say it is because it is written, 'And the house... sixty *amos* long...' and it is written, 'And forty *amos* was the house, that is, the *Heichal* before it,' and it is written, 'And before the *Dvir* was twenty *amos* long and twenty *amos* wide and twenty *amos* high,' and we do not know if the *amah tarksin* is included in those twenty or in those forty—perhaps it is included in neither the twenty nor the forty, and the text is counting the interior space and not counting the walls? You can know this, for wherever it counts the walls, it counts them explicitly, as we learned: The *Heichal* was one hundred by one hundred, with a height of one hundred... the wall of the *Heichal* was six, its length forty *amos*, and the *amah tarksin*, and twenty *amos* for the house of the *Kodesh HaKodashim*... Rather, the doubt is regarding its holiness: is it as within or as without?".

However, according to the simple reading of the *Bavli* (at the conclusion of the discussion), they knew the location of the *amah tarksin* (it was not included in the twenty nor in the forty, but was an *amah* in its own right), and they were only in doubt as to whether its holiness was as within (*Kodesh HaKodashim*) or as without (*Heichal*). Since they could not build a wall in the Second Temple (as will be explained below), they "made two *parochos*," with an *amah* of air between them, corresponding to the place of the *amah tarksin*. According to this, they did not add to the measurement of the structure, but rather made two *parochos* in place of the wall, the *amah tarksin*.

But the Rambam (following the *Kesef Mishneh*) holds like the *Yerushalmi*, that "they were in doubt whether the thickness of the wall was from the measurement of the *Kodesh* or from the measurement of the *Kodesh HaKodashim*; therefore, they made... an extra *amah* between the *Kodesh* and the *Kodesh HaKodashim*".

## 137

Seemingly, this requires study : Since they were not building a wall in the Second Temple anyway, why was it necessary to leave an extra *amah* corresponding to the thickness of the wall? Would it not have sufficed to make a *paroches* between the forty of the *Kodesh* and the twenty of the *Kodesh HaKodashim*?

Furthermore, it requires study: how are they permitted to add to the measurements of the structure, when "everything is in writing, from the hand of Hashem, giving understanding" ?

The difference here is in two things: In the First Temple, the *Heichal* and the *Kodesh HaKodashim* together were sixty *amos*, and the *amah tarksin* was either at the beginning of the *Kodesh HaKodashim* (as the first *amah*) or at the end of the *Heichal* (as the fortieth *amah*), and there was no separation at all between the *Kodesh* and the *Kodesh HaKodashim*. This is not the case in the Second Temple: (a) The *Kodesh HaKodashim* was twenty full *amos* and the *Kodesh* was forty full *amos*, and between them (a separation) was an extra *amah*. (b) There was a change in the area of the floor of the *Heichal* (or *Kodesh HaKodashim*) from what it was in the First Temple.

One cannot say that according to the Rambam there is no contradiction here to "everything is in writing," for if so, it is not understood why they did not build a wall in the Second Temple and instead only made two *parochos*. For in the *Gemara*, the reason they did not make an *amah tarksin* is that "it stands at thirty, it does not stand higher" (a wall of one *amah* thickness cannot stand at a height of more than thirty *amos*). Rashi (in the discussion in *Yoma*) explains, "And to add to its thickness is impossible, for it is written, 'Everything is in writing, from the hand of Hashem, giving understanding.'" In another place, Rashi explains, "And to widen it they could not, because they would be shortening the *Heichal* or the *Kodesh HaKodashim*". But if you say that according to the Rambam, the fact that they add between the *Kodesh* and *Kodesh HaKodashim* does not relate to "everything is in writing," why did they not add the measurement of an "extra *amah*" to build a wall and "add to its thickness"?

B. It is also necessary to understand:
In *Tosafos*, they ask why it was necessary to make two *parochos*, and why they did not make one *paroches* that was an *amah* thick. They answer, "Because the Sages understood the explanation of the verse, 'And the *paroches* shall divide for you between the *Kodesh*...' to mean that the outer side of the *paroches* should be *Kodesh*, and from the beginning of its thickness and inward should all be *Kodesh HaKodashim*. Therefore, one cannot make a single *paroches* an *amah* thick and place it in the location of the *amah tarksin*, for perhaps its holiness is as 'without,' and it would turn out that from the beginning of its thickness and inward, it is not all *Kodesh HaKodashim*... But now that they made two *parochos*, it is fine: one was placed at the beginning of the place of the *amah tarksin*, and the second at the end of the *amah tarksin* in the *Kodesh HaKodashim*, at the end of the twenty-cubit space of the *Kodesh HaKodashim* towards the outside, attached to the place of the end of the *amah tarksin*. For if the place of the *amah tarksin* is 'as within,' then from the thickness of the outer *paroches* and inward, it is all *Kodesh HaKodashim*; and if the place of the *amah tarksin* is 'as without,' then from the thickness of the second *paroches* and inward, it is *Kodesh HaKodashim*."

However, from the words of the Rambam, "They left an extra *amah* between the *Kodesh*... and between them an *amah* corresponding to the thickness of the wall that was in the first," it is proven that he does not hold like *Tosafos* that the separation must be in the *paroches* itself. For the Rambam specifies that the two *parochos* were "one on the side of the *Kodesh HaKodashim* and one on the side of the *Kodesh*, and between them an *amah*...". This is not the case according to the opinion of *Tosafos* (that the *paroches* itself separates), where the first *paroches* was "at the beginning of the place of the *amah tarksin*" in such a way that there was not a full *amah* between them.

Accordingly, it is not understood: why, according to the Rambam, did they not make one *paroches* an *amah* thick ?

C. Now, in the continuation of his words, the Rambam writes, "But in the First Temple there was only one *paroches* alone, as it is said, 'And the *paroches* shall divide for you...'" The *Kesef Mishneh* there notes: "And what our Master wrote, 'But in the First Temple there was only one *paroches*,' it appears he is calling the *Mishkan* the 'First Temple,' for in the First Temple there was no *paroches*, but a wall."

It has already been asked that the Scripture explicitly states in *Divrei HaYamim*, "And he made the *paroches*," so there was a *paroches* in the First Temple as well. And so it is explicitly stated in the *Gemara* that there was a "curtain of the doors," and the Rambam himself wrote this in his *Commentary on the Mishnah*. They answered that it is obvious that the *Kesef Mishneh* also holds that according to the Rambam there was a *paroches* at the entrance of the *amah tarksin*, as is explicit in the text and the *Gemara*. Rather, here the Rambam is addressing the definition of the *paroches* as a partition, like the *paroches* in the Second Temple, which is learned from the verse (regarding the *Mishkan*), "And the *paroches* shall divide for you..." In contrast, in the First Temple, the *paroches* was not by the law of "And the *paroches* shall divide for you"—because the separation between the *Kodesh* and the *Kodesh HaKodashim* was by means of a built wall and a complete partition—and the *paroches* (at the opening of the *amah tarksin*) was only for the sake of modesty, as is stated in Rashi. Therefore, the *Kesef Mishneh* wrote that the Rambam's intent in what he wrote (following the two *parochos* in the Second Temple), "But in the First Temple there was only one *paroches* alone, as it is said, 'And the *paroches* shall divide for you...'," refers to the *Mishkan*.

But this is still difficult: why did the Rambam need to conclude here that "in the First Temple (meaning the *Mishkan*) there was only one *paroches* alone"?

And even according to the commentators who say the Rambam's intent is simple, that it refers to the First Temple, it is difficult to understand why the Rambam did not mention this *paroches* immediately at the beginning of the *halacha*. It goes without saying for the interpretation that the Rambam's intent in this separating *paroches* is the separating wall, the *amah tarksin* —the Rambam should have begun the *halacha*: "In the First Temple there was a wall separating... one *amah* thick, as it is written, 'And the *paroches* shall divide for you...'," for in the Temple there was a built wall in place of a *paroches*. But even for the interpretation that the Rambam's intent is simple, that there was a *paroches* on the *amah tarksin* due to the law of "And the *paroches* shall divide" (and not only for the sake of modesty), it is difficult to understand why the Rambam did not include both matters together: "In the First Temple there was a wall separating... one *amah* thick, and likewise a *paroches*, as it is written, 'And the *paroches* shall divide for you...'" (and afterwards explain the changes in the Second Temple, that they left an extra *amah* corresponding to the wall and made two *parochos*)—and why did he mention the *paroches* and the verse "And the *paroches* shall divide..." only after he explained the making of the two *parochos* in the Second Temple?

D. The explanation of this can be understood by first explaining the definition of the *amah tarksin* according to the Rambam.
Regarding this *paroches*, we find several expressions in the verse: In the first command it is said, "And the *paroches* shall divide for you between the *Kodesh* and the *Kodesh HaKodashim*." But afterwards, in the command for the erection of the *Mishkan*, it is said, "And you shall place there the Ark of the Testimony, and you shall screen the Ark with the *paroches*." And in the construction it is said, "And he brought the Ark into the *Mishkan* and placed the *paroches* of the screen, and screened over the Ark of the Testimony," and similarly.
This means there are two aspects to the *paroches*: (a) A partition separating between the *Kodesh* and the *Kodesh HaKodashim*; (b) A screen over the Ark.

Based on this, we must examine the law of the *amah tarksin* in the First Temple:
Simply put, since the place of this *amah* was in the place of the *paroches* of the *Mishkan*, and it was only a built wall, it must be that it also contained these two aspects: a partition between the *Kodesh* and the *Kodesh HaKodashim*, and also a screen over the Ark. This is also the meaning of the word "*tarksin*," as Rashi brings from the *Yerushalmi* that it means "a term for inside and outside." In *Tosafos*, it says, "Others explain it is from the expression 'shut the doors' (*torku gali*), that inside of it were closed the Tablets that were given at Sinai". For the interpretation that it is "inside and outside," one can say simply that it is because it is a wall that separates between inside and outside, the *Kodesh HaKodashim* and the *Kodesh*. And the interpretation brought in *Tosafos* is (primarily) because it is a screen over the Ark [the difference is only what is primary: that it is a partition that separates, or that it protects the Ark. Therefore, according to Rashi, who holds that primarily it is a law of a partition, it is called *tarksin* because of 'inside and outside', unlike the interpretation of Rabbeinu Tam and the 'others' in *Tosafos*].

E. It can be said that the Rambam has a different approach to this. That the two aforementioned aspects of the *paroches* (and the *amah tarksin*) are only as a detail and secondary to another part of the *Mishkan* and Temple—a partition serving the *Kodesh HaKodashim*, or protecting the Ark. But the Rambam holds that the *amah tarksin* in the Temple is not like the *paroches* in the *Mishkan*. For the *paroches*, as its name implies, serves only as a partition that separates, or as a screen over the Ark. But the "*amah tarksin*" also has a place of its own; it is an object in its own right, which also served the two aforementioned aspects (separation between the *Kodesh* and *Kodesh HaKodashim*, and also a screen over the Ark), but that is only its function and not its essential definition.

This is also implied by the fact that this place is called in the Scripture by a name of its own, "*Dvir*". This is proven even more from the words of the *Zohar*, which explains the verse in *Melachim*, "And it was when Shlomo finished building the house of Hashem and the house of the King,"—"That is the Temple, such as the courtyards, the chambers, the *Ulam* (Hall), and the *Dvir*." The *Zohar* lists the "*Dvir*" as one of the parts of the Temple. However one explains the reason the *Zohar* listed these four things specifically, it is in any case proven that the "*Dvir*" is not just a detail and secondary to another part of the Temple, but is a matter in its own right (like the courtyards, the chambers, and the *Ulam*).

[Especially according to what is explained in the writings of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, on the *Zohar*, that the four things listed in the *Zohar* there are "corresponding to the four letters of the Name *Havayah*." If so, certainly the *Dvir* has a name and importance of its own.]

Regarding the reason for this, one can say—based on the words of the Rambam at the beginning of *Hilchos Beis HaBechirah*, "It is a positive commandment to build a house for Hashem... 'And they shall make for Me a sanctuary,' and it has already been explained in the Torah the *Mishkan* that Moshe Rabbeinu made, which was for a time...". It has been explained elsewhere that the Rambam's intent in this emphasis (that the *Mishkan* was only "for a time," and seemingly, what does he teach us?)—is that he defines the difference between the *Mishkan* that Moshe made and the Temple. The *Mishkan* was only "for a time," whereas the Temple is a house and a permanent structure ("a house for all generations"). This is the foundation for the differences between the details of the construction of the *Mishkan* and the details of the construction of the Temple. Namely: many of the changes between the construction of the Temple and the *Mishkan* come from the difference between a temporary structure ("for a time") and a permanent structure.

Similarly in our case: In the *Mishkan*, which was only a temporary structure, "for a time," the thing that separated between the *Kodesh* and the *Kodesh HaKodashim* (and protected the Ark) was not part of the body of the structure, but only a "*paroches*," a name which indicates that it is not something permanent in the body of the structure, but a partition for the function of separation (or protection of the Ark). This is not the case in the Temple, which was a permanent structure : "There was a wall separating... one *amah* thick," for a "wall" is not a temporary thing, but a part of the essence of the structure. And this place has a name of its own—"*Dvir*."

In other words:
The *Kodesh HaKodashim* is distinct in its holiness from all parts of the house, for it is the most exalted holiness in all the sanctified places. Its holiness is not just another level in holiness, similar to the other holinesses where each one is sanctified from the level below it, but its holiness is separated from all the holinesses below it. Therefore, it is not enough that the separation (which is permanent in the Temple) be only by means of a partition of a wall (like the other separations between two places), but there must be a place in its own right which is like a "mediator" between the *Kodesh* and the *Kodesh HaKodashim*, and this place is what separates between them.

[One can say that primarily this relates to the person: in order to enter the *Kodesh HaKodashim*, the most sanctified place, "where no one enters except the High Priest on Yom Kippur at the time of the service" (even from the place of the *Kodesh*, where all the priests enter all year), it is appropriate and necessary that there be a cessation and a delay before the entry].

F. Based on this, one can say that the Rambam holds that even in the Second Temple, where they did not build a wall, they were obligated to leave this *amah* of the place of the "*Dvir*," for this is one of the definitions of the "Temple," that there must be a place in its own right—a width of an *amah*—between the *Kodesh* and the *Kodesh HaKodashim*. This matter is precise in the language of the Rambam in this *halacha*, where he wrote, "And they did not build a wall in the Second Temple, but rather made two *parochos*, one on the side of the *Kodesh HaKodashim* and one on the side of the *Kodesh*, and between them an *amah* corresponding to the thickness of the wall that was in the first." For seemingly, the length of the Rambam's language, "corresponding to the thickness of the wall that was in the first," is superfluous—rather, in this he comes to teach us that the definition of the Temple requires leaving this *amah*.

This is also the reason they did not make one *paroches* an *amah* thick, but (in the words of the Rambam) "made two *parochos*, one on the side of the *Kodesh HaKodashim* and one on the side of the *Kodesh*, and between them an *amah* corresponding to the thickness of the wall"—because the purpose of making the *parochos* was not (only) for the sake of making a partition and separation between the *Kodesh* and the *Kodesh HaKodashim*, but for the sake of making the place of the "*Dvir*". This is why they made two *parochos* on the two sides, for thereby this place of an *amah* was designated in its own right [similar to the explanation of Rashi, "And they made two *parochos*—to enclose between them the air of the place of the partition"]. This is not the case if there were only one *paroches* (even if it were an *amah* thick); since, in addition to this, a "*paroches*" is a temporary thing as mentioned above, then by means of one *paroches* an *amah* thick, this place would not be designated as a separate place in its own right (as mentioned in section E).

This is also the reason for the difference between the opinion of the Rambam and the opinion of *Tosafos* regarding the place of these *parochos* (as mentioned in section B)—for according to *Tosafos*, the *parochos* were "one placed at the beginning of the place of the *amah tarksin* and the second at the end of the *amah tarksin*," which implies that there was not a full *amah* between them. Whereas the Rambam specifies "and between them an *amah*" —because according to the opinion of *Tosafos*, the purpose of these *parochos* is from the law of "And the *paroches* shall divide for you," and the separation must be in the *paroches* itself (as *Tosafos* wrote). Therefore, the outer *paroches* must be specifically in the place of this *amah*, so that "from the outer side of the *paroches* should be *Kodesh*, and from the beginning of its thickness and inward should all be *Kodesh HaKodashim*." This is not the case according to the opinion of the Rambam, where the main purpose of making these *parochos* was to designate the place of the *amah tarksin*, and if so, on the contrary, there must be "between them an *amah* corresponding to the thickness of the wall that was in the first."

With this, it is also resolved why they did not build a wider wall in the Second Temple, even though they left an extra *amah* (more than in the First Temple), because even the Rambam holds that they were not permitted to add simply to the width of the structure. Rather, regarding this *amah*, since they were obligated to make a permanent place of an *amah* between the *Kodesh* and the *Kodesh HaKodashim*, and they could not make it from the measurement of the *Kodesh* or from the measurement of the *Kodesh HaKodashim* (as in the First Temple) because "they were in doubt whether the thickness of the wall was from the measurement of the *Kodesh* or from the measurement of the *Kodesh HaKodashim*" [and if they were to make it in the *Kodesh* or in the *Kodesh HaKodashim*, it is possible they would be detracting from the place of the *Kodesh* or from the place of the *Kodesh HaKodashim*]—they were compelled by the law of the "*amah tarksin*" to leave an "extra *amah*."

Based on all this, one can also explain the conclusion of the Rambam's words, "But in the First Temple [meaning the *Mishkan*, according to the *Kesef Mishneh*] there was only one *paroches* alone, as it is said, 'And the *paroches* shall divide for you...'," in which his intent is to emphasize that the making of the two *parochos* in the Second Temple is (not the making of a separation between the *Kodesh* and the *Kodesh HaKodashim*, but) the designation of the place of the *amah tarksin*. Therefore, they were required to make two *parochos* in the Second Temple specifically, unlike "in the First Temple—the *Mishkan*—(where) there was only one *paroches* alone [since the definition of the *Mishkan* *paroches* is like] that which is said, 'And the *paroches* shall divide for you...'"

G. One can also explain the conclusion of the Rambam's language mentioned above according to the commentators who say the Rambam's intent is to the First Temple itself (and not to the *Mishkan*), because even in the First Temple they made a *paroches* (in addition to the wall of the *amah tarksin*)—for seemingly, the Rambam should have written this at the beginning of the *halacha* (as mentioned in section C).

To begin with, regarding this law that was innovated in the Temple relative to the *Mishkan*, that a *paroches* alone is not sufficient but there must be a wall of the *amah tarksin* (as mentioned in section E)—this can be explained in two ways:
(a) That no new obligation was innovated in the Temple, but rather the difference between the definition of the *Mishkan* and the definition of the Temple (for a time or permanent for generations) requires that in the Temple there should not be a *paroches* (whose existence and nature is a temporary thing) but there must be a permanent place (as mentioned in section E).
(b) That this is a new obligation, from the aspect of "Everything is in writing, from the hand of Hashem, giving understanding," which does not relate to the making of the *paroches* and the law of "And the *paroches* shall divide for you," similar to what is written in *Tosafos*, "That in the First Temple... the *amah tarksin* was made according to the Divine command, as it is written, 'Everything is in writing'" (and for this reason, the *amah tarksin* does not have the definition of "And the *paroches* shall divide for you," for the *paroches* itself must separate between the *Kodesh* and the *Kodesh HaKodashim*).

According to the second way, one can say that this is the Rambam's intent in this *halacha*, that at the beginning of the *halacha* he wrote only, "In the First Temple there was a wall separating between the *Kodesh* and the *Kodesh HaKodashim*," and did not bring the verse "And the *paroches* shall divide for you" regarding it. And only at the conclusion of the *halacha*, after the lengthiness regarding the making of the two *parochos* in the Second Temple, he concludes, "But in the First Temple there was only one *paroches* alone, as it is said, 'And the *paroches* shall divide for you...'," in which he emphasizes that the obligation of making the *amah tarksin* (and also the leaving of an extra *amah* corresponding to the thickness of the wall in the Second Temple) is not from the law of "And the *paroches* shall divide for you," for that is an obligation in its own right, and that was the purpose of making the *paroches* (at the opening of the wall) in the First Temple (from the aspect of the obligation of "And the *paroches* shall divide...").

H. Now, the words of the *Zohar* were brought above (section E), which listed the four parts of the Temple—"the courtyards, the chambers, the *Ulam*, and the *Dvir*," and the explanation of my father-in-law, the Rebbe, that they are corresponding to the four letters of the Name *Havayah*. One can say that in this note, my father-in-law, the Rebbe, innovates a matter in the *halacha* regarding the approach of the *Zohar*.

To begin with, in the *Zohar* there, he explains the expression of the verse, "the house of Hashem and the house of the King"—"The house of Hashem, that is the Temple, such as the courtyards, the chambers, the *Ulam*, and the *Dvir*... The house of the King, that is the *Kodesh HaKodashim*." From the simple words of the *Zohar*, it is proven, seemingly, that he holds that the holiness of the *Dvir* is not like the holiness of the *Kodesh HaKodashim*, for the *Kodesh HaKodashim* was specified in the verse in its own right, "the house of the King," from which it is understood that this is a matter in its own right, and if so, it is impossible to say that the *Dvir* is from the measurement of the *Kodesh HaKodashim* and its holiness.

[And even though in the *Gemara* it says that the Sages were in doubt regarding the holiness of the *Dvir*, whether it was as within (holiness of *Kodesh HaKodashim*) or as without (holiness of *Heichal*)—we find that according to several interpretations, Rabbi Yosi holds that there was no doubt about this, and if so, there is room to say that the *Zohar* also determined according to the first side.]

Based on this, it would have been possible to resolve also that which the *Zohar* mentioned only the *Ulam* and did not mention the *Heichal* at all—because according to the *Zohar*, the holiness of the *Dvir* is like the holiness of (the outside, meaning) the *Heichal*, and if so, the *Heichal* is also included in the division of the "*Dvir*".

This is what my father-in-law, the Rebbe, innovates, that "four things... corresponding to the four letters of the Name *Havayah*," and from the order of the things it is understood that the *Dvir* is corresponding to the letter *Yud* of the Name *Havayah*—according to which it is understood that the holiness of the *Dvir* is not like the holiness of the *Heichal* but like the holiness of the *Kodesh HaKodashim*.

For regarding the four letters of the Name *Havayah*, it is known that the letter *Yud* (which is corresponding to the attribute of *Chochma*) is separated completely from the other letters of the Name *Havayah*. Just as the difference between *Chochma* and the other *Sefiros*, and in *avodah*, *Chochma* is a matter of absolute *bitul


## תצוה

## Tetzaveh

### Sicha 1

1. "Six of their names on the one stone, and the names of the remaining six on the second stone, according to their generations". Rashi explains "according to their generations" as "in the order they were born: Reuven, Shimon, Levi, Yehudah, Dan, and Naftali on the one, and on the second: Gad, Asher, Yissachar, Zevulun, Yosef, and Binyamin—full, for so it is written in the place of their birth—twenty-five letters on each and every one".

This requires clarification: 
a) Is there any novelty in Rashi’s explanation? Seemingly, this is the simple meaning of the word "according to their generations"—in the order they were born. 
b) Why does Rashi elaborate and detail their order of birth here ? It is already explicitly stated in the Torah (in Parshas Vayeitzei).

Some commentators suggest that Rashi’s intent is to negate the notion that "according to their generations" means the order of the *commencement* of their births by the mothers (whereby all the children of the mother who began giving birth first would precede the others—even if some of them were born later—to the rest of the children). In that case, the order would be: all the children of Leah (Reuven, Shimon, Levi, Yehudah, Yissachar, Zevulun), the children of Bilhah (Dan and Naftali), the children of Zilpah (Gad and Asher), and then the children of Rachel (Yosef and Binyamin). Therefore, Rashi explains "in the order they were born," referring to each of the tribes individually, rather than the order of the *commencement* of their births by the mothers.

However, beyond this, it is still difficult to understand why it was necessary to elaborate and detail all of this here, as mentioned above. Why would it even enter one’s mind to interpret "according to their generations" as the order of the mothers' births rather than the order of their own births?

Furthermore, it is difficult to understand: Why did Rashi need to add "and Binyamin—full, for so it is written in the place of their birth," and similarly the continuation, "twenty-five letters on each and every one" ? Rashi did not set out to establish a practical halachic ruling, but rather to explain the simple meaning of the verse. The simple meaning of the verse only states that six of their names were on the one stone and the remaining six on the second stone, and their order was "according to their generations." It does not state how they were written or how many letters were on each stone.

[Especially since, according to this, Rashi adds another interpretation to "according to their generations." According to the beginning of his words, the meaning of "according to their generations" is "in the order they were born," whereas according to his conclusion, "Binyamin—full, for so it is written in the place of their birth," it turns out that "according to their generations" comes to instruct the *manner* of its writing (i.e., as it is written in the place of their birth)].

2. The explanation for all this: 
Although the simple meaning of "according to their generations" is "in the order they were born," there are still two difficulties in this verse: 
a) The word "according to their generations" was not stated (as would have been appropriate) at the beginning of the matter—"and you shall engrave upon them the names of the children of Israel according to their generations," etc.—but rather at the end of the matter, following "and the names of the remaining six on the second stone," which implies that it refers only to the second stone. 
b) What is the precision of the verse’s language, "and the names of the remaining six"? Is it not self-evident that the second six are "the remaining ones" after the first six?

Because of these two difficulties, there was room, seemingly, to interpret that the verse comes to distinguish between the names engraved on the one stone and the names on the second stone: that on the one stone were engraved the names of the more important tribes, and on the second stone, the names of the tribes who are not as important [for example, that on the second stone were written the names of all the children of the handmaids, or perhaps (also) those whom Yaakov rebuked before his passing, or something similar]. Therefore, the verse calls them "the remaining ones". There would also be a difference in the order of their engraving on the stones: on the one stone, it is according to their level of importance (as understood from the blessing of Yaakov: Yehudah, Yosef, etc.), and on the second stone, where it says "the remaining ones," their order is "according to their generations."

This is what Rashi comes to teach us in his commentary, to the point that he needed to detail their order specifically : "Reuven, Shimon, Levi, Yehudah, Dan, and Naftali on the one, and on the second: Gad, Asher, Yissachar, Zevulun, Yosef, and Binyamin" —to teach that what is written "according to their generations" applies to both stones equally.

However, one must still ask: Since this is so, why is "according to their generations" stated at the end of the verse, after the words "on the second stone," and not at the beginning of the verse (or at the end of the previous verse), in a way that would make it clear that it applies to both? Therefore, Rashi continues and explains that "Binyamin—full, for so it is written in the place of their birth," by which he comes to resolve that what is stated "according to their generations" at the end is to hint at the manner of writing the name of Binyamin (which is written at the [end of the] second stone).

Accordingly, it is also understood why Rashi added "twenty-five letters on each and every one," to negate the initial assumption that on the one stone there is an importance relative to the second, or something similar—rather, there is no difference between them, to the point that they are equal even in the number of letters on each one.

3. Now, the Rambam wrote the order of the tribes on the Shoham stones of the Ephod in a different way: Reuven, Levi, Yissachar, Naftali, Gad, Yosef on one stone; Shimon, Yehudah, Zevulun, Dan, Asher, Binyamin on the second stone. There has already been discussion regarding the Rambam’s position, as it does not align with any opinion in the Gemara, neither the opinion of the Tanna Kamma nor the opinion of Rabbi Chanina ben Gamliel.

Commentators have explained the Rambam’s view: he holds that the meaning of "according to their generations" is the order in which the mothers gave birth to the tribes (Leah, who gave birth first, all her children come one after another, and likewise Bilhah, Zilpah, and Rachel), except that "their arrangement is not on each stone (individually), but on the two stones (as one): Reuven on this stone (the first) and Shimon after him on the other stone (the second), and Levi under Reuven, and Yehudah under Shimon, and so on for all of them." That is to say, since it is written, "six of their names on the one stone and the names of the remaining six on the second stone according to their generations," it implies that the order "according to their generations" is not in the manner of their arrangement one after another on one stone and then one after another on the second stone—for "according to their generations" is only stated after "the second stone". This means that when they are joined together, their reading is from this stone to the second stone (as in the diagram in the Rambam).

It turns out that even the Rambam, according to his method regarding the order of the names, emphasizes the equality between the two stones. Moreover, according to the Rambam, the equality of the two stones is even greater than according to Rashi:
According to Rashi, the "according to their generations"—"in the order they were born"—is fulfilled on each stone individually, and it is only that the "according to their generations" regarding all twelve tribes is between the two stones, in that the six on the second stone come after the six on the first stone. Whereas according to the Rambam, the two stones are as if they were placed side-by-side and are like one stone, and their names are read together. There is no beginning to the "fulfillment" of "according to their generations" on one stone, but only in the combination of the second stone, for after Reuven on the first stone comes Shimon on the second stone, and so on.

Seemingly, this can be explained based on what is explained elsewhere that according to the Rambam, the Choshen and the Ephod together constitute one garment (even though in the count of the priestly garments, they are two garments). [Based on this, it is understood why, regarding the wearing of the Ephod and the Choshen, it is implied from the words of the Rambam that the fastening with the belt of the Ephod was done after he had put on the Choshen, and not like the opinion of the Ramban (and others) that the fastening of the Ephod with the belt was done before putting on the Choshen].

Therefore, just as regarding the Choshen, even though all twelve tribes were written on it individually, each on an individual stone, and it had "four rows of stone as specified in the Torah", the Rambam did not specify in his commentary which of them were in each row, but simply stated, "and he engraves on the stones the names of the tribes according to their generations, and it turns out he writes on the Odem: Reuven, and on the Yashpeh: Binyamin". This is because the fulfillment of "according to their generations" is not in each row individually, but in the names of the tribes on all twelve stones together. Likewise, it is regarding the Shoham stones on the shoulders of the Ephod (by which the attachment of the Choshen and the Ephod is achieved, "so that they cleave to one another and the Choshen does not shift from the Ephod")—that "he engraves on the two stones the names of the tribes, six on this stone and six on this stone, according to their generations" in a way that they are like one stone, and "according to their generations" is fulfilled when they are read "together," immediately at its beginning: Reuven, Shimon, as mentioned above.

However, based on this, it is slightly difficult why, according to the Rambam, "according to their generations" is not "in the order they were born" (as per Rashi) but rather in the order of the mothers' births. Seemingly, "the order of their birth" signifies more their equality and unity, and does not emphasize the division between them, whereas the order of their mothers' births emphasizes more the division between the tribes, as the children of Leah were separate, the children of Bilhah and Zilpah were separate, and the children of Rachel were separate.

4. The explanation for this in the inner dimension of the matters:
The content of the matter of engraving the names of the tribes on the Shoham stones on the shoulders of the Ephod is explained in the verse : "And you shall place the two stones on the shoulders of the Ephod, stones of remembrance for the children of Israel, and Aharon shall carry their names before Hashem on his two shoulders for a remembrance." Rashi explains: "For a remembrance, that the Holy One, Blessed be He, should see the tribes written before Him and remember their righteousness."

Since the matter of the names of the children of Israel is their elevation as a remembrance before Hashem, this is connected and dependent on the unity of the children of Israel together, for then they are worthy to ascend before Hashem, because all the children of Israel are one complete stature. When there is separation and division between one and another, it is not worthy to ascend for favor before Hashem.

However, there are two levels in this:
The division between the tribes (the children of Israel) as they are from the perspective of the order of their birth and as they are from the perspective of the birth of their mother. "In the order they were born" points to their birth as it is from the perspective of the father, in contrast to the order of their birth from the perspective of the mothers.
It is known that the actual existence of the child is from the father's drop, and the division in the specific formation of the limbs is through having been nine months in the mother's womb. It is similar in our matter: from the perspective of the father (in the order they were born), the matter of unity is felt in them as it is from the perspective of their root—"we are all children of one man" —and the order of their birth from the perspective of the mother points to their division.

These are two modes that must exist in the unification of the children of Israel and the tribes, so that they can ascend "before Hashem": The first mode, "according to their generations—in the order they were born," where their unity is felt as it is from the perspective of their root in our father Yaakov; and the second mode, according to the order of their birth from the perspective of the mothers, where the unity is emphasized even in the place of their division.

There is a virtue in each of them:
In the first mode, i.e., the unity from the perspective of the root, this is a more exalted unity, for from the perspective of their root there is no division at all. This is not the case for the unity as it is according to the order of their birth from the perspective of the mothers—it allows room for division.
But on the other hand, for this very reason—that the unity from the perspective of the father is through the feeling of their root—it turns out that only in their root are they united, but in their place they are divided and separated from one another (and as mentioned above, according to Rashi, the "according to their generations," "in the order they were born," is fulfilled on each stone individually).
This is the novelty in the second mode—the Rambam’s method—that it is specifically emphasized that even as they are on two separate stones, they become true unity, as if they were one stone, that they are read together—that even in the place of division they act and continue the unity of the children of Israel.

5. This unity of the tribes of Israel among themselves is connected with their unity with the Holy One, Blessed be He. According to this, it is understood that the two modes mentioned above in the unity of the children of Israel also have their counterparts in the unity of Elokus.
This can be explained based on what is written in the seforim regarding the names of the children of Israel on the shoulders of the Ephod: that the twenty-five letters on each stone correspond to the twenty-five letters of "Shema Yisrael Hashem Elokeinu Hashem Echad," the twenty-five letters of Shacharis and the twenty-five of Maariv. And another interpretation : that what is stated "six of their names on" is the "initial letters of Shema, that they said Shema Yisrael". This means that the twenty-five letters on the right shoulder correspond to "Shema Yisrael Hashem Elokeinu Hashem Echad," and the twenty-five letters on the left shoulder are the six of "Baruch Shem Kevod Malchuto Leolam Vaed" (in each of which there are six words and twenty-five letters). As it is in the Gemara, that the tribes said, "Just as there is only One in your heart, so there is only One in our heart."

The difference between the two explanations: The Shema of Shacharis and Maariv, both of their content is *Yichuda Ila'ah* (the Higher Unification), which is the unification as it is from Above. Whereas "Baruch Shem" points to *Yichuda Tata'ah* (the Lower Unification), which in the service of Hashem is the category of *iskafya* (coercion), where one does not have the desire of the heart for Elokus, but must coerce himself, in the category of the mind ruling over the heart.

This is similar to the two modes mentioned above, for according to the first mode, the difference between the two stones is not so evident, unlike the second mode, as emphasized in the language of the Rambam : "And the stone on which he wrote Reuven on his right shoulder, and the stone on which he wrote Shimon on his left shoulder."

And yet, there is a virtue in this unification of "Just as there is only One in your heart, so there is only One in our heart," that they act and continue the unity even in the place of division, the *alma depiruda* (world of separation), for "just as they are unified Above in One, so too she is unified Below in the mystery of One... to be One in One," which thereby creates a more exalted unity even than how it is from the perspective of *Yichuda Ila'ah* in itself.

This is not the case for the Shema of Shacharis and Maariv; although both are in the category of *Yichuda Ila'ah*, the unity as it is in the root, the Shacharis and Maariv are two levels. This means that as it is continued in the place of division, the unity is not felt as much as it is from the perspective of *Yichuda Ila'ah*.

And through the addition in *ahavas Yisrael*, the unity of all the children of Israel together, in all the modes mentioned above—which is the foundation of the entire Torah, as the Alter Rebbe explains in the Tanya : "For the foundation and root of the entire Torah is to elevate and raise the soul above the body, higher and higher, until the source and root of all worlds, and also to continue the light of the Ein Sof, Blessed be He, into the Knesset Yisrael... into the source of the souls of all Israel, to be One in One specifically, and not when there is separation, Heaven forbid, in the souls, for the Holy One, Blessed be He, does not dwell in a flawed place... (for then it is achieved) 'Bless us, our Father, all of us as one in the light of Your countenance'."

And until the complete and primary blessing in the days of Moshiach, that "a great congregation shall return here", "with our youth and with our elders, with our sons and with our daughters," immediately and truly.

(From a Sicha of Shabbos Parshas Tetzaveh 5735)

## Sicha 2

### 153

A. The Rambam writes at the end of *Hilchos Kilayim*: "Kohanim who wore priestly garments when not performing the service, even in the Mikdash, are liable to lashes because of the *avnet* (belt), which is *kilayim*. They were only permitted to wear it during the service, which is a *mitzvah aseh*, like *tzitzis*." (He writes similarly in *Hilchos Klei HaMikdash*: "The *avnet*, because it is *shaatnez*, is forbidden for a *kohen hedyot* to wear except during the service.") The Raavad disagrees, based on the Gemara which states that "in the Mikdash, it is permitted even when not during the service."

The Raavad further challenges the Rambam’s statement that they are liable only "because of the *avnet*, which is *kilayim*" — "Are we to say that the *choshen* and *eifod* do not contain *kilayim*?" The Radbaz explains that "the *avnet* was embroidered wool and was soft, but the *choshen* and *eifod* were woven and were likely stiff, and thus not *kilayim*." According to this explanation of the Rambam, it emerges that the Rambam and the Raavad disagree on whether the law of *kilayim* applies only to the *avnet* or to other priestly garments (of the *Kohen Gadol*) as well.

It can be said that these two disagreements between the Rambam and the Raavad are interdependent:

The disagreement regarding whether *kilayim* in priestly garments is permitted only during the service or as long as one is in the Mikdash (even when not performing the service) is a disagreement regarding the definition of the permission for *kilayim* in priestly garments:

The Rambam writes explicitly that the permission for *kilayim* in priestly garments is like the permission for *kilayim* in *tzitzis*, which is merely a *dichuy* (or a permission). This means that the prohibition of *kilayim* is pushed aside and permitted in the place of the *mitzvah aseh* of the priestly service, just as it is permitted in the place of the *mitzvah* of *tzitzis*. Therefore, there is no room to permit it except during the time of the service.

In contrast, the Raavad holds that there is a fundamental difference between *kilayim* in *tzitzis* and *kilayim* in priestly garments. Regarding *tzitzis*, there is a *limud* (derivation) to permit *kilayim* (from the juxtaposition of the verses in the Torah, "You shall not wear *shaatnez*... you shall make yourself twisted threads..."). However, regarding priestly garments, there is no such derivation from Scripture to permit *kilayim*; rather, it is because the Torah commanded that the *avnet* be made of wool and linen, and by definition, we know that there is no prohibition involved. This means that from the outset, this is the manner of fulfilling the wearing of priestly garments—that they contain *kilayim*. Therefore, he holds that the permission to wear *kilayim* in priestly garments is not dependent on performing the service; rather, for priestly garments, the prohibition of *kilayim* was never stated to begin with.

Based on this, it can be said that this is also the reason for their disagreement as to whether the law of *kilayim* applied only to the *avnet* or also to the *choshen* and *eifod*:

According to the Raavad, who holds that the law of *kilayim* was not stated for priestly garments to begin with, it is logical to include *kilayim* in the priestly garments (that the *choshen* and *eifod* also contain *kilayim*), to emphasize that this is how the Torah commanded the priestly garments to be made from the start. Conversely, according to the Rambam, who holds that in essence the prohibition of *kilayim* was indeed stated for priestly garments, but was pushed aside (and permitted) in the place of a *mitzvah aseh*, it is logical to minimize the *dichuy* and the permission of the *kilayim* prohibition as much as possible. Therefore, the Rambam holds that regarding the *choshen* and *eifod*, there is no need to permit the prohibition because "they were stiff and are not *kilayim*," and only regarding the *avnet* is there a law of *kilayim* that is permitted during the service.

B. Based on the above regarding the Rambam’s view, perhaps we can explain, on the level of *nigleh*, why only the *avnet* has the law of *kilayim* and none of the other priestly garments (and according to the Rambam, this is also the case for the *Kohen Gadol’s* garments) — for seemingly, what is different about this garment that only regarding it did the Torah command that it be made of *kilayim*?

It can be said regarding this, based on what is found in the Gemara, that a belt during *tefillah* has the aspect of "Prepare to meet your G-d, O Israel" (and therefore the *Rishonim* wrote "that one must gird his loins when he prays," and this is also ruled in the *Tur* and *Shulchan Aruch*).

Based on this, it can be said that this is also the essence of the *avnet* in the priestly garments: its entire purpose is for the sake of "Prepare to meet your G-d, O Israel." The other priestly garments are clothing and garments to cover a person (like the regular function of garments), except that they were made in a special way, "for honor and for glory", to be holy garments for the priestly service [and the garments special to the *Kohen Gadol* each have a special function: on the *eifod* were the *avnei shoham* upon which were engraved the names of the children of Israel; and likewise, in the *choshen* there were twelve stones corresponding to the twelve tribes, and furthermore, the *Urim V’Tumim* were in it; on the hem of the robe were the bells, "and its sound shall be heard when he enters the holy place..."; and the *tzitz*, upon which was engraved "Holy to Hashem", was to atone for the "iniquity of the holy things"].

In contrast, the *avnet’s* main essence and function is the matter of "Prepare to meet your G-d, O Israel" [for the trousers had "strings" to tie them, and the tunic does not need an *avnet* to hold it in place], as is the simple meaning of the *avnet*, which indicates the completion of putting on the garments, and being prepared to perform the service.

And since, according to the Rambam, the main reason for the permission of *kilayim* is only because of the *mitzvah aseh* of the priestly service, therefore, *kilayim* existed only in this garment, whose entire essence is "Prepare to meet your G-d, O Israel," a preparation for the service.

C. And it requires study in the *pnimiyus* (inner dimension) of the matters: ultimately, what is the reason the Torah said that the priestly service must be performed specifically by wearing a garment that contains *kilayim*?

Especially since the very essence of the priesthood depends on the priestly garments, as they said, "When their garments are upon them, their priesthood is upon them." It turns out that the matter of the priesthood is brought about by wearing a garment that contains *kilayim*.
