Based on this, it can be said that even according to the Rambam, the *kilayim* in the priestly garments is not merely a matter of permission, but rather it contains a special content, for the essence and service of the priesthood requires that there be a garment of *kilayim* in it. However, according to the Rambam, this content is relevant (a) only during the service, and (b) only in the *avnet*.

D. This can be understood based on what is explained in *Rabbeinu Bachya* regarding the reason and secret of the prohibition of *shaatnez* (according to *Kabbalah*): "He who establishes offspring below from a species with a different species causes the opposite of peace, for he mixes the supernal forces and nullifies them and uproots them from their appointments... The two who were born in the world first (Cain and Abel) were *kilayim*; this one was from the side of the good spirit and this one was from the side of the evil spirit, and we were commanded to follow the holy spirit and to distance ourselves from the evil which is from the side of the spirit of impurity. Therefore, *kilayim* was forbidden to us, for it is a joining of two extremes which are two opposites... their joining does not turn out well... the garments were forbidden to us... because the first-borns' offering was wool and linen; this one brought wool and this one brought linen. Therefore, the joining of wool and linen together was forbidden to us... He who wears this garment mixes the supernal forces... Israel was warned against this joining, for they are opposites, in order to distance themselves from the spirit of impurity and to be sanctified with the holy spirit."

It can be said that in his lengthy language, he hints at two ways of explaining the prohibition of *kilayim*, that it is "a joining of two extremes which are two opposites... their joining does not turn out well":

1) Mixing the "good spirit" with the "evil spirit" (like Cain and Abel), "and we were commanded to follow the holy spirit and to distance ourselves from the evil which is from the side of the spirit of impurity."

And as the *Admur HaTzemach Tzedek* wrote (in explaining the words of *Rabbeinu Bachya*), "that the essence of the prohibition of *kilayim* is because through this, one joins the spirit of holiness with the spirit of impurity, which are a species with a different species." Based on this, he explains why we find that in holiness there must be "a combination and blending of the *midos* with each other," which is not a matter of *kilayim*, because in holiness "everything is unity... everything is one species, the *bitul* and submission to Him, may He be blessed... The essence of 'a species with a different species' is holiness with the *kelipos*, for holiness is *bitul* to Him, may He be blessed, and the *kelipos* are *yesh* and a separate thing."

2) The very mixing of two types of supernal forces is the opposite of the Will, similar to what *Rabbeinu Bachya* wrote earlier (from the *Ramban*) regarding the reason for the prohibition of *kilayim* according to the *peshat*: "For so the King, may He be exalted, established in the act of Creation that each and every one should be according to its kind, and he who crossbreeds or grafts two species is changing and denying the act of Creation, where it is written 'according to its kind', and he is doing the opposite of Hashem, who wanted to distinguish between the species." Similarly, this is the secret of the prohibition of *kilayim*, that it "mixes the supernal forces," for the very mixing of two types of supernal forces is the opposite of the Will, as is explained in the *sefarim*, that wool and linen (and similarly ox and donkey) correspond to *Chesed* and *Gevurah*, which are opposite *midos*, and therefore their joining does not turn out well.

And according to this explanation, it must be said that the fact that we find that in holiness there is "a combination and blending of the *midos* with each other" is not like the matter of *kilayim*, for the definition of *kilayim* is when one nullifies the "according to its kind," by mixing two species together in a way that the limitations and definitions of the species are nullified. Likewise, in the mixing of the supernal forces (caused by performing *kilayim* below), a complete mixing occurs in a way that the force does not function according to its role. In contrast, the matter of combining and blending the *midos* does not nullify the function of each of the *midos*, but rather each *mida* is also combined with the opposite *mida*.

An analogy for this is the King’s ministers, where each one has a special role, which is certainly the King’s will, that not only should all his ministers work together in a way that the work of one does not contradict the mission of the other, but furthermore, that each one (in his private role) should assist in fulfilling the mission and roles of the other ministers of the King, for they are all appointed and sent to fulfill the King’s will. But together with this, each of the ministers must act specifically in his own domain and not interfere in the domain of his fellow. And when the domains and limitations of the King’s ministers are nullified, this is the opposite of the King’s will.

E. And according to the difference between these two explanations of the secret of the prohibition of *kilayim*, the difference in the reason for the permission of *kilayim* in *tzitzis* and also in the priestly garments will be understood:

According to the first way, that the reason for the prohibition of *kilayim* is because of the mixing of the holy spirit and the spirit of the *sitra achra*, the reason for the permission of *kilayim* in *tzitzis* and in the Mikdash is because "the holiness is so great there that the *sitra achra* has no power at all", meaning that the power of the *sitra achra* that is in the linen (of Cain) is nullified.

[And as the *Admur HaTzemach Tzedek* explains the words of *Rabbeinu Bachya* there regarding the reason "that *kilayim* was permitted in *tzitzis* and also in the garments of the *Kohanim*" — "because all the forces that change from one another are all included in the *mitzvah* of *tzitzis*... it goes without saying in the *Beis HaMikdash* during the service, where all the forces are included there" — "seemingly this has no understanding, for if the linen is from the aspect of the *sitra achra* of Cain, how are they included above together with holiness?" And the *Tzemach Tzedek* explains that the root of the linen above is "very high," but below it becomes a matter of *kelipah* and *sitra achra* (similar to the *Ari*, that below it is an impure animal, whereas at its root it is one of the four living creatures of the *Merkavah* and, on the contrary, belongs to the right), "and therefore in *tzitzis* and in the *Beis HaMikdash*, the linen is like it is at its root, where it is a species with its own species." This means that in the *Beis HaMikdash* (a place that is entirely holy), the root of the linen shines, where it is a matter of holiness.]

In contrast, according to the second way mentioned above, the fact that *kilayim* was permitted in *tzitzis* and in the *Beis HaMikdash* is not only because it is in the world of holiness (for according to this explanation, even in the world of holiness one should not mix the supernal forces in a way of "*kilayim*," as mentioned above), but because in holiness itself they are a very high matter, and "in a high place, the two opposites can be joined, in the manner of 'He who makes peace in His high places'", "Michael, the minister of water, and Gabriel, the minister of fire, and they do not extinguish one another".

For the matter of "He who makes peace in His high places" is not from "Michael" and "Gabriel" themselves, for since they are opposites, therefore, even though it is possible for there to be a combination and blending between them, nevertheless, it is impossible (from their own perspective) for there to be complete unity between them, that they should become like one reality (just as it is impossible to join fire and water together). Rather, the true peace between them is only through the power of the Holy One, blessed be He, who is above both of them, and makes peace between them.

An example of this is the King’s ministers when they stand before the King, for then their reality and power are not felt at all, but only the reality of the King, and therefore they stand there in complete unity without any separation between them.

And likewise regarding the permission of *kilayim* in *tzitzis* and in the *Beis HaMikdash*, which are a very high level, the *Atzmus* and *Mahus* of Him, may He be blessed, who is simple with absolute simplicity, and therefore "the two opposites can be joined."

F. It can be said that this is the inner explanation of the disagreement between the Rambam and the Raavad as to whether *kilayim* in priestly garments was permitted only during the service or even when not during the service if they are in the Mikdash:

The *shita* (view) of the Raavad is in accordance with the first explanation mentioned above regarding the secret of the prohibition of *kilayim*, from the aspect of mixing the holy spirit and the spirit of the *sitra achra*. According to this, it is understood that the permission of *kilayim* in priestly garments is from the aspect of the holiness of the Mikdash, for from the aspect of the holiness of the Mikdash, the definition of *kilayim* is nullified from it (since it is entirely holy and there is no matter of *sitra achra* there, G-d forbid) and it is considered a species with its own species.

[And based on this, one can answer (in the *pnimiyus* of the matters) the *shita* of the Raavad, that regarding priestly garments he holds that the prohibition of *kilayim* was permitted even when not during the service, whereas regarding *tzitzis* he ruled that it was not permitted except in the place of a *mitzvah*, whereas not in the place of a *mitzvah* it was not permitted, as he wrote that the fact that women who wanted to wrap themselves in *tzitzis* wrap themselves without a blessing, is "specifically with *tzitzis* that do not contain *kilayim*" — for seemingly, what is the difference (as the *Acharonim* have elaborated) — and based on the above it is understood, for only regarding priestly garments do we say that the prohibition of *kilayim* is nullified even when not during the service because of the holiness of the (place of the) Mikdash, whereas regarding *tzitzis*, the *tallis* itself does not have "any holiness at all and it is permitted to use it for mundane matters" (and also the *tzitzis* themselves are only "*tashmishei mitzvah*... that they themselves have no holiness, only that they are used for the sake of a *mitzvah*"), and therefore only in the performance of the *mitzvah* is the power of the *sitra achra* nullified and there is no prohibition of *kilayim*.]

In contrast, the *shita* of the Rambam is in accordance with the second way mentioned above regarding the secret of the prohibition of *kilayim*, that it is from the aspect of the very mixing of the supernal forces, and the permission of *kilayim* in *tzitzis* and priestly garments is because of the greatness of the revelation of *Elokus*, the simplicity of the *Atzmus*, that is in them. Therefore, the Rambam holds that the permission in priestly garments is only during the service (and not from the aspect of the general holiness of the Mikdash), for only when the *Kohanim* perform their service, which is in *bitul* as one standing before the King, before Hashem, then because of the greatness of the revelation of His *Elokus*, may He be blessed, that is in this service, "the two opposites can be joined" [and similarly in the *mitzvah* of *tzitzis*, which is a general *mitzvah*, through which "you shall remember all the commandments of Hashem", therefore also in the performance of this *mitzvah* there is this immense revelation].

G. According to the above, the (inner) reason is understood why the Rambam holds that only in the *avnet* was there *kilayim*:

The matter of the *avnet* is (as mentioned in section B) — "Prepare to meet your G-d, O Israel," meaning that the person prepares himself to stand before the King. For each of the other garments (for each one belongs to a different part of the body) indicates a specific service, belonging to a specific part of the person, whereas the *avnet* indicates a general preparation for the service, and it is the general *bitul* of the person, as he prepares himself to stand before the King.

It should be added regarding this, based on what is explained regarding the "*avnet*" according to *Chassidus*, that it corresponds to the *makif* of *yechidah*, for the essence of the *yechidah* in the soul is — "*yechidah* to unify You," that one becomes one with the *Kudsha Brich Hu*, the Unique One of the world. It can be said that this is also the reason that "its length is thirty-two *amos*, he wraps it and returns it, fold upon fold," meaning that he is not satisfied with the preparation of wrapping the *avnet* around his entire body once, but "he wraps it and returns it, fold upon fold" many times, for through this, the *mesiras nefesh* and giving over to the Holy One, blessed be He, is emphasized with absolute perfection, from the aspect of the essence of the soul, the level of *yechidah*. It can be said that this is also the hint in the fact that "its length is thirty-two *amos*" (and some say that he wraps it thirty-two times), like the numerical value of *lev* (heart) — for "the essential point in the soul (the level of *yechidah*)... is grasped in the heart" specifically.

And therefore, specifically in this garment, which belongs openly to the *bitul* of standing before Hashem, there is the matter of the permission of *kilayim*, in the manner of "He who makes peace in His high places," the *bitul* of the King’s ministers when they stand before the King (as mentioned above).

And similarly it is in the *mitzvah* of *tzitzis* (as the Rambam emphasizes, that the permission of *kilayim* in priestly garments is like the permission of *kilayim* in *tzitzis*), for the content of this *mitzvah* is general *mesiras nefesh* and giving over to the Holy One, blessed be He, from the aspect of his essence of the soul, for which it is said regarding it (not only "and you shall remember all the commandments of Hashem," but also) "so that you may remember and perform all My commandments", not only "commandments of Hashem" but also "My commandments," "a joining and connection of His very *Atzmus*," "to draw down the *Atzmus* of the light of *Ein Sof* from the aspect of the *chayah* and *yechidah* that are above the aspect of *keilim* etc.," for this drawing down is accomplished through the perfection of the service that is from the aspect of the essence of the soul.

(From a *sicha* of *Shabbos Parshas Vayakhel-Pekudei* 5750)


## פורים

## Sicha 1

### 1

At the beginning of the Megillah, it is recounted that on the seventh day of the banquet that Achashverosh made "for all the people who were present in Shushan the capital", he commanded "to bring Queen Vashti before the king... but Queen Vashti refused to come... and the king was very angry... and the king said to the wise men who knew the times... what should be done with Queen Vashti, because she did not do the word of the king..."

The reason Achashverosh presented the matter before his wise men is "for so was the king’s manner before all who knew law and judgment" (for such was the king’s custom in every legal matter, to place the issue before all who knew law and judgment — Rashi), which implies that he was in doubt regarding Vashti’s legal status. Seemingly, this is puzzling: Vashti’s behavior (that she "did not do the word of the king") was clearly an act of rebellion against the kingdom—so what was the doubt regarding her judgment? Does not one who fails to fulfill the command of a king—according to the laws of Israel, under the Torah of kindness, the king has the authority to execute him (based on the law of rebellion against the kingdom)—and it is understood that this is certainly the case among the kings of the nations, for one who rebels against the kingdom, "one law applies to him: to be put to death".

Even more puzzling is the response of Memuchan—one of the "wise men who knew the times"—"Vashti the queen has not done wrong to the king alone, but to all the princes and to all the peoples... for the queen’s deed will go out to all the women, to despise their husbands in their eyes..." In other words: the reason she is liable for death ["the queen’s deed (a royal decree of vengeance, that he commanded her to be killed — Rashi) will go out... that Vashti shall not come (and therefore she was killed — Rashi) before the king..."] is not because of the sin of her rebellion against King Achashverosh, but because she did wrong to "all the princes... all the peoples... to despise their husbands in their eyes"!

Many commentators have addressed this and provided various answers. Yet, one must examine this, for it is a tremendous difficulty in the plain meaning of the verses, and it is not even hinted at in Rashi’s commentary on the Megillah!

### 2

This can be explained by prefacing what was said above (regarding this banquet): "And the drinking was according to the law, there was no compulsion, for so the king had established upon all the officers of his house to do according to the desire of each and every man." Seemingly, the expression of the verse "for so the king had established" is difficult; especially since it is not a phrasing that fits the continuation of the verse "(the king had established) upon all the officers of his house."

And even though Rashi explains "the language of 'established' (yasad), meaning he thus ordained and commanded"—it requires study as to why he says "established" [which requires in Rashi the addition of "meaning," a specific precision — "meaning he thus ordained and commanded"], and did not use the standard expression "for so the king commanded" (as the Megillah itself uses later, "for so the king had commanded him")?

Also, Rashi’s commentary on the words "upon all the officers of his house" is difficult, where he explains "upon all the officers of the banquet, the chief baker, the chief butcher, and the chief cupbearer"—for the verse comes to provide a reason for "the drinking was according to the law, etc."—"for so, etc.," and if so, what are "the chief baker and the chief butcher" doing here?

And although the phrasing of the verse "upon all the officers of his house" implies that he commanded this to more than one officer—[in addition to the fact that even if we say the command was also to the chief baker and chief butcher, Rashi should have started with the "chief cupbearer" with whom we are concerned]—this does not force the conclusion that the verse intends to include the chief baker and especially the chief butcher, who according to the plain meaning is not present at the banquet (unlike the chief baker and cupbearer who bring the bread and drink)—for it is reasonable to explain simply that the intention is to all the servants and supervisors of the banquet (that the drinking should be in a manner of "no compulsion" but "according to the desire of each and every man"), but the "chief baker and chief butcher"—who mentioned them?!

### 3

It can be said that Rashi’s intention is to resolve the wordiness of the verse, "for so the king had established upon all the officers of his house to do according to the desire of each and every man," rather than saying briefly, "And the drinking was according to the law, there was no compulsion, for so the king had commanded"?

It is also not understood—why did the Megillah elaborate on all these details, even if the practical difference is that there is no compulsion in the drinking—which is also one of the praises of the arrangement of the banquet.

From this, Rashi learned that the fact that the drinking was in a manner of "(according to the law) no compulsion" was not a specific command from the king regarding the drinking, but a result of the fact that the king "established" and ordained the entirety of this banquet in a special manner—meaning, that all the officers of the banquet were commanded by the king "to do according to the desire of each and every man."

Therefore, Rashi explains "upon all the officers of his house"—"upon all the officers of the banquet, the chief baker, the chief butcher, and the chief cupbearer," that the king’s ordinance was regarding all the details of the banquet (that it should be "according to the desire of each and every man"), which includes all types of food and drink; and from this, that "all the officers of his house" here includes "all the officers of the banquet," who are "the chief baker, the chief butcher, and the chief cupbearer." And since the banquet in general is called "bread", its essence is eating and the drinking is secondary to it, therefore Rashi lists "the chief baker and the chief butcher" before "the chief cupbearer."

Based on this, the phrasing of the verse "to do according to the desire of each and every man" is precise, as it implies not (only) the negation of compulsion, but also the doing (the obligation)—for the essence of the king’s ordinance and command (also regarding the drinking of wine) was not (only) a matter of negation ("no compulsion"), but a positive matter ("to do according to the desire of each and every man"), that the officers of the banquet arranged it in such a way that each of the guests ate and drank according to his desire. And this is the wordiness of the verse, that the fact that "the drinking was according to the law, there was no compulsion" is "for so the king had established, etc., to do according to the desire of each and every man," which is a result of a general matter that the king established and ordained for this banquet.

### 4

And yet it requires study: since the main novelty of this banquet was that "the king had established upon all the officers of his house to do according to the desire of each and every man"—why was this stated as a reason for "the drinking was according to the law, there was no compulsion," and not as a matter in its own right?

Furthermore: what does the verse teach us by preceding "And the drinking... no compulsion"—a matter that is implied automatically from the fact that it continues immediately "for so the king had established... to do according to the desire of each and every man"? If "upon all the officers of his house" [it was commanded] to fulfill the desire of each one in everything pertaining to this banquet, it is obvious that there is no compulsion in it, there is no need to force and compel to drink!

And also the precision of the verse's language "so the king had established" is still not fully settled—for even though this pertains to the entirety of the banquet, it would have been sufficient, seemingly, to say "for so the king had commanded all the officers of his house ('all the officers of the banquet') to do according to the desire of each and every man."

It can be said that by the fact that the matter of "so the king had established, etc., to do according to the desire of each and every man" comes as a reason for "the drinking was according to the law, there was no compulsion," the novelty of this banquet is emphasized more, that it was "according to the desire of each and every man."

### 5

Explanation of the matter:
"And the drinking was according to the law, there was no compulsion," Rashi explains: "According to the law—for there are banquets where they force the guests to drink a large vessel, and there is one who cannot drink it except with difficulty, but here there is no compulsion."

Rashi’s necessity (in the plain meaning of the verse) to explain this way is understood simply—both from the content of the verse, for seemingly, why would it enter one's mind that they would force [people] to drink until it required a special command (and from the king himself—"so the king had established") that "the drinking... there was no compulsion"? And from the language of the verse, for the word "according to the law" (kadas), what is its meaning, and it should have said "and the drinking was without compulsion"?

To this, Rashi explains "for there are banquets where they force the guests to drink a large vessel"—and since there are banquets whose "law" (custom) requires forcing the drinking, it was reasonable to say that at the banquet of a king, in the present case, which was entirely "according to the hand of the king" (in all its details, and especially regarding the wine, where it is explicitly stated "and royal wine was abundant according to the hand of the king"), surely this "law" would prevail there; therefore the verse teaches us that here it was in a manner of "no compulsion, for so the king had established," that the king himself commanded not to compel, contrary to the law.

But it requires study, for the reason for the custom of such a "law" (to force drinking in a large vessel) is to show the greatness of the host, that his banquet is with abundance, and therefore they drink a large cup specifically. And if so, in the present case where we are speaking of a king’s banquet, how can they forgo this "law," and is such behavior (to change the "law" and not force drinking in a large cup) not contrary to the honor of the kingdom?

This question is answered by the precision of the verse’s language "for so the king had established, etc., to do according to the desire of each and every man"—that this banquet is different, that its entire foundation and essence from the beginning was ordained to be according to the desire of the people specifically. Meaning: there are banquets whose purpose is to increase the honor of the host, similar to the first banquet that Achashverosh made "for all his princes and his servants... when he showed the riches of the glory of his kingdom and the honor of the majesty of his greatness"—and at such a banquet, the honor of the kingdom requires acting according to the "law," "to drink a large vessel" specifically; but the banquet that Achashverosh made afterward "for all the people who were present in Shushan the capital" was for a different purpose—not to show the king’s greatness and increase his honor, but that it should be "according to the desire of each and every man," according to the desire of the people specifically (as Rashi says "to do... for each and every one his desire"). And since "so the king had established" this banquet from the outset, it is understood that this banquet is not included among those banquets (as per the precision of Rashi’s language "for there are banquets where they force, etc.," that this is not in every banquet) where they force to drink a large cup specifically, because that is contrary to the purpose of this banquet.

And this is also the reason that the verse is precise in recounting "And the drinking was according to the law, there was no compulsion," for in this it is emphasized to what extent this banquet was "to do according to the desire of each and every man" (the desire and enjoyment of the people specifically), to the point that from this aspect they could abolish the "drinking according to the law" (contrary to the honor of the kingdom), since the purpose of this banquet was "to do according to the desire of each and every man" as mentioned above.

### 6

Based on the above, the aforementioned difficulty (Section 1) regarding Vashti is simply resolved (to the point that Rashi does not need to explain it in his commentary);
Since this entire banquet was to do according to the desire of each and every one, and it is obvious that "there is no compulsion" in everything related to this banquet (since so the king had established and this is his honor)—there is room to say that Vashti is not obligated to come "before the king... to show the peoples and the princes her beauty" under compulsion (and her refusal is not a rebellion against the kingdom, but on the contrary, in accordance with the king’s desire, "to do according to the desire of each and every man").

But it is understood that there is room to say the opposite [and for several reasons: 1) It can be said that bringing Vashti before the princes is not a detail and part of the banquet and the command "to do according to the desire of each and every man" does not apply to it. 2) The command "to do according to the desire of each and every man" is a general one, but here there is an explicit command from the king. 3) "To do according to the desire of each and every man" was regarding "the people who were present in Shushan the capital," whom Achashverosh wanted to provide enjoyment, etc., and the queen is not included]—and therefore Achashverosh consulted with "the wise men who knew the times," and they required the additional reason "not to the king alone, etc."

### 7

It is known from our Sages that every "king" mentioned in the Megillah hints at the Holy One, Blessed be He, from which it is understood also in our verse, "for so the king had established, etc., to do according to the desire of each and every man"—that in this verse is hinted the conduct of the Holy One, Blessed be He, in relation to His people Israel.

And it can be said that in this verse is hinted the elevation and the novelty that was brought about through the self-sacrifice (mesiras nefesh) of Israel in the days of Purim. For our Sages said that at the time of the Giving of the Torah "the Holy One, Blessed be He, overturned the mountain over them like a cask, etc. From here there is a great protest regarding the Torah (that if He summons them to judgment, 'Why did you not fulfill what you accepted upon yourselves?' they have an answer that they accepted it under compulsion — Rashi), etc. They accepted it again in the days of Achashverosh, as it is written 'The Jews confirmed and accepted,' they confirmed what they had already accepted."

And this is also the hint in our verse—regarding the banquet that the king made "for all the people who were present in Shushan the capital," which refers to Israel, who are His close people (and are present in the capital of the King of Kings, the Holy One, Blessed be He)—that the Holy One, Blessed be He, invites them to the banquet, which is the study of Torah and the fulfillment of mitzvos, which is the banquet and enjoyment of a Jewish person; and the Holy One, Blessed be He, gives free choice, "no compulsion," and on the contrary, "to do according to the desire of each and every man."

And this was the elevation of the days of Purim, that then "they confirmed what they had already accepted," not out of compulsion but specifically out of their free choice.

And therefore the joy of Purim grew beyond all measurement and limitation, to the point that "a person is obligated to become intoxicated on Purim until he does not know, etc.", and as Rashi explains "to become intoxicated—with wine," for it can be said that this is a matter of "v'nahafochu" (and it was turned around), that in place of the "wine" that Achashverosh gave at his banquet, in a manner of "and royal wine was abundant according to the hand of the king," which was not for the good, there comes "wine... abundant" of holiness, the joy of Purim that is beyond measurement and limitation, to the point that even when all the festivals are nullified (that the joy of the festivals will not have the same importance relative to the joy that will be in the joy of the Redemption), that there will be joy beyond measurement and limitation, and to the point that "everlasting joy shall be upon their heads"—the joy of Purim will not be nullified, "and these days of Purim shall not pass from among the Jews, nor shall their memory perish from their descendants."

(From a Sicha of Purim 5727)

## Sicha 2

### 167

### Sicha 2

a. We learned in the Gemara (regarding the reason for the Prophets’ enactment to read the Megillah on Purim): "If for the transition from slavery to freedom we recite a song (at the exodus from Egypt they recited a song at the sea. Rashi), then from death to life, is it not all the more so? If so, we should also recite Hallel. (The Gemara answers:) Because we do not recite Hallel for a miracle that occurred outside the Land. How then do we recite a song for the exodus from Egypt, which was a miracle outside the Land? (The Gemara answers:) As it is taught in a Baraisa, before the Jewish people entered the Land, all lands were fit for reciting a song; once the Jewish people entered the Land, not all lands were fit for reciting a song. Rav Nachman said: This reading [of the Megillah] is the Hallel. Rava said: Granted, there (at the exodus from Egypt), it is 'Give praise, you servants of Hashem' and not servants of Pharaoh (for they went out to freedom. Rashi), but here, [can we say] 'Give praise, you servants of Hashem' and not servants of Achashverosh? We are still servants of Achashverosh (for they were only saved from death. Rashi)."

It emerges that there are three reasons why it was not enacted to recite Hallel on Purim: 1) Because the miracle of Purim occurred outside the Land. 2) The reading of the Megillah takes the place of Hallel. 3) Because it is impossible to say "Give praise, you servants of Hashem (and not servants of Achashverosh)," since "we are still servants of Achashverosh."

This means that according to the first and third reasons, there is no obligation to recite Hallel, whereas according to the second reason, one is obligated in Hallel, but one fulfills the obligation of Hallel through the reading of the Megillah (and according to the Meiri, this has a practical halachic difference for one who does not have a Megillah, as according to the reason "this reading is the Hallel," he would be obligated to recite Hallel).

Seemingly, the third reason, "we are still servants of Achashverosh" (and Rashi explains that "they were only saved from death"), requires study—for the Gemara’s original question still stands: "If for the transition from slavery to freedom we recite a song, then from death to life, is it not all the more so?" Does the fact that the situation does not fit the verse "Give praise, you servants of Hashem" exempt a person from praising Hashem for his salvation "from death to life" (which is greater than the salvation "from slavery to freedom")?

We must conclude that the concept of "servants of Hashem" is essential to the very act of reciting Hallel. When this condition is lacking, it prevents one from reciting Hallel. The reading of the Megillah was enacted specifically because of the *kal v'chomer*—"If for the transition from slavery to freedom we recite a song, then from death to life, is it not all the more so?"—but for the recitation of Hallel itself, one requires the state of being "servants of Hashem."

b. The Gemara there continues: "Both according to Rava and according to Rav Nachman, there is a difficulty [and Rashi explains: "Both according to Rava, who said that they do not recite Hallel because they were still servants of Achashverosh—if not for that, they would recite it; and according to Rav Nachman, who said that the reading of the Megillah is in place of Hallel"]—but it is taught in a Baraisa that once they entered the Land, not all lands were fit for reciting a song? (The Gemara answers:) Once they were exiled, they returned to their original status." This implies that even according to Rava, an obligation to recite Hallel is possible even after the Jewish people were exiled, for a miracle that occurred outside the Land ("they returned to their original status").

Seemingly, this is puzzling. According to Rava, as long as they were not redeemed "from slavery to freedom," they do not recite Hallel. If so, how can there be an obligation of Hallel after "they were exiled" and are in a state of "still being servants" (as is explicitly stated in the Ran that "this reason of Rava applies to every miracle performed outside the Land while the Jewish people are in exile")?

c. The explanation for all of this is as follows:

All three aforementioned reasons for why it was not enacted to recite Hallel on Purim are rooted in one point—that the miracle of Purim was not an open miracle [for we do not find in any of the details of the Purim miracle a suspension of the laws of nature or a nullification of the world's conduct, but rather that it was "perceived through the intellect that all the causes were above nature"], whereas the enactment to recite Hallel is only for an explicitly open miracle. To the extent that they said, "One who recites Hallel every day is a blasphemer and a reviler," for even though we thank Hashem every day in our prayers "for Your miracles that are with us every day," that He, may He be blessed, performs miracles for us "every day"—nevertheless, this does not obligate the recitation of Hallel (and on the contrary, "one who recites Hallel every day, etc."), because they are not open miracles, and the recitation of Hallel is specifically for an open miracle, as mentioned above.

However, there is a fundamental difference between "Your miracles that are with us every day" and the miracle of Purim. "Your miracles that are with us every day" are the kindnesses that come through natural conduct, and they are called "miracles" because, in truth, even the conduct of nature is a matter of miracles (as is explained in many places, that nature itself is "many constant and frequent miracles, etc."). The miracle of Purim, by contrast, was entirely a miraculous conduct, only the miracle was clothed in the ways of nature.

Therefore, there is room for debate as to whether there is an obligation to recite Hallel on Purim: from the perspective of the miracle in the Purim event, it is possible that there is an obligation to recite Hallel, whereas from the perspective of the natural garb of the Purim miracle, one could say that we are exempt from Hallel (similar to the absence of reciting Hallel every day).

Based on this, we can explain the opinion of Rav Nachman, "This reading is the Hallel." It is possible that his intention is not that they did not enact Hallel on Purim because they already enacted the reading of the Megillah and therefore there is no need (also) for Hallel—but rather that the recitation of Hallel on Purim is impossible except through the reading of the Megillah. In and of itself, a person does not feel the miracle in the Purim story in a way that obligates him to recite Hallel (which is only for an open miracle, as mentioned above). Only through the reading of the Megillah is it possible for him to praise Hashem for this miracle—for since it is one of the twenty-four books of Holy Scripture, a part of the true Torah and "Torah Or," through it and by means of it, the truth of the matter is illuminated and felt: that this was a miracle from the Holy One, Blessed be He (which was merely clothed in the garments of nature). This is what Rav Nachman meant: "This reading is the Hallel," meaning that the recitation of Hallel depends upon and is included within the reading of the Megillah.

[And a practical difference: according to this reasoning, even one who does not have a Megillah does not recite Hallel. This is in accordance with what is written in the later authorities that "from the silence of the authorities, it is implied that there is no Hallel for it ever." However, they explained that since the Sages did not enact Hallel on Purim, there is no place for its recitation even if one does not have a Megillah. According to the above, it goes further than that: from the outset, the recitation of Hallel on Purim is impossible except through the reading of the Megillah alone.]

d. The reason why it is impossible to recite Hallel for the miracle of Purim, because it is a miracle clothed in nature, can be explained in two ways:

1) This is from the perspective of the *cheftza* (the object/nature) of the miracle: a miracle clothed in nature is not in the category of a miracle sufficient to obligate the recitation of Hallel (just as with natural conduct, even though it is also "Your miracles that are with us every day," it does not obligate the recitation of Hallel; on the contrary, it is forbidden to recite Hallel for such conduct).

2) This is from the perspective of the deficiency in the *gavra* (the person): because it is difficult for a person to recognize the miracle within this conduct, it is impossible to enact and obligate the recitation of Hallel for it.

It can be said that this is the difference between the two reasons why there is no obligation to recite Hallel at all on Purim—(a) because we do not recite Hallel for a miracle outside the Land, (b) "we are still servants of Achashverosh":

According to the first reason, it is because of the *cheftza* of the miracle: it is known that in Divine Providence there is a difference between the Land of Israel and outside the Land, for regarding the Land of Israel it is said, "The eyes of Hashem your God are upon it from the beginning of the year to the end of the year," meaning that Divine Providence there is in an open manner, unlike outside the Land, where Divine Providence is hidden there in the garments of nature. Therefore, this opinion holds that a miracle performed outside the Land (where there is no open Providence, and thus the miracle is also clothed and hidden in nature) is not of the type of miracle that obligates the recitation of Hallel.

Rava holds that after they were exiled, the location of outside the Land does not preclude the possibility of reciting Hallel, and the deficiency is from the perspective of the *gavra*: "We are still servants of Achashverosh." In order to recite Hallel, one must be "servants of Hashem and not servants of Achashverosh," for only one who is at the level of "servants of Hashem" can feel the miraculous conduct within a miracle clothed in nature. Unlike when the Jewish people are enslaved to the nations of the world, it is impossible for them to reach this feeling, and consequently, there is no place for the recitation of Hallel.

e. This can be explained in the context of a person's *avodah*:

The difference between the Land of Israel and outside the Land within the person himself is the difference between the matters of his *neshamah* and the matters of his body. In the human *neshamah*, which is "a part of Hashem from above in truth", the Divine light is felt in an open manner (similar to the matter of "the eyes of Hashem your God are upon it"), unlike the matters of the body, which is "outside" this revelation.

This is the content of the first opinion that we do not recite Hallel for a miracle outside the Land, for the recitation of Hallel is the praise for Divine revelation, and this belongs only to the perspective of the person's *neshamah*, which feels this revelation, and not the body.

Based on this, we can explain (in the inner dimension of the matters) the opinion of Rava that we do not recite Hallel for the miracle of Purim because "we are still servants of Achashverosh," yet he holds "once they were exiled, they returned to their original status":

The fact that "we are still servants of Achashverosh" is only regarding the matters of the body. Unlike regarding the matters of the *neshamah*, for since the giving of the Torah, the Jewish people became "servants of Hashem" forever, as it is explicitly stated in the verse, "For the children of Israel are servants to Me, they are My servants," and as the Sages said, "My deed of sale is prior" [and as the Rebbe Rayatz said in the name of his father, "Only our bodies were given into exile and the servitude of kingdoms, but our *neshamos* were not surrendered to exile and the servitude of kingdoms"].

Therefore, Rava did not rule out the category of Hallel during the time of exile, because the matters of the *neshamah* were not affected by the exile, and in them, praise is possible. Only regarding the miracle of Purim, which was clothed in nature, is praise impossible, because in relation to nature (meaning physical things, and in the person himself—the matters of the body), "we are servants of Achashverosh."

Rav Nachman holds "this reading is the Hallel," that through the power of Torah, it is possible for a person to draw down Divine revelation even into the matters of nature, so that in them too, the light of holiness will be felt.

And in a person's *avodah*—when he serves Hashem in all his physical matters in a way that "all your deeds shall be for the sake of Heaven" and "in all your ways know Him," then he draws down into them Divine revelation similar to the matters of his *neshamah*.

This is also the inner meaning of what the Sages said, "to juxtapose one redemption to another," that we must juxtapose the redemption of Purim to the redemption of Pesach. The redemption of Pesach, which came about because "the King of Kings, the Holy One, Blessed be He, was revealed to them and redeemed them" and through open miracles, caused the Jewish people to be "servants of Hashem and not servants of Pharaoh." The ultimate goal is to juxtapose and draw this "redemption" also into the redemption of Purim, so that even in the matters of nature it will be felt that in essence they are matters of miracles,

until the fulfillment of the prophecy, "And the glory of Hashem shall be revealed, and all flesh shall see together that the mouth of Hashem has spoken," in the true and complete redemption, speedily in our days, literally.

(From a Purim Sicha, 5722)


## תשא

## Sicha 1

### 172

**1.** The Gemara states: "A person should never deviate from the local custom, for Moshe ascended to the heavens and did not eat bread, while the ministering angels descended below and ate bread. Did they actually eat? Rather, say that they appeared as if they had eaten and drunk."

Similarly, it is stated in *Shemos Rabbah* on our Parsha (regarding the verse, "And he was there with Hashem forty days and forty nights; he did not eat bread and he did not drink water"): "Is it possible for a person to be forty days without food and drink? Rabbi Tanchuma in the name of Rabbi Acha bar Rabbi Avin in the name of Rabbi Meir says: The proverb says, 'If you enter a city, follow its customs.' Above, where there is no eating and drinking, Moshe ascended and it appeared to them below as if there were eating and drinking. The ministering angels descended and ate and drank, as it is written, 'And he stood over them under the tree, and they ate.' Rabbi Yochanan says: They appeared as if they were eating; [the food] would disappear bit by bit."

One must note the difference between the language of the Gemara and the language of the Midrash. The Gemara uses the language of negation—"A person should never deviate from the local custom"—whereas the Midrash uses the language of affirmation—"If you enter a city, follow its customs."

Seemingly, the difference between the Gemara and the Midrash lies in the nature of the requirement to follow local custom. For the Gemara (which we can say refers to a halachic obligation), it is a decree for the sake of peace and the like, and therefore it uses the negative phrasing, "A person should never deviate..." However, the Midrash (which speaks in every instance of conduct according to the path of Chassidus) adds that it is an obligation; that anyone who comes to a specific place is obligated to conduct himself according to the customs of the people of that place. Therefore, it uses the language of affirmation, "If you enter a city, follow its customs."

With this, we can also explain the intent of the Tosafos in the Gemara there, which, regarding the Gemara’s statement, "Did they actually eat? Rather, say they appeared as if they had eaten and drunk," brings that "in *Seder Eliyahu Rabbah* it is taught: Not as the one who says they appeared as if they were eating and drinking, but they actually ate and drank out of respect for Avraham, and this disagrees with this [Gemara]." According to the above, the intent of Tosafos is not that they disagree on whether they ate in practice or only "appeared as if they were eating," but rather that this is a disagreement in halacha. The Gemara holds that it is only a negation—"a person should never deviate from the local custom"—and therefore it is sufficient that the angels "appeared as if they were eating" so that there would be no dispute. Conversely, for *Seder Eliyahu Rabbah*, it is an obligation to conduct oneself according to the custom of the place, and therefore they had to actually eat and drink.

The Midrash, which holds (as above) that it is an obligation ("follow its customs"), writes (like *Seder Eliyahu Rabbah*) "and they ate and drank" (and only Rabbi Yochanan disagrees, saying "they appeared as if they were eating; it would disappear bit by bit"). They actually ate and drank. Accordingly, the precision of the Midrash's language regarding Moshe’s lack of eating on the mountain is understood: "Above, where there is no eating and drinking, Moshe ascended and it appeared to them" (rather than the Gemara's language, "and he did not eat bread"), because it is not enough that he simply did not eat; it must be in a manner of obligation, that "it appeared to them."

### 173

**2.** The statement of Rabbi Tanchuma in the Midrash is also brought in *Bereishis Rabbah*, where the language is: "If you enter a city, follow its customs. Above, where there is no eating and drinking, Moshe ascended to the heavens and did not eat, as it is written, 'And I sat on the mountain forty days and forty nights; I did not eat bread and I did not drink water.' But below, where there is eating and drinking, 'And he stood over them under the tree, and they ate.' Were they actually eating? Rather, they appeared as if they were eating..."

Here, the language is "and he did not eat," like the Gemara (and not "it appeared to them" as in *Shemos Rabbah*). Furthermore, the Midrash there concludes, "Were they actually eating? Rather, they appeared as if they were eating," meaning this is not a separate opinion (as in *Shemos Rabbah*, where it is the view of Rabbi Yochanan) but a continuation of that same statement.

However, on the other hand, even there the language is "If you enter a city, follow its customs," which is the language of obligation, unlike the language of the Gemara.

It can be said that the view of *Bereishis Rabbah* is a third approach. The statement "If you enter a city, follow its customs" can be interpreted in two ways: 1) It is a law of actual conduct—one must behave according to the local custom. 2) It is not merely a matter of conduct, but that the parameters of that place apply to him; he becomes like one of the people of the place, and consequently, he must behave according to their custom.

This is the point of difference between *Shemos Rabbah*, which emphasizes that Moshe Rabbeinu "appeared to them" [as if he were eating], and *Bereishis Rabbah*, which notes only that Moshe "did not eat." For *Bereishis Rabbah*, the basis of the law to follow local custom is merely a matter of conduct, and therefore it is sufficient that Moshe Rabbeinu "did not eat." For *Shemos Rabbah*, it is a law and an obligation that since he became like the people of the place, he is obligated to conduct himself according to the custom of the place, and therefore it emphasizes that "it appeared to them," as follows.

### 174

[One might say there is a practical difference between the three aforementioned approaches: (a) It is only the negation of "do not deviate"; (b) It is a law of obligation to behave like the people of the place; (c) One becomes like one of the people of the place. According to the first approach, not only is it sufficient that he merely appears to act like the people of the place, but we do not care about the reason for his conduct; if his conduct is not different from the people of the place, that is sufficient (because no dispute will arise from it). According to the second approach, the reason for the conduct must be that he wants to equate himself to the people of the place (at which point he fulfills "follow its customs"), but it is sufficient that he appears to the people of the place to be equating himself to them. Whereas according to the third approach, he must behave that way from his own essence, because he must be like them.]

**3.** It can be said that this dispute (between the Gemara, *Shemos Rabbah*, and *Bereishis Rabbah*) also exists regarding the nature of Moshe Rabbeinu’s lack of eating on the mountain. According to halacha, a person cannot live without food for more than seven days—as per the ruling of the Rambam that one who vows not to eat anything for seven days has made a vain vow—which can be explained in three ways:

a) As the Rambam explains in *Moreh Nevuchim*: "Whoever directs all his thought toward his perfection in Elokus and is entirely inclined toward Hashem Yisbarach... clears his mind of everything else... Among them are those who have come to the King's palace, and this is the level of the prophets. There are those who have reached, through the greatness of their apprehension and the clearing of their mind from everything other than Hashem, the point where it is said of him, 'And he was there with Hashem,' etc... And because of the greatness of his joy in what he apprehended, he did not eat bread and he did not drink water, for the intellect was strengthened to the point that every coarse power in the body was nullified." According to this approach, it turns out that this was a natural matter (as explained by the commentators, "for he did not feel any hunger, and therefore he did not eat").

[According to this, one must say that the halachic ruling that one who vows not to eat for seven days has made a vain vow applies because the Torah speaks of the majority of cases, but a situation can exist according to nature where the body does not need food and drink for more than seven days, as with Moshe Rabbeinu on the mountain.]

b) When he ascended the mountain with Hashem, the nature of his body changed to be like an angel, in such a way that he did not require food and drink (just as angels do not require food and drink).

### 175

There is a practical difference between these two approaches regarding the aforementioned law of one who vows not to eat anything for seven days. We must inquire regarding the law of Moshe Rabbeinu after the forty days: if he had vowed then, immediately, not to eat (for less than seven days), would it be in the category of a vain vow? According to the first approach, that the nature of Moshe’s body did not change, and furthermore, that even during the forty days the body (in its own essence) was hungry for bread but it did not act upon or control Moshe, and therefore he did not eat—it is reasonable to say that after the forty days, when he descended from the mountain, Moshe’s body (which had been afflicted for forty days) could not fast any longer, and it would be a vain vow. Conversely, according to the opinion that the nature of his body changed to be like an angel, then after the forty days, when his body's nature returned, a new calculation begins, and it is not a vain vow until he vows not to eat for seven days.

c) The fact that he did not need food and drink was by way of a miracle that continued the entire time. That is to say, according to the nature of his body, he needed food and drink, but there was a miracle during the forty days that he did not eat or drink [for according to the second approach mentioned above, there was no miracle that continued for the entire forty days (because according to his nature then, he did not need to eat), and only at the first moment did Hashem perform a miracle that changed the nature of Moshe’s body; whereas according to the third approach, it was a continuous miracle throughout the entire forty days].

### 176

**4.** This is the difference between the three opinions—the Gemara, *Shemos Rabbah*, and *Bereishis Rabbah*:

For the Gemara, which holds that the law is only that a person should not deviate from the local custom, it is sufficient that he does not eat in practice. Accordingly, it holds that Moshe’s lack of eating was not a miracle but a natural matter, that due to the greatness of his joy and his preoccupation with receiving the Torah (and his being "with Hashem"), he did not feel the body's hunger.

[This is similar to the angels only "appearing as if they had eaten and drunk," because they did not change and did not need food and drink—and likewise here, that Moshe’s body (even during these forty days) needed food and drink, but it appeared as if he did not need it because of the greatness of Moshe’s engagement in receiving the Torah.]

Even though according to this, Moshe’s lack of food and drink is not similar to the angels' lack of food and drink (who are spiritual and do not need food and drink from the outset), it is sufficient to fulfill "a person should never deviate from the local custom," since Moshe was not eating and drinking (and as mentioned in section 2, according to this approach, we do not care about the reason he does not deviate from the local custom, for the main thing is only that there should not be a change that could incite dispute).

For *Shemos Rabbah*, which holds that "follow its customs" means that he becomes like one of the people of the place, it is not sufficient that he did not eat in practice; rather, there must have been a change in the nature of Moshe Rabbeinu’s body, that he became like an angel who does not require food and drink [similar to the angels who actually ate and drank], and this is the precision of the language of *Shemos Rabbah*, "it appeared to them" (as above).

Whereas *Bereishis Rabbah*, which holds that the law of "follow its customs" is only a matter of a person's conduct, maintains that there was a constant miracle here. Even though the nature of his body did not change, nevertheless, since in practice the "conduct" of Moshe’s body was like the conduct of the angels (due to the constant miracle that occurred in his body), this is also called fulfilling "follow its customs," since the reason for this miracle was that it should be in the conduct of the higher beings.

[But since he does not need to become exactly like the people of the place, and it is sufficient that he resembles them in his conduct, therefore when the angels descended below, it was sufficient that they "appeared as if they were eating," even though they did not eat in practice (similar to the above regarding the Gemara’s approach).]

### 177

**5.** If you would say that this is explicitly addressed in the three aforementioned ways in the Midrash itself, in our Parsha regarding the verse, "And Hashem said to Moshe, 'Write for yourself these words,'" it states:

"This is what is written: 'It is good for me that I was afflicted, so that I might learn Your statutes.' For the benefit of Moshe, he was afflicted for 120 days while he received the Torah. And from where did Moshe eat? From the radiance of the Shechinah, as it is written, 'And You give life to all of them.' Another explanation: From where did he eat? From the Torah, as it is written, 'Son of man, eat what you find,' etc. Another explanation: From the 'bread' of Torah, as it is written, 'Go, eat of my bread.' From where do we know that he did not sleep or doze? A parable of a king who loved a treasurer. He said to him, 'Measure out for yourself gold dinars.' In his joy, he did not ask to eat or drink. He wanted to sleep, but he said, 'If I sleep, I will lose these.' So too, Moshe was measuring the Torah; he forgot and did not eat or drink. He wanted to sleep, but he said, 'If I sleep, I will lose, for He only told me forty days.' The Holy One, Blessed be He, said, 'You have suffered; by your life, you will not lose. In the first tablets, there were only the Ten Commandments. Now that you have suffered, I am giving you halachos, midrashim, and aggados,' etc."

It requires understanding what they differ on: whether he ate from the radiance of the Shechinah, or from the Torah, or from the "bread" of Torah.

Now, among the differences between the three interpretations: In the first interpretation, it says he ate "from the radiance of the Shechinah," and cites the verse "And You give life to all of them." In the second interpretation, it brings the verse "Son of man, eat what you find," etc. In the third interpretation, the teaching is from the verse "Go, eat of my bread," and it continues, "From where do we know that he did not sleep," and it elaborates on the aforementioned parable that "in his joy he did not ask to eat or drink... he forgot and did not eat or drink... The Holy One, Blessed be He, said, 'You have suffered,'" etc.

Accordingly, it can be said that these three interpretations of what Moshe’s food was correspond to the three aforementioned approaches regarding Moshe’s abstention from food and drink on the mountain:

In the third interpretation, it is emphasized that Moshe’s lack of food and drink (and sleep) was only due to his preoccupation with receiving the Torah. This is similar to the words of the *Moreh Nevuchim* mentioned above, according to which there was no miracle here; Moshe Rabbeinu’s body needed food and drink and was hungry, but Moshe Rabbeinu did not feel the hunger and did not eat.

This is also the interpretation of the Midrash’s words that Moshe Rabbeinu "suffered"—for seemingly it is puzzling: how can it be said that Moshe Rabbeinu would suffer from the lack of food and drink when he ascended to receive the Torah from the Holy One, Blessed be He? Rather, the intent of the Midrash is that Moshe Rabbeinu’s body, in its own essence, was in affliction and suffering, but Moshe Rabbeinu did not feel the suffering of the body because of the greatness of his preoccupation and joy in receiving the Torah.

According to the second interpretation, it can be said that the nature of Moshe Rabbeinu’s body changed to be like the nature of an angel, but it holds that just as for an angel there is a category of food and drink, and his nourishment is spiritual nourishment, so too was Moshe’s nourishment during these forty days spiritual nourishment—Torah. Therefore, it brings the verse "Son of man, eat what you find," which emphasizes that the nature (of "son of man") that requires food remained in him, but when he ascended the mountain, his nourishment and food were from the Torah.

[This is the difference between the third interpretation, "bread of Torah," and the second interpretation, "from the Torah." "From the Torah" emphasizes that his nourishment is from the Torah itself, whereas "bread of Torah" can be said to mean that the Torah is in place of physical bread—meaning that due to the preoccupation with Torah, one does not feel the need for physical food.]

Whereas according to the first interpretation, that "he ate from the radiance of the Shechinah," and it brings the verse "And You give life to all of them," there was no action of eating here, but only a miraculous matter from the Holy One, Blessed be He, that "You give life to all of them," that the vitality of Moshe’s body was truly like the existence of a person’s soul (and likewise for the angels according to this view), that they do not need food and drink and only enjoy the radiance of the Shechinah.

### 178

And this is their existence and vitality.

**6.** It can be said that since these and those are the words of the living G-d, each of these three approaches occurred during different forty-day periods that Moshe was on the mountain:

Moshe’s ascent during the first forty days was to receive the first tablets, which were the work of G-d and the writing of G-d—a miracle entirely above nature. Accordingly, Moshe’s lack of food and drink was not in the category of nature, and not even because his body was elevated to be like the nature of angels—but it was a miraculous matter from the Holy One, Blessed be He.

During the middle forty days (which were in anger), there was no miraculous conduct, and Moshe’s lack of food and drink was a natural matter, that due to the greatness of his preoccupation and engagement in being "with Hashem," he did not feel the demands of his body.

And during the last forty days, when he ascended to receive the second tablets, and they were "with will" (like the first days), his lack of food and drink was a miracle, but just as the second tablets were not the work of G-d, so too Moshe’s lack of food and drink on the mountain was not a constant miracle from Hashem, but rather his body was refined to be like the body of an angel that does not need (physical) food and drink.

Accordingly, the difference between the aforementioned *Shemos Rabbah* and *Bereishis Rabbah* is precise: *Bereishis Rabbah* brings the verse from Parshas Eikev, "And I sat on the mountain... I did not eat bread," which speaks of the first forty days, whereas *Shemos Rabbah* relates to the verse in our Parsha, which speaks of the last forty days. For *Bereishis Rabbah* holds that it was in a miraculous manner as above, and therefore it brought the verse from Parshas Eikev which speaks of Moshe’s ascent during the first forty days. Conversely, *Shemos Rabbah*, which holds that the nature of Moshe’s body changed until "it appeared to them"—relates to the verse in our Parsha regarding the last forty days.

But on the other hand, just as there is a superiority in the second tablets over the first tablets—that even though the first tablets were in a higher state, nevertheless, breaking was possible for them, whereas the second tablets are eternal—so too regarding the matter of Moshe’s lack of food and drink: even though according to the first approach it is more of a miracle, nevertheless it does not relate to the nature of Moshe’s body. Conversely, when

### 179

Moshe’s body was refined to be like an angel, this is a more internal matter.

It can be said that this is the reason that "the skin of his face shone" specifically when Moshe descended from the mountain with the second tablets on Yom Kippur, and not when he descended from the mountain with the first tablets after the first forty days—because the fact that Moshe’s face shone with rays of splendor indicates the refinement of the physicality itself, until his face shines like a soul. Therefore, this came specifically after he was on the mountain during the last forty days, at which time Moshe’s body was refined, until "the skin of his face shone."

(From a Sicha of Shabbos Parshas Nasso, 5746)

## Sicha 2

1. In the Zohar of our Parsha it is related that Rabbi Yosi and Rabbi Chiya were walking on the road and discussing matters of Torah. Eventually, Rabbi Chiya began to expound on the verse, "And it shall be in the end of days, the mountain of the House of Hashem shall be established at the top of the mountains... and all the nations shall stream to it." Afterwards, Rabbi Yosi said, "Fortunate is this path, that we have merited this teaching." He (Rabbi Yosi) asked him, "From whom did you hear it?" He (Rabbi Chiya) replied, "One day I was walking on the road and I heard and saw Rav Hamnuna Sava (another version: Rabbi Shimon) expounding this verse to Rabbi Acha."

My revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, in his notes on the Zohar, explains this passage of the Zohar at length according to Kabbalah. Within his words, he writes (the following is a translation of his words):

"Initially, when Rabbi Chiya said this exposition to Rabbi Yosi, he did not say it in the name of Rav Hamnuna or Rabbi Shimon. Seemingly, one is required to say a thing in the name of its author, as the statement goes: 'Whoever says a thing in the name of its author brings redemption to the world.' Now, according to the simple meaning, one could say that when one is required to say a thing in the name of its author is when the author said the thing *to* the person who is now repeating it; then he must say it in the name of the author. Just as with Mordechai and Esther, from whom we learn this—as it is written that Mordechai told Queen Esther—then the one who hears it from him must say it in the name of the author, as it is written that 'Esther told the king in the name of Mordechai.' This is not the case in our instance: Rav Hamnuna or Rabbi Shimon did not say this to Rabbi Chiya, but rather to Rabbi Acha. Rabbi Chiya, who happened to be there, also heard it, but Rav Hamnuna or Rabbi Shimon did not say it *to him*. Therefore, he is not obligated to say it in the name of the author, for there is no proof for this from Mordechai and Esther, since there, Mordechai spoke to Esther. It was only when Rabbi Yosi asked him, 'From whom did you hear it?' that Rabbi Chiya told him from whom he heard it.

"And according to this, the phrasing of Rabbi Yosi, 'From whom did you hear it?'—rather than 'Who told you?'—is also well-understood. It is because Rabbi Yosi understood that this exposition was not said *to* Rabbi Chiya, for if it had been, he would have told him in the name of the author. Therefore, he asked him, 'From whom did you hear it?'—meaning, not 'Who told you,' but 'From whom did you hear it,' because it was not said to Rabbi Chiya, but rather Rabbi Chiya heard it." End quote.

2. This requires understanding: Saying a thing in the name of its author is not merely a *segulah* for redemption, as the Sages said above, "Whoever says a thing in the name of its author brings redemption to the world" (the source of which is in Maseches Avos, and it is also cited in Chullin and Niddah, as my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, wrote in the continuation of his words there). Rather, it is an absolute obligation, and it is brought as *halacha* in the Magen Avraham that "anyone who does not say a thing in the name of its author transgresses a negative commandment (Nedarim, first chapter)" [and its source is in the Midrash: "Rabbi Chizkiyah said in the name of Rabbi Yirmiyah bar Abba in the name of Rabbi Yochanan: Whoever does not say a thing in the name of its author, the verse says about him, 'Do not rob the poor because he is poor,' and a person must, when he hears a thing, say it in the name of its author"].

According to this, we must understand what my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, wrote: "According to the simple meaning, one could say that when one is required to say a thing in the name of its author is when the author said the thing *to* the person... as with Mordechai and Esther." Seemingly, this distinction has a place only regarding the *segulah* of bringing redemption to the world. For when the speaker only heard the thing from the author but it was not said *to him*, there is no proof for this from Mordechai and Esther, since "there, Mordechai spoke to Esther." But regarding the negative commandment of "Do not rob the poor," it is understood that it applies even when one hears a thing that was said to someone else. On the contrary, it would seemingly fall more into the category of "robbery" from the author when one hears a thing that was said to another—that the author did not intend to say it to him but to someone else—and he is as if snatching and saying what he heard from the mouths of others without mentioning the name of the author.

We must therefore say that he is speaking here of a case where this prohibition of "Do not rob the poor" does not apply.

3. This can be understood by prefacing that we find several statements of the Sages that were not said in the name of their authors. This is especially emphasized regarding Rabbi Eliezer the Great, who, even though he "never said a thing that he did not hear from his teacher," nevertheless has many statements in which he did not mention the name of his teacher. Why did he not worry about the fact that anyone who does not say a thing in the name of its author transgresses a negative commandment?

[Seemingly, one could say based on what is said in the Gemara: "Rabbi Elazar went and said a teaching in the Beis Medrash and did not say it in the name of Rabbi Yochanan. Rabbi Yochanan heard and was upset... Rabbi Ami and Rabbi Asi went to him and said to him... 'And on every thing that Joshua said, did he say to them, "Thus said Moses to me"? Rather, Joshua sat and expounded anonymously, and everyone knew that it was the Torah of Moses. So too, Rabbi Elazar, your student, sits and expounds anonymously, and everyone knows that it is yours.'"

According to this, one could say that it is the same with Rabbi Eliezer the Great, that he did not need to say his words in the name of his teacher because everyone knew that all his words were those of his teacher.

However, it is seemingly forced to explain the conduct of Rabbi Eliezer the Great this way. From the fact that he said, "I have learned much Torah and I have not lacked from my teachers,"—"teachers" being plural—it is proven that he had more than one teacher. If so, he should have mentioned for every thing from which teacher he received it.]

According to the simple meaning, one could say that the negative commandment of "Do not rob the poor" is like robbery in the simple sense: a thing that the owner does not mind [if others use] does not involve robbery. Similarly, regarding words of Torah, the prohibition is only when the author is particular that it be said in his name. But when he is not particular, the negative commandment of "Do not rob the poor" does not apply. Therefore, it is understood that Rabbi Eliezer did not transgress this, because his teachers were not particular about this.

But in addition to the difficulty in the matter itself—for seemingly the teacher should have to explicitly inform [the student] that he is not particular, and it is not enough that the student knows this through logic [and furthermore, seemingly, in order to nullify the obligation to say a thing in the name of its author out of respect for one's teacher, it is not enough that the teacher merely not be particular; there must be a formal waiver]—this only nullifies the prohibition. It is obvious that this is not *mehudar* (beautified) and not *lifnim mishuras hadin* (beyond the letter of the law). On the contrary, one performs a mitzvah if he says it in his name, for "Do not withhold good from its owners."

4. The explanation for this, it seems to me, is as follows:
Even though Rabbi Yochanan ben Zakkai said of his student Rabbi Eliezer that he was a "cistern that does not lose a drop," it is understood that Rabbi Eliezer did not suffice with knowing the Torah of his teacher. Rather, he certainly labored and deepened his understanding of the matters until they were internalized in his intellect and comprehension, in such a way that he was convinced and understood the matters and they became his own intellect. If so, what he said afterwards—this thing—is not a matter of merely repeating the words of his teacher, but rather that this thing was necessitated by his own intellect.

[This is similar to what is explained in many places regarding the Tannaim and Amoraim, that their halachic rulings were according to the root of their souls. As in the dispute between Beis Shammai and Beis Hillel: Beis Shammai, whose root is from the aspect of *Gevuros*, are stringent, and Beis Hillel, whose root is from the aspect of *Chasadim*, are lenient.—Seemingly, "It is not in heaven," so how is it possible to rule according to the root of their souls?—Rather, their ruling was after contemplation and labor in their intellects as they are below, souls specifically in bodies. That is, they did not suffice with what was received by them from the side of the root of their souls, but labored in the understanding and comprehension of the matter until they understood it thus in their intellect below in the body.]

This matter—that a word of Torah that is internalized with one's intellect is considered his own—we find also in *halacha*. As it is stated in the Gemara that "a teacher who waives his honor, his honor is waived, for it is written, 'And Hashem went before them by day...' How is this? There, the Holy One, Blessed be He, the world is His and the Torah is His, so He waives His honor. Here, the Torah is His (in astonishment; the honor depends on the Torah and he cannot waive the honor of the Torah, which is of the Holy One, Blessed be He. Rashi). Rava then said: Yes, the Torah is his, as it is written, 'And in His Torah he meditates day and night.'" And Rashi explains: "For if he desires the Torah of Hashem and meditates in *his* Torah; at first it is called the Torah of Hashem, and after he learns it and reviews it, it is called *his* Torah." Thus, the labor of Torah is in such a way that a person has ownership over the Torah, to the point that he can waive the honor of the Torah.

According to this, the conduct of Rabbi Eliezer the Great in our case is understood: it does not involve the matter of "Do not rob the poor," because he became the owner of the Torah that he learned.

From now on, it is understood why my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, did not mention in his explanation of the words of the Zohar the prohibition of "Do not rob the poor," but only the matter of "brings redemption to the world"—because regarding Rabbi Chiya, the matter of "Do not rob the poor" does not apply [as is proven from this itself, that Rabbi Chiya did not mention the name of the author of the teaching until Rabbi Yosi asked him, "From whom did you hear it?"]. This is because he labored in the matter until he understood the thing in his intellect and became internalized with it, as above.

[And similarly regarding what my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, brought: "What we find in Chullin 104, Kerisus 23, and Niddah 19, that they taught in the Mishnah the opinion of the first Tanna and afterwards the opinion of Rabbi Yosi, and the Gemara there asks, 'Rabbi Yosi is the first Tanna?' and answers, 'This comes to teach us: Who is the first Tanna? Rabbi Yosi. Whoever says a thing in the name of its author brings redemption to the world, as it is written, "And Esther told the king in the name of Mordechai."'... And see Rashi in Chullin there, that the Tanna forgot and did not mention his name at first, and then went back and mentioned his name"—and my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, explained that from the fact itself that the Tanna forgot and did not mention his name at first, and then went back and mentioned his name, it is understood that there was no obligation to mention his name—and it is understood that *all the more so* there was no prohibition of robbery here. And this is as above, because he labored until he understood the matter in his intellect.]

5. Now, it is understood that the matter of "whoever says a thing in the name of its author" that is stated in the chapter of *Kinyan Torah* (Acquisition of Torah) is not in a place where there is a negative commandment, but is a matter of the quality of *chassidus* (piety), like the other matters in *Pirkei Avos*.

That is, it is not speaking of a tradition or a halacha (and all the more so, teachings, interpretations, and logical deductions) that he received and did not internalize in his mind and intellect—for in such a case he is obligated in the matter. Rather, it is speaking of a case where, openly, this is a thing that has been internalized in his intellect, and the reason he says this thing is not because he received it from his teacher, but it is a matter unique to his intellect specifically, to the point that it is entirely his own learning, interpretation, and logic.
