Even in this, there is a special quality (as a matter of *chassidus*) when he says the thing in the name of its author.

And it must be understood why, in such a case where this thing has been verified in his intellect, there is in it the aspect of "whoever says a thing in the name of its author brings redemption to the world," since this statement is not because he heard the thing from the author, but because he understood it thus in his intellect.

On the other hand, since in this case there is the matter of "brings redemption to the world," what is the explanation for this: that when the speaker did not say it to him, but he heard it when the other person said it to someone else, in that case the *segulah* of "brings redemption to the world" does not apply (as in section 1 above)?

6. This can be understood by prefacing the explanation of the matter of "whoever says a thing in the name of its author," which was brought initially in Maseches Avos in the *beraisa* of *Kinyan Torah*. It is the last of the forty-eight things through which Torah is acquired, and afterwards it concludes, "From this you have learned: whoever says a thing in the name of its author brings redemption to the world, as it is written, 'And Esther told the king in the name of Mordechai.'"

From this it is understood that even though it is a matter of the quality of *chassidus*, like the other matters in *Pirkei Avos*, and not an absolute obligation, nevertheless it touches upon the acquisition of Torah. And furthermore, from the fact that this thing is counted at the end of the forty-eight things (which are counted from the easiest to the most difficult), it implies that in the matters of *chassidus* of the acquisition of Torah itself, it is at a high level and degree (and afterwards it adds another quality, that it brings redemption to the world).

The explanation of the matter:
Just as it is in the Torah as a whole, that the learner must know the tradition of who received from whom, as the Tanna arranged at the beginning of Maseches Avos, and as the Rambam elaborated in his introduction to *Sefer HaYad* regarding the forty generations of the transmission of the Torah, so too it is in every specific matter in *halacha* and the logic of *halacha*. Even things that he innovated himself and the like, when one says from which *Beis Medrash* this innovation came, there is in this a strengthening of the matter of the "tradition" that is in the Torah.

And this is not only in things that came from a tradition or a *halacha l'Moshe miSinai* and the like, in which there is no innovation in saying from whom he received this tradition, but also in things that depend on logic, that a veteran student innovated through one of the hermeneutical rules by which the Torah is expounded, or through logic in the depth of the reasons for the *halachos*. The foundation and "way of learning" he received from his teacher (and the like), and therefore there is a matter to mention the name of his teacher.

And this is the inner meaning of what it concludes, "From this you have learned: whoever says a thing in the name of its author brings redemption to the world" (for seemingly, "redemption to the world"—what is it doing here?)—that the quality in this, that he says even these things in the name of their author, is this: that through this he reveals (redeems) the concealment of all the matters of the Torah, that the root and truth of all the details of the *halachos*, interpretations, and logical deductions that were innovated by each and every one is in what was said to Moses at Sinai.

That is, in addition to the knowledge of the Torah in general, that he knows its tradition up to Moses at Sinai, he redeems every detail of the words of Torah by revealing the first author, who is Moses our teacher from the mouth of the Almighty.

7. And according to all this, perhaps one can explain the words of my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe: "According to the simple meaning, one could say that when one is required to say a thing in the name of its author is when the author said it to this person, but if he did not say it to him, then he is not obligated to say it in the name of its author":

In the definition of the Torah, there are two things: (a) transmission from generation to generation, (b) what Israel adds and innovates in the Torah. Like the Tannaim, for whom "many matters were innovated in the Oral Torah and they only supported them with a verse," to the point that there are "the decrees, the enactments, and the customs" that come entirely through the Sages, to the point that they became Torah specifically through Israel. For only things that spread among Israel and the majority of the public can uphold become part of the Torah.

And this thing is from the side of the very definition of the Torah, because since the Torah is not in heaven but was given to the ownership of Israel, and the ruling of the laws of the Torah is according to what appears in their intellect, to the point that the Holy One, Blessed be He, smiles and says, "My children have defeated Me, My children have defeated Me," therefore it is from the definition of the Torah that there are such matters that came in the manner of innovation by the Sages of Israel.

And these two manners come also in the hearing of words of Torah from one's teacher: (a) a student whose teacher learns with him, (b) one who hears a thing that was not said to him, which is as if the matter became known to him by himself. And these are the two manners that are in the definition of the Torah—as it comes in a tradition from one to another, and as it comes by itself, since the fact that he knows this matter in the Torah is not because his teacher or his friend said it to him, but he received it without the matter being transmitted to him specifically.

And on this depends the definition of saying a "thing in the name of its author": The obligation to say a thing in the name of its author emphasizes (as above) the matter of the transmission of all the matters of the Torah from generation to generation, and this thing is when the author said it to him specifically (even when afterwards the thing was verified in the vessel of his intellect). And the manner in which he is not obligated to say it in the name of its author (even as a matter of *chassidus*)—when he heard the thing without the author speaking to him—comes to teach us this definition that is in the Torah, which comes from the side of Israel who add and innovate in the Torah (not through transmission from generation to generation).

8. And according to this, one can explain what my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, wrote in the continuation of his words, that the reason for this, that Rabbi Yosi asked him "From whom did you hear it?" is because "Rabbi Yosi specifically is particular that they say it in the name of the author... even if the author did not say it to him, but he only heard from the author in the way that it was with Rabbi Chiya here, that Rav Hamnuna did not say it to Rabbi Chiya but to Rabbi Acha, and Rabbi Chiya only heard [and furthermore, that "in this exposition, Rabbi Chiya understood one thing from another... Rabbi Chiya innovated them himself," as my revered father-in-law, the Rebbe, wrote here]—even so, he also must say it in the name of the author, and therefore he asked him, 'From whom did you hear it?'"

He explains that "Rabbi Yosi, his level is like the level of Esther, for Esther is the level of *Malchus* (Kingship), and so too Rabbi Yosi is the level of *Malchus*."

The explanation of the matter is that the *Sefirah* of *Malchus* is the end of all levels, which has nothing of its own. Therefore, in it, it is emphasized that even the innovations that come from the side of Israel are from the Holy One, Blessed be He, and on the contrary, from a higher level, from the side that Israel and the Holy One, Blessed be He, are entirely one.

And for this reason itself, the story comes regarding Rabbi Yosi, that initially he did not tell him from whom he heard it, but only after Rabbi Yosi asked him, "From whom did you hear it?"

[And similarly regarding the Mishnah in Chullin, Kerisus, and Niddah, that they taught in the Mishnah the opinion of the first Tanna and afterwards the opinion of Rabbi Yosi, that the Tanna did not mention his name at first and went back and mentioned his name, this comes to teach us: Who is the first Tanna? Rabbi Yosi.]

Because from the side of the level of revelation as it is arranged according to the *Seder Hishtalshelus* (order of progression) of the Torah (both in the revealed Torah and in the inner Torah in the Zohar), there is not the matter of tradition here, but its matter is entirely from the side of the innovation of Israel. And only from the side of Rabbi Yosi, the level of *Malchus*, is it felt as it is from the side of the *Atzmus* (Essence), that even the innovation that is from the side of the recipient, the power of the innovation itself is from the Holy One, Blessed be He, as above.

(From the *Sichos* of *Shabbos Parshas Tisa* 5740, *Shabbos Parshas Balak* 5743)


## ויקהל

## 187

### Vayakhel

A. The Rambam writes in Hilchos Shabbos: "One who kindles [a fire], even a small amount, is liable, provided he needs the ash. But if he kindles it by way of destruction, he is exempt, because he is a 'destroyer.' However, one who kindles his neighbor's grain stack, etc., is liable, even though he is destroying, because his intent is to take revenge on his enemy, and his mind is thus settled, etc.—they are considered 'repairs' in the eyes of the Evil Inclination. Similarly, one who lights a lamp or wood, whether to warm himself or to provide light, is liable."

It is well known that the Alter Rebbe’s position (regarding the Rambam’s view) on the definition of the work of *mavir* (kindling) is that "the essence of *hav'arah* is the bringing forth of fire or the increasing of fire." He explains that "even though one who kindles is not liable unless he needs the ash, the primary liability is not because of the burning and consumption of the wood, but because of the increase of fire". He proves this from what the Rambam writes (at the end of this *halacha*) regarding heating iron in fire ["One who heats iron in order to temper it in water is a *toldah* of *mavir* and is liable"], and the *Maggid Mishneh* there notes that "anything that itself becomes fire, etc., is *mavir*"—even though in the heating of iron there is no consumption [of the material]. The Alter Rebbe continues that this is also implied by the ruling (cited by the Rambam here) that "one who lights a lamp, even if he does not need the ash, is liable".

In *Tzafnas Paneach*, he brings further proof that the Rambam’s position is that the "definition of *hav'arah*" is not "that which causes the combustible material to be consumed," but rather "the existence of fire." This is proven from the fact that we hold (as the Rambam rules here) that one who kindles even a small amount is liable; for "if it were because of the combustible material, a specific measure would be required for each [type of material]."

Based on this, the language of the Rambam and the order of his words in this *halacha* are difficult to understand: at the beginning (as the primary definition of the work), he cites *hav'arah* for the sake of the ash, and only at the end does he cite *hav'arah* for the sake of the fire itself ("to warm himself" or "to provide light").

According to the reasoning that the work of *mavir* is defined as "the burning and consumption of wood," it makes sense that he cites the example of "needing the ash," because this need relates to the essence of the work (the burning and consumption of the wood), since the ash is created from the consumption of the wood. Only afterward is there room to add that even when one does not need the ash (which is created from the consumption of the wood), but rather the warmth and light (the fire), one is also liable for *mavir*.

But since the Rambam’s position is that the definition of the work of *mavir* is "the bringing forth of fire," he should have started with the example he brings later: "one who lights a lamp... whether to warm himself or to..."

## 188

"...provide light," as warmth and light are needs of the fire itself. Why did he start with the example of "needing the ash," where the ash is created through the "consumption and burning of the wood"?

B. Simply put, one could say:
What the Rambam says—that one is liable for *mavir* only when he "needs the ash," or similarly "whether to warm himself or to provide light"—is only (as is explicit in the Rambam here) to ensure that it is not an act of destruction (and not because otherwise it would be a *melacha she'einah tzricha legufah*, for according to the Rambam, one is liable even for a *melacha she'einah tzricha legufah*).

Based on this, we can explain why he began with *hav'arah* in a manner where "he needs the ash": because in this way, it is more strongly emphasized that there is no element of destruction or ruin here, even from the perspective of the act of burning and consuming the wood. Since he needs the ash, it turns out that this is not an act of destruction and consumption (the nullification of the existence of the wood), but an act of repair, as he creates (and transforms) the wood into ash (which he needs). This is in contrast to one who lights a lamp "whether to warm himself or to provide light"; even though the person’s intent is for the repair accomplished by the fire (warmth or light), there is still an aspect of destruction in this act—the destruction of the [wick/oil] material for the sake of the repair accomplished by the fire.

However, this is not a sufficient explanation, because ultimately, since "the primary liability... is because of the increase of fire," he should have begun the description of the work of *mavir* in a manner that corresponds to the essence of the work, where the person performs the work for the sake of the intent of the work itself (the bringing forth of fire), meaning that he needs the fire itself (for the warmth of the fire or the light of the fire).

[It should be added and noted: The primary need and necessity to rule out that *mavir* is not destruction applies to the reasoning that the work of *mavir* is "the burning and consumption of wood." According to that view, the work of *mavir* is essentially an act of destruction (burning and consumption), and it is understandable that one must emphasize, right at the beginning of the description of the work, that one is liable for it only when it is not (merely) destruction and ruin. But since, according to the Rambam, "the primary liability is not because of the burning and consumption of the wood, but because of the increase of fire," then the content of the work of *mavir* is not a matter of destruction (burning and consumption) but a matter of repair: "the bringing forth of fire or the increasing of fire."]

From this it is understood that even according to what the Alter Rebbe says—that "the essence of *hav'arah* is the bringing forth of fire or the increasing of fire"—the primary definition of the work of *mavir* is when one "needs the ash." Therefore, the Rambam begins in this manner, and continues: "Similarly, one who lights a lamp... whether to warm himself or to provide light, is liable," for even though in this case he does not need the ash, nevertheless, "he is liable."

C. This can be explained (in the inner dimension of things). By way of introduction: the thirty-nine *melachos* forbidden on Shabbos are the works that were performed in the *Mishkan*. From this itself it is understood that even though the thirty-nine *melachos* of Shabbos are weekday labors (which is why they are forbidden on the holy day of Shabbos), at their root and source in *kedushah* (as is known, that everything in the world has its root and source in *kedushah*), they are the general and essential labors of a Jew in his service to his Creator: to make the world a dwelling place and a *Mishkan* for Hashem, "And they shall make Me a sanctuary, and I will dwell among them".

The same applies to the details of the conditions and definitions of the thirty-nine *melachos* of Shabbos that determine what constitutes a (*av*) *melacha* for which one is liable: their foundation and essence are as they are labors in the service of man to his Creator. The service is not considered a complete service without these conditions and definitions; it is from this that it subsequently descends to the matter of the *melacha* of Shabbos, where without these conditions and definitions, it is not a *melacha* for which one is liable on Shabbos.

Based on this, we can say regarding our subject that the inner reason why the Rambam established at the beginning of the work of *mavir* the mode of *mavir* when one "needs the ash" (even though "the primary liability... is because of the increase of fire") is because the essence of the work of *mavir* in spiritual service requires that "he needs the ash."

D. Explanation of the matter:
It is written, "The soul of man is the lamp of Hashem." The work of *mavir* in the service of man refers to the lighting of the "lamp" of the *neshamah*, so that it burns with love like the flames of fire for Hashem.

It is explained in *Tanya* that just as the light of a lamp "flickers constantly upward by its nature, because the light of the fire desires by nature to separate from the wick and cleave to its source above... even though by doing so it will be extinguished and will not illuminate anything below, and also above in its source its light will be nullified in existence in its source... so too the soul of man... desires and yearns by its nature to separate and depart from the body and cleave to its source and origin in Hashem." This means that the essence of the "fire" of the *neshamah* is the flames of love for Hashem that cause a person to ascend on high in *kelos hanefesh* (longing of the soul) for Hashem.

This is also the inner explanation for why "one who kindles (even) a small amount" is liable—that the work of *mavir* has no measure (in quantity). This is because the essence of the work of *mavir* in the service of Hashem is the movement of *mesiras nefesh* to Hashem, for this desire "to separate and depart from the body and cleave to its source and origin... even if it becomes nothing and naught and is completely nullified there in existence, and nothing remains of its original essence and being", has its source in the point of Jewishness, which is "a part of Elokus from above, literally". Therefore, quantity is not important here, for it is a simple point that is above division; even in a "small amount," the entire (quality of the) point of the soul is present.

And here the Rambam adds that along with this, there must be the condition: "provided he needs the ash."

"Ash" is the most physical and material part of the thing burned in fire. In the words of the Alter Rebbe, when one burns wood, the three elements of fire, air, and water that are in the wood "have passed and gone and been consumed in smoke." In contrast, the ash that remains is "the earth within it, which descends below and the fire does not rule over it; it is what remains and exists... it is more material than all of them."

This is the condition in the work of *mavir*: "provided he needs the ash." The purpose of kindling the fire of the *neshamah* must not be for the ascent (the fire) alone—"to separate and depart from the body"—but for the *hemshech* (drawing down). "He needs the ash"—that a person should feel that he needs the physicality of the world, meaning that he needs to remain a soul within a body and engage with the physicality of the world to refine and purify it, etc. On the contrary, this is the purpose of kindling the fire of the *neshamah*: that the fact that he is aroused with the flames of the fire of love to the point of a feeling of *kelos hanefesh* and a desire to cleave to his source and origin is only so that from this he will come to an added strengthening in the service of fulfilling Torah and *mitzvos* while being a soul within a body in this physical world.

This is because the entire matter of the work of the *Mishkan*—which is the source of the thirty-nine *melachos*—is to complete the intent of the creation of the world, for the Holy One, Blessed be He, desired to have a dwelling place in the lower realms. The same is true regarding the private *Mishkan* within each person (as the Sages say on the verse, "And I will dwell among them—within each and every one of Israel"): the essence of the work of the *Mishkan* is what a person must do to make the world a dwelling place and a *Mishkan* for Him, may He be blessed. Therefore, the work of *mavir* is not complete until "he needs the ash," so that it is felt that the intent of kindling the Divine fire in his *neshamah* is for the sake of the "earth"—making a dwelling place for Him, may He be blessed, in the lower realms.

E. And more deeply:
The meaning is not that the kindling of the fire (*kelos hanefesh* of the *neshamah*) and "needing the ash" are two opposites. Rather, since the ultimate intent is to make this world a dwelling place for Him, may He be blessed, therefore the purpose of the arousal of the fire of love must be for the sake of the "ash"—the refining and purifying of the physicality of the world. Moreover, the condition of "needing the ash" is the test that the kindling of the fire of the *neshamah* is as it should be. For when the arousal of the flames of the fire of love for Hashem is complete, then the fire of the *neshamah* itself necessitates that "he needs the ash."

The explanation for this:
Since the matter of a "dwelling place in the lower realms"—making this world a dwelling place and a *Mishkan* for Him, may He be blessed—is the intent of the *Atzmus* (the Essence), what He, may He be blessed, "desired," it is understood that the more a person ascends on high and draws closer to his source and origin "in Hashem, the Life of Life, blessed be He", the more he feels the necessity and the obligation to descend below and engage in the refining and purifying of the world, to make it a dwelling place and a *Mishkan* for Him, may He be blessed, since only through this is the intent of the *Atzmus* fulfilled.

It turns out that the kindling of the fire of the *neshamah* is a *melacha* and service in its completeness—where the soul’s desire to cleave to Him, may He be blessed, is complete and it is a true ascent—only when it leads to a drawing down below: "(he needs) ash".

(From the *sichos* of *Shabbos Parshas Yisro* 5749, *Shabbos Parshas Vayakhel* 5714)


## ויקהל — פקודי

## 192

### Vayakhel — Pekudei

A. The Ramban writes in his commentary on the Torah (beginning of Parshas Terumah): "The primary purpose of the Mishkan is to be a place for the resting of the Shechinah, which is the Aron, as it is said, 'And I will meet with you there, and I will speak with you from above the Kapores.' Therefore, he prioritized the Aron and the Kapores here (in Parshas Terumah), for it is first in importance, etc. However, Moshe prioritized the Mishkan, its tent, and its covering in Parshas Vayakhel, and Betzalel did the same, because that is what is appropriate to prioritize in the actual construction."

Seemingly, one could explain his words as follows: According to the Ramban, Hashem’s command to Moshe regarding the construction of the Mishkan (in Parshas Terumah) occurred before the sin of the Golden Calf, and Moshe’s command to the Jewish people (in Parshas Vayakhel) occurred after the sin and its atonement, on the day after Yom Kippur. Given this, one might have interpreted that in Hashem’s command to Moshe, the Aron and Kapores were prioritized because, before the sin of the Golden Calf, the primary intent of building the Mishkan was for the resting of the Shechinah, which is the matter of the Aron and Kapores. But afterwards, when they sinned with the Calf, the content of the Mishkan was for the sake of atonement for the sin of the Calf, which is primarily achieved through the avodah in the Mishkan—the offering of sacrifices. Therefore, one could say that this is why Moshe did not prioritize the Aron and Kapores in Parshas Vayakhel, to indicate that from now on, the primary purpose of the Mishkan was not the resting of the Shechinah, but rather the atonement for sins, etc.

To refute this notion, he explains that the reason for the change—that "Moshe prioritized the Mishkan, etc., in Parshas Vayakhel, and Betzalel did the same"—is "because that is what is appropriate to prioritize in the actual construction." From this, it is understood that even after the sin of the Golden Calf, the primary matter of the Mishkan remained its being "a place for the resting of the Shechinah, which is the Aron."

This is also understood from the Ramban’s words at the beginning of our Parshah (Parshas Vayakhel), that this command "may have been on the day after he descended, and he told everyone about the matter of the Mishkan as he had been commanded initially before the breaking of the Luchos. Since Hashem was reconciled with them and gave him the second Luchos, etc., they returned to their original state and to the love of their betrothal, and it is known that His Shechinah would be among them, as He had commanded him initially, as it is said, 'And they shall make Me a sanctuary, and I will dwell among them.' Therefore, Moshe now commanded them everything he had been commanded initially."

However, according to the Rambam, the primary matter of the Mishkan and the essence of its mitzvah is as he writes at the beginning of Hilchos Beis HaBechirah : "It is a positive commandment to build a house for Hashem, prepared for offering sacrifices within it and celebrating...

## 193

...before Him three times a year, as it is said, 'And they shall make Me a sanctuary'".

B. In the continuation of his words at the beginning of Parshas Terumah, the Ramban explains the matter of the resting of the Shechinah in the Mishkan: "The secret of the Mishkan is that the Glory which rested upon Mount Sinai should rest upon it in a hidden manner, just as it is said there, 'And the Glory of Hashem rested upon Mount Sinai,' and it is written, 'Behold, Hashem our G-d has shown us His Glory and His greatness.' Similarly, it is written regarding the Mishkan, 'And the Glory of Hashem filled the Mishkan,' and he mentions in the Mishkan twice, 'And the Glory of Hashem filled the Mishkan,' corresponding to 'His Glory and His greatness.'" And at the end of our Parshah (Pekudei), he writes: "And the Glory of Hashem filled the Mishkan, for its interior was filled with the Glory, for the Glory rests within the cloud inside the Mishkan, as it is said regarding Mount Sinai, 'To the thick darkness where G-d was.'"

It is explained in the seforim that his intention here is to explain the definition of the resting of the Shechinah in the Mishkan and also in the Beis HaMikdash (as he also brings there several verses regarding the Beis HaMikdash)—that there are two opinions regarding this:

According to the Rambam, one cannot say that the resting of the Shechinah was in the place of the Mikdash itself, for Hashem is not within the category of place. Rather, the place of the Mikdash is qualified so that the revelation of Hashem passes through it [analogous to the fact that prophecy only rests in Eretz Yisrael, for prophecy is a perception in the category of spiritual vision, where the thing seen is not in the category of place, and yet this vision is not revealed to the prophet except in Eretz Yisrael alone]. This is similar to the writing of intellect by the fingers of the hand, where the intellect does not shine and rest in the hand—for the fingers of the hand have no connection to the intellect—but rather the hand is only a medium through which the intellect passes and is drawn. And even though it is possible to convey the intellect only through the fingers of the hand and not through the toes of the foot, as only the hand is qualified for this, nevertheless, the hand itself does not grasp the intellect.

The opinion of the Ramban is that the resting of the Shechinah in the Beis HaMikdash was in the manner of *hislabshus* (enclothement), that the revelation was in the place of the Mikdash itself, analogous to the dwelling of the intellect in the brain, where the intellect is enclothed in the brain and the brain grasps the intellect.

That is to say, according to the Rambam, the resting of the Shechinah in the Beis HaMikdash was by way of passage and does not pertain to the place of the Mikdash itself, while according to the Ramban, it was in the manner of *hislabshus* in the place of the Mikdash.

## 194

C. From the fact that the Ramban explains the matter of the resting of the Shechinah (the Glory) in the manner of *hislabshus* as a continuation of his words that the primary purpose of the Mishkan is the place of the resting of the Shechinah, which is the Aron, it is logical that his position on the definition of the purpose of the Mishkan and his position on the manner of the resting of the Shechinah within it are dependent upon one another. And it is also logical that the Rambam, who disagrees with him on these two matters, has a position on both that is founded upon the same thing.

We must explain the connection between these matters. Simply put:
The Ramban wrote that "the primary purpose of the Mishkan is the place of the resting of the Shechinah (which is the Aron)," because he holds that the resting of the Shechinah in the Mikdash is not by way of passage, but by way of *hislabshus* in the place of the Mikdash itself. Therefore, it is appropriate to say that the primary purpose is the place of the resting of the Shechinah, because this pertains to the body of the place of the Mikdash.

Conversely, the Rambam, who wrote that the primary mitzvah of building the Mishkan is for the offering of sacrifices and for making a pilgrimage to it, follows his own logic: the resting of the Shechinah in the Mikdash is only by way of passage, as it does not rest in the place of the Mikdash itself; rather, the place of the Mikdash is qualified so that through it, the revelation of the Shechinah will be for the Jewish people who come there, and through it, the speech and prophecy pass to Moshe, as it is written, "And I will meet with you there, and I will speak with you from above the Kapores."

However, this is not a sufficient explanation, for even according to the Ramban, the resting of the Shechinah in the Mikdash is not due to the place of the Mikdash itself, but rather for the sake of the Jewish people, as the Ramban elaborated : that after the Giving of the Torah, "the Jewish people accepted upon themselves to do everything He commanded them, etc. From now on, they are a people to Him, etc., a holy nation, and they are holy, worthy that a sanctuary should be among them to rest His Shechinah among them. Therefore, He commanded first regarding the matter of the Mishkan, that He should have a house among them, sanctified to His Name, and there He would speak with Moshe and command the Children of Israel" (and he continues, "And the primary purpose of the Mishkan is the place of the resting of the Shechinah, which is the Aron, etc.").

It turns out that even according to the Ramban, the primary resting of the Shechinah in the Mikdash is only for the sake of the Jewish people. Rather, their dispute is that according to the Rambam, the primary matter of the Mikdash is for the sake of the avodah of the Jewish people (offering sacrifices, etc.), whereas according to the Ramban, its primary matter is for the sake of the resting of the Shechinah among the Jewish people.

Furthermore, it has been explained elsewhere regarding the dispute between the Rambam and the Ramban concerning the primary purpose of the Mikdash, that one says one thing and the other says another, and they do not disagree; for the verse explicitly states, "And they shall make Me a sanctuary, and I will dwell among them." However, the Rambam, in his book of law (and also in the Sefer HaMitzvos)—where the definition of law is the command to the person—speaks of the content of the mitzvos that the Jewish people were commanded to perform in the Beis HaMikdash. The Ramban speaks of the matter and purpose that was in the Mishkan itself, which is "and I will dwell among them," the resting of the Shechinah that was in it from Above, and this was primarily through the Aron.

Based on the above, that the two disputes are dependent upon one another, it is logical to say that also regarding the second dispute—whether the resting of the Shechinah was by way of passage or by way of *hislabshus*—one says one thing and the other says another, and they do not disagree.

D. The explanation for this can be given by prefacing it with what is explained in the Zohar, that the connection between the Jewish people and the Holy One, blessed be He, is that the revealed aspect of the Jewish people connects with the revealed aspect of the Holy One, blessed be He, and the hidden aspect of the Jewish people connects with the hidden aspect of the Holy One, blessed be He.

It is the same regarding "And they shall make Me a sanctuary, and I will dwell among them," which contains these two levels: 1) "And I will dwell among them"—in the revealed aspect of the Jewish people, which is the connection between the revealed aspect of the Jewish people and the revealed aspect of the Holy One, blessed be He. 2) "And I will dwell among them"—the hidden aspect of the Holy One, blessed be He, in the hidden aspect of the Jewish people.

The difference between them is: The revealed connection between the revealed aspect of the Jewish people and the revealed aspect of the Holy One, blessed be He, is something that is accomplished through the action and avodah of the Jewish people—"And they shall make Me a sanctuary," in the language of the Rambam, "for the avodah within it, there shall be the offering and the burning of the fire continually, and to it shall be the pilgrimage and the ascent for the festival, and the gathering every year, and this is what He, blessed be He, said, 'And they shall make Me a sanctuary.'" Whereas the hidden connection between the hidden aspect of the Holy One, blessed be He, and the hidden aspect of the Jewish people is accomplished by the Mishkan and the Mikdash itself. That is to say, even one who did not come to the Mikdash to offer a sacrifice and did not make a pilgrimage—the very existence of the Mishkan and the Mikdash within the Children of Israel brings about "and I will dwell among them," within the Children of Israel.

It can be said that these are the two definitions mentioned above regarding the resting of the Shechinah in the Mishkan: The Rambam deals with the matter of "And they shall make Me a sanctuary, and I will dwell among them" as it is in the revealed, meaning the connection between the Jewish people and the Holy One, blessed be He, that comes through their avodah, the offering of sacrifices, and the pilgrimage. The resting of the Shechinah (and I will dwell among them) that is connected to this is in the category of revelation, for it comes through the deeds and actions of the Jewish people—and this is the connection of the revealed aspect of the Jewish people with the revealed aspect of the Holy One, blessed be He.

Whereas the Ramban, who includes in his commentary "pleasant words for those who hear and those who know grace", who explains the secrets of the Torah, deals with "And they shall make Me a sanctuary, and I will dwell among them" in the category of the hidden—the connection between the hidden aspect of the Jewish people and the hidden aspect of the Holy One, blessed be He. This connection is not accomplished through the deeds and actions of the Jewish people, but through the very establishment of the Mishkan and the Mikdash within the Children of Israel.

It can be said that these two details are connected to two different places in the Mishkan itself: The revealed connection pertains to the revealed place in the Mikdash, the place of avodah—the *Azarah* (courtyard) and the *Heichal* (sanctuary). The hidden connection pertains to the hidden place in the Mikdash, the *Kodesh HaKodashim* (Holy of Holies), which is not a place of avodah and no one enters there all year long (only the Kohen Gadol on Yom Kippur). And within that itself—in the Aron, in which there was no avodah, and its entire purpose is to be the place of the resting of the Shechinah.

E. Based on the above, we can explain another detail according to the opinion of the Ramban regarding the Second Temple, which seemingly requires study: Since there was no Aron in it, was the primary purpose of the Mikdash—the place of the resting of the Shechinah, which is the Aron—missing?

Furthermore, our Sages of blessed memory said that what is said, "The glory of this latter house shall be greater than the former," means that the Second Beis HaMikdash was greater in construction and in years than the First. This is difficult to understand, because the Second Temple lacked five things, and foremost among them was the lack of the Aron, which is the "primary purpose" of the Mikdash. How can one compare the Second Temple to the First in the category of the Mikdash, whose matter is "and I will dwell among them," and all the more so to say that it is greater than it in construction and years? What value does the duration of the house in years and construction have compared to the essence of the Beis HaMikdash, which is "the place of the resting of the Shechinah, which is the Aron"?

Rather, the matter of "the resting of the Shechinah, which is the Aron," its primary definition is its being hidden and concealed, which points to the hidden connection between the Jewish people and the Holy One, blessed be He, and not the revealed connection accomplished through their avodah in the revealed. Therefore, in the Second Temple, even though the Aron was not there in a revealed manner in the Kodesh HaKodashim, nevertheless, it was in a manner of being hidden within the Kodesh HaKodashim itself [analogous to what is in the Gemara according to one opinion, "The Aron was hidden in its place," and as it is said, "And they were there until this day," and as the Rambam elaborated, "At the time that Shlomo built the house and knew that it would eventually be destroyed, he built a place to hide the Aron within it, below in deep and winding subterranean chambers, and King Yoshiyahu commanded and hid it in the place that Shlomo built"].

It turns out that the matter of "and I will dwell among them," the resting of the Shechinah that pertains to the hidden aspect of the Jewish people, was not only not lacking in the Second Temple, but was in a higher state. For the very fact that the Aron was in a manner of being hidden in the Kodesh HaKodashim points to a more hidden and concealed level in the resting of the Shechinah, and consequently to a more hidden and internal connection between the Jewish people and Hashem.

This matter is connected and dependent upon the level of the Children of Israel during the time of the Second Temple: Since in the revealed, the situation of the Jewish people was then at a lower level in the fulfillment of Torah and mitzvos and the avodah of Hashem in general—and furthermore, not all the Jewish people were on their land, so there was no "coming of all of you"—the connection between the Jewish people and Hashem had to be also at a level and in a state where, in the revealed, it does not appear that they have a connection to the holiness of the Mikdash, even to those who are in the Diaspora.

This is the content of the matter of the hiding of the Aron in the Kodesh HaKodashim, that then the connection between the Jewish people and Hashem was in a more hidden category from the side of the Jewish people, and consequently, it is understood that this reaches a more hidden category Above.

F. Based on this, we can explain more deeply the connection between the Ramban’s position on the primary purpose of the Mikdash and his position on the manner of the resting of the Shechinah, as well as the Rambam’s position on this:

The difference between an influence by way of passage and an influence by way of *hislabshus* is simply : The matter of *hislabshus* means that the thing being enclothed changes according to the vessel in which it is enclothed. Conversely, an influence by way of passage is more exalted, for it is not constricted according to the manner and definition of the place. As in the analogies mentioned above (Section B), where through the intellect being enclothed in the physical brain, a constriction and physicalization occurs within it, whereas in the writing of intellect by the fingers of the hand, the intellect does not change through the fingers. Therefore, the matter of *hislabshus* is possible only in something that is relative.

The reason the Rambam holds that the resting of the Shechinah in the Mikdash was by way of passage is, as mentioned above, because Hashem is above the definition of place, and therefore it is not applicable to say that He is constricted and changed to be within the definition of a physical place. However, this reason is valid only in the category of the revealed aspect of the Holy One, blessed be He, for since He is defined, so to speak, in the category of "revelation," therefore it is applicable to say that He is revealed only in an exalted and spiritual place, and it is not applicable that He be drawn down and constricted in a physical place. But in the category of the hidden aspect of the Holy One, blessed be He, which is not defined by definitions, there is no difference for Him between a spiritual place (the heavens and the heavens of the heavens) and a physical place; He is above a spiritual place just as He is above a physical place. And from this very aspect—that there are no limitations in Him—it is within His power, blessed be He, to be enclothed also in a physical place even without descent and constriction [as it is said, "Who is exalted to dwell, who looks down to see in the heavens and on the earth," that from this very aspect that He is "exalted to dwell," above the definition of descent and lowering, therefore "He looks down to see... (also) on the earth," and He can be drawn down also into the category of place].

Therefore, the Rambam, who speaks in his book about the revealed matters in the Beis HaMikdash, the sacrifices and the avodah within it which is connected to the revealed aspect of the Jewish people, holds that the resting of the Shechinah was by way of passage, and not in the manner of *hislabshus*, because the revealed aspect of the Jewish people is connected with the revealed aspect of the Holy One, blessed be He, and in this category, there is a grasp of place (by way of negation) for a physical place, and therefore it is not applicable to say of Him that He is enclothed in a specific place.

Conversely, the Ramban deals with the category of the hidden aspect of the Holy One, blessed be He, that rests in the Beis HaMikdash, which is connected with the hidden aspect of the Jewish people—meaning a connection that is not accomplished through avodah but a more internal and hidden connection, from the side of the very resting of the Shechinah in the Kodesh HaKodashim. Therefore, he holds that the resting of the Shechinah was in the manner of *hislabshus*, "And the Glory of Hashem filled the Mishkan," "for its interior was filled with the Glory, for the Glory rests within the cloud inside the Mishkan," because the connection of the hidden aspect of the Jewish people, which is above revelation, is with the category of the hidden aspect of the Holy One, blessed be He, in which the matter of *hislabshus* is not a descent and constriction, for He is not defined even by the negation of a physical place. On the contrary—this is an expression of His infinitude, that the true infinitude is in His power to be enclothed also in a physical place.

G. Based on the above, we can explain the precision of the Ramban’s language (at the beginning of Parshas Terumah) mentioned above in his commentary on the verses of our Parshah: "The secret of the Mishkan is that the Glory which rested upon Mount Sinai should rest upon it in a hidden manner." His intention in "rest upon it in a hidden manner" is not only the manner in which it rests in the Mishkan (that it was not in a revealed manner as it was on Mount Sinai), but the level of the Shechinah, which is the category of the hidden aspect of the Shechinah, which is above the level of the Shechinah in the revealed, which was at the Giving of the Torah. It can be said that this is what he continues and explains, that "he mentions in the Mishkan twice, 'And the Glory of Hashem filled the Mishkan,'" that this category of the Shechinah, which is in the category of the hidden, is itself what was drawn down into the Mishkan below, for the Glory of Hashem from Above is the very same Glory of Hashem that filled the Mishkan—internally, in the category of *hislabshus*.

This is the difference between the revelation that was at the time of the Giving of the Torah and the revelation of the Glory of Hashem in the Mishkan and the Mikdash: Even on Mount Sinai it is said, "Face to face Hashem spoke with you," which was drawn down to each one of the Jewish people in his "face"—internally, from the category of "face"—the interiority from Above, and the category of Havayah that is above the *Seder Hishtalshelus* became "your G-d," your power and your life-force of each one of the Jewish people. But at the Giving of the Torah, their souls departed, for it was not in *hislabshus* internally to such an extent. Whereas regarding "And I will dwell among them" in the Mishkan and the Mikdash, it was in the manner of *hislabshus* internally, "and I will dwell" within each one of the Jewish people. And therefore, this very thing acted and aroused in each one that they should come to ascend, to see, and to bow down before Him, in internal *bitul*, above the vessel of his intellect and his *midos*.

This is what the Ramban emphasizes, that the reason for this is because "the Glory which rested upon Mount Sinai rests upon it in a hidden manner," for the level of the Shechinah was in an exalted manner, a more hidden category, and therefore it came and was drawn down in a more internal *hislabshus*.

(From the sichos of Shabbos Parshas Re'eh and Shabbos Parshas Tavo, 5723)


## ויק"פ — פ' החודש

## 199

### Vayakhel-Pekudei — Parshas HaChodesh

A. After the completion of the construction of the First Beis Hamikdash by Shlomo, the Scriptures recount that Shlomo said: "Blessed be Hashem... who spoke with His mouth... saying, 'From the day that I brought My people, Israel, out of Egypt, I did not choose a city from any of the tribes of Israel to build a house for My name to be there, but I chose David to be over My people Israel.'" Regarding the explanation of why the verse begins with the choice of Jerusalem ("I did not choose a city...") and concludes with the choice of David, the Radak writes: "'But I chose David'—why did he preface it with 'I did not choose a city'? He should have said, 'But I chose Jerusalem.' Rather, the explanation is that I did not make My choice of Jerusalem known until I chose David to be over My people Israel, and I showed him the chosen place in Jerusalem when I answered him at the threshing floor of Arvona with fire from heaven."

Seemingly, however, his explanation is insufficient. For the simple meaning of the verse, "But I chose David to be over My people Israel," implies that the choice of David is not merely a means—that through David the "chosen place in Jerusalem" was revealed and made known—but rather that the choice of David as King of Israel is intrinsically connected with the choice of Jerusalem.

[And this is seemingly proven from what is written in Divrei Hayamim (where Shlomo’s words are also cited, but the phrasing there is): "From the day... I did not choose a city... and I did not choose a man to be a leader over My people Israel. But I chose Jerusalem for My name to be there, and I chose David to be over My people Israel"—this explicitly states that the intent of the verse is (also) to emphasize the choice of David as King of Israel, except that here (in the Book of Melachim) it emphasizes an additional detail: that these two choices belong to one another.]

In the Zohar (our Parsha) it is stated: "This verse does not match its beginning with its end, nor its end with its beginning, for it is written, 'I did not choose a city, but I chose David.' What does this have to do with that? It should have said, 'But I chose Jerusalem.' Rather, when the Holy One, blessed be He, has the desire to build a city, He first looks at the leader who will lead the people of the city, and afterwards He builds the city and brings the people into it. This is the meaning of, 'I did not choose a city until I looked at David to be a shepherd over Israel.'"

According to the Zohar, the choice of Jerusalem is dependent upon the choice of David as King of Israel, for before David was chosen as "shepherd over Israel" (King of Israel), the choice of Jerusalem was not complete.

We must examine the explanation of this matter: why Hashem’s choice of Jerusalem is dependent upon His choice of David.

Now, the Zohar there continues, "Because the city and all the inhabitants of the city all exist through the shepherd who leads the people. If the shepherd is good, it is good for him, good for the city, and good for the people. And if the shepherd is evil, etc., woe to the city, etc." This means that the choice of David causes there to be "good for the city" in Jerusalem.

## 200

However, it is difficult, for even if the choice of David were to come after the choice of Jerusalem, his merit could protect Jerusalem so that there would be "good for the city, etc."

Furthermore: from the fact that the verse concludes only regarding the choice of David (and does not mention the choice of Jerusalem at all), it implies that the choice of David is not a secondary matter (or a detail) of the choice of Jerusalem (i.e., that if it lacks a good leader, "woe to the city, etc."), but rather that it touches upon the very essence of the choice of Jerusalem—that the choice of Jerusalem is effected by the choice of David, to the point that the primary element is the choice of David.

B. This can be understood by first adding an explanation regarding the nature of the choice of Jerusalem, which is an eternal choice. As explained in the Rambam’s Commentary on the Mishnah: "He called Jerusalem an inheritance because of the permanence of its holiness and its existence forever. And about it the prophet said, 'And He will not forsake His inheritance,' because it was already said at the beginning of the matter that Hashem chose Jerusalem for His Shechinah and chose Israel for Himself as a treasured people. And he said afterwards that Hashem will not abandon this nation which He chose for His inheritance, nor that place which He chose... And he already explained the eternity of its holiness and said, 'This is My resting place forever'"—from the continuation of his words, it is understood that the eternity of Jerusalem is because of Hashem’s choice of it.

Now, regarding the eternity of Jerusalem, there are two laws:
1) The law regarding the building of the Beis Hamikdash—"Once the Mikdash was built in Jerusalem, all other places were forbidden to build a house for Hashem therein, etc., and there is no house for generations upon generations except in Jerusalem alone... 'This is My resting place forever,'" meaning that for all generations, Jerusalem is the only place in the world to build a "house for Hashem".
2) The law regarding the holiness of Jerusalem, as per the Rambam’s ruling that "the first holiness... sanctified... for their time and sanctified them for the future to come; therefore, we offer all the sacrifices even though there is no house built, etc.," and as mentioned above from the Commentary on the Mishnah, that "its holiness and its existence are forever... He chose Jerusalem for His Shechinah, etc." From these words, it is understood that even the "eternity of its holiness" is a result of the choice of Jerusalem. However, in the *Sefer HaYad* (in the continuation of the matter), he adds a reason for the eternity of the holiness: "Because the holiness of the Mikdash and Jerusalem is on account of the Shechinah, and the Shechinah does not cease."

We must examine the reason why, regarding the holiness of Jerusalem, the Rambam did not cite the verse "This is My resting place forever" (which seemingly is [also] proof that the Shechinah dwells there forever, "forever," just as he cited in the Commentary on the Mishnah there regarding the "eternity of its holiness"), but rather writes a reason and explanation: "Because the holiness of the Mikdash and Jerusalem is on account of the Shechinah, and the Shechinah does not cease."

Also, it is difficult to interpret the reason "because the holiness of the Mikdash and Jerusalem is on account of the Shechinah, and the Shechinah does not cease," for from this it is understood that this is not a decree of the verse (that this is what Hashem decreed, that "this is My resting place forever," which is the place of the Shechinah forever and ever), but rather that it is necessitated by

## 201

logic: just as it is impossible for the Shechinah to cease, so too it is impossible for the holiness of the Mikdash and Jerusalem (which is "on account of the Shechinah") to cease. This is difficult: the holiness of the Mishkan in the desert was also on account of the Shechinah that rested within it, yet the holiness did not remain in the places where the Mishkan was erected. We do not say that since the holiness is "on account of the Shechinah, and the Shechinah does not cease," therefore the holiness of that place also does not cease!

Simply put, one could say that in the Mishkan there is no law of the holiness of the place, since it was not permanent in one location but rather they carried it from place to place. Therefore, the resting of the Shechinah in the Mishkan does not pertain to the place. But it is still difficult regarding the Mishkan of Shiloh, "where they built a house of stones... and it stood for 369 years"—there was a permanence of place there, to the point that "it was a resting place" [as stated in the Commentary on the Mishnah that it is called a resting place "because they settled and there was no traveling there"]; and furthermore—it is explicit in the Sifri on the verse "The place which Hashem will choose"—"this is Shiloh." Thus, there was Hashem’s choice of this place—if so, why does this reason not apply (also) to the Mishkan of Shiloh, that its holiness is "on account of the Shechinah, and the Shechinah does not cease"?

C. The explanation for this is as follows:
Hashem’s choice of a specific place can be in two ways: 1) The choice is in order to come, achieve, and complete a specific goal; in this manner, it is understood that it is possible for the choice to be for a specific time, for when the goal is completed, there is no longer a need for the (choice of the) place. 2) The choice of this place is His will and His choice in essence, from His own perspective.

This is the difference between the choice of Shiloh and the choice of Jerusalem, as emphasized by the difference in the phrasing of the verses regarding Shiloh and Jerusalem—regarding Shiloh it is said, "The place which Hashem will choose... to place His name there," but regarding Jerusalem the phrasing is "which Hashem your G-d will choose in it (and only afterwards does it continue) to cause His name to dwell there." Based on the above, we must explain the reason for this: In Shiloh, the choice of this place was for the goal of "placing His name there." Not so the matter of the choice of Jerusalem, which is that Hashem chose "in it," in this place, and as a result of this, He caused "His name to dwell there." For there is His will and His choice, and automatically His Shechinah is found there (and in a permanent manner, "to cause His name to dwell there"—and not merely "to place," which implies (also) a temporary state).

This is the intent of the Rambam in his words, "the holiness of the Mikdash and Jerusalem is on account of the Shechinah, and the Shechinah does not cease"—he did not come to bring proof for the eternity of the holiness, but to explain that since Hashem’s choice of Jerusalem (and the Mikdash) is in the manner of "Hashem your G-d will choose in it," in it in essence, this place became connected and unified with the Shechinah to the point that this is the permanent place of the Shechinah. Consequently, just as the "Shechinah does not cease," so too the holiness of this place will not cease.

[Not so in Shiloh, for even though it is also the "place which Hashem will choose," one cannot say that this place became the place of the Shechinah in essence, because the choice was for a goal, "to place His name there," a choice dependent upon a condition, and when...]


## פ' החודש

## 206

### Parshas HaChodesh

1. We find in the Yerushalmi: "Rabbi Ba said in the name of Rabbi Chiya bar Ashi: One does not interrupt between Purim and Parah. Rabbi Levi said in the name of Rabbi Chama bar Chanina: One does not interrupt between Parah and HaChodesh. Rabbi Levi said: The sign for these parshiyos is [the law] 'between these cups' [in the four cups of the Pesach Seder, we learned 'between these cups,' meaning between the first and second, and between the second and third. Korban HaEda]—if one wishes to drink, one may drink; but between the third and the fourth, one shall not drink."

This means that Rabbi Levi decides in accordance with the view of Rabbi Chama bar Chanina that "one does not interrupt between Parah and HaChodesh," and as proof and reinforcement, he provides a mnemonic from the law of interruption regarding the four cups of the Pesach Seder, where between the third and fourth cup, "one shall not drink."

One must examine this mnemonic of Rabbi Levi. Even though we find similar instances throughout the Torah (where Chazal provided mnemonics for certain matters for the benefit of memory and the like)—yet [aside from the fact that even in those places there is room to say (since all matters of Torah are precise) that there is some connection in content between the mnemonic and the subject for which it was given], in the present case, where Rabbi Levi used this mnemonic to assist in deciding the halacha (in accordance with the view of Rabbi Chama bar Chanina), it is certainly reasonable to say that there is a connection between the mnemonic and the halachic issue at hand.

It also requires study: In the Yerushalmi there, it continues (after Rabbi Levi's mnemonic): "Rabbi Levi said in the name of Rabbi Chama bar Chanina: It is logical that HaChodesh should precede Parah, for on the first of Nissan the Mishkan was erected, and on the second the Parah was burned. Why then does Parah precede? Because it is the purification of all Israel". The Korban HaEda there explains (and its source is the Ran) that this is the reason for the view of Rabbi Chama bar Chanina that one does not interrupt between Parah and HaChodesh, because "it is not appropriate to interrupt between Parah and HaChodesh, so that it [Parah] should not be so early, since it was appropriate to be later."

But according to this, it is not understood why the Shas interrupted between the two statements of Rabbi Levi in the name of Rabbi Chama bar Chanina—that is, between the law itself and the reason for the law—with the statement of Rabbi Levi (in his own name) regarding the mnemonic that "between the third and fourth, one shall not drink"?

2. This can be explained by first prefacing the reason for the enactment of reading Parshas Parah and Parshas HaChodesh. The reading of Parshas Parah is "to warn Israel to purify themselves so that they may perform their Pesach offerings in purity", and Parshas HaChodesh is read because "the parsha of the Pesach is there" [for just as

## 207

in Egypt Moshe warned Israel regarding the performance of the Pesach on Rosh Chodesh Nissan, so too for generations we read Parshas HaChodesh, "for the parsha of the Pesach is there," in proximity to Rosh Chodesh Nissan (or on Rosh Chodesh Nissan itself)].

Based on this, there is room to define the enactment of reading these two parshiyos in two ways:
a) These two readings are fundamentally one enactment, which is divided into two—meaning that on the Shabbosos between Purim and Pesach, one must read in the Torah two parshiyos regarding the preparations for performing the Pesach: in one, we read about the purity required so that "they may perform their Pesach offerings in purity," and in the other, we read Parshas HaChodesh, "for the parsha of the Pesach is there."
b) These two readings are two separate enactments [similar to the first two parshiyos, Parshas Shekalim and Parshas Zachor, which are two different matters; for the reading of Parshas Shekalim is "to inform them that they should bring their shekalim in Adar", and "Zachor" is "to connect the wiping out of Amalek to the wiping out of Haman"]—that the primary enactment of reading Parshas Parah is to warn about the matter of tumah and taharah (in general), but they enacted to read it at this time (in proximity to Pesach) because this warning is relevant to the performance of the Pesach.

According to this, one can say that Rabbi Levi (in his own name) holds like Rabbi Chama regarding the law itself (that one does not interrupt), but not for his reason, as they disagree whether they are two enactments or one enactment:
The reason of Rabbi Chama bar Chanina that "one does not interrupt between Parah and HaChodesh" is because he holds that fundamentally they are like one reading (enactment), the preparation for the performance of the Pesach, and the reading of Parshas Parah is like an earlier detail (the purification of Israel) in the reading of Parshas HaChodesh (the performance of the Pesach), and therefore it is understood simply that there is no place to interrupt between them.
And this is his intent in his statement, "And why does Parah precede? Because it is the purification of all Israel," which also explains the reason why one does not interrupt between Parshas Parah and HaChodesh, for by placing Parshas Parah before Parshas HaChodesh (because "it is the purification of all Israel"), it is emphasized that the performance of the Pesach (Parshas HaChodesh) is dependent upon "the purification of all Israel" [in the language of the Midrash, "those who eat the Pesach need it (the Parah)"], and from this it is understood that one should not interrupt between them (as above).

But Rabbi Levi does not hold this way, because by providing a mnemonic (for the law of Rabbi Chama) from the law that "between the third and fourth, one shall not drink"—his intent is to define the enactment of the reading, that the enactment of reading all four parshiyos (including Parshas Parah and Parshas HaChodesh) is like the enactment of the four cups:
Just as with the four cups of the Pesach Seder, "each one is a mitzvah in its own right", and "each cup was enacted for a specific matter" [the first cup, one recites Kiddush over it; the second cup, one reads the Haggadah over it; the third cup, one recites Birchas Hamazon over it; the fourth cup, one completes the Hallel over it and recites the blessing of the song]; and the fact that one does not drink between the third and fourth cup is not because there is a connection between them (because of which one should not interrupt), but for a completely different reason—
In the same way, it is with the enactment of reading the four parshiyos, which are four different (enactments and) readings; the reading of Parshas Parah is not part of the enactment of reading Parshas HaChodesh, but is a matter in its own right, and the fact that one does not interrupt between Parah and HaChodesh is due to an additional law.

3. One can explain the view of Rabbi Levi based on what is brought in several poskim as the reason for what is stated in the Mishnah that "on the third Shabbos [we read] the Red Heifer"—"because one inquires about the laws of Pesach thirty days before Pesach, to remind them to perform the Pesach in purity."

Now, this law that thirty days before the festival one inquires and expounds upon the laws of the festival is not only regarding Pesach, but regarding all the festivals, as is explained at length in the Shulchan Aruch of our Rebbe the Alter Rebbe regarding the reason for this enactment: "Because every person living in Eretz Yisrael is obligated to bring three offerings on the festival, etc., and every offering must be clean from any blemish and from other things that invalidate the offering; therefore the Sages enacted to expound the laws of the festival thirty days before it, to remind the people about the festival so they will not forget to prepare animals fit for an offering, and they will have the duration of all thirty days."

Based on this, one can say that the fact that they enacted the reading of Parshas Parah to warn Israel regarding tumah is not only because of the Pesach offering, but because of the purity of the festival in general (which is required for the pilgrimage and the three festival offerings (aside from the Pesach offering)), and this is also understood from what was brought in several poskim regarding the enactment of reading Parshas Parah, that "a person is obligated to purify himself on the festival".

And it appears that this is the reason for Rabbi Ba, who holds that "one does not interrupt between Purim and Parah," because in his view, the reading of Parshas Parah is related to the enactment that one inquires and expounds, etc., thirty days before Pesach (which is a preparation for the purity of the festival in general), and therefore it must be in proximity to Purim specifically (in which these thirty days begin).

Thus, the view of Rabbi Levi, who provided the mnemonic that "between the third and fourth, one shall not drink," is an intermediate view between the position of Rabbi Ba and the position of Rabbi Chama bar Chanina—for in practice, Rabbi Levi decided in accordance with the view of Rabbi Chama bar Chanina that one does not interrupt between Parah and HaChodesh, but not for his reason; for in the view of Rabbi Chama bar Chanina, they are like one enactment divided into two, as above, whereas Rabbi Levi holds that fundamentally they are two enactments [similar to the cups of the Pesach Seder], because Parshas Parah is a warning regarding the purity of the festival in general, only that there is a (further) law that one does not interrupt between Parah and HaChodesh.

[And based on this, it is understood why the Shas inserted the statement of Rabbi Levi (in his own name) immediately after the law of Rabbi Chama bar Chanina, before bringing the reason of Rabbi Chama—because his intent in this is to emphasize that he stands with the position of Rabbi Chama only for the halacha, but he does not hold like his reasoning.]

4. According to the inner dimension of these matters, one can say that the negation of an interruption between Parah and HaChodesh is related to what can be said regarding the inner content of the law that between the third and fourth cup, one shall not drink.

Prefacing this with what is explained in the sefarim, the content of the difference between Parshas Parah and Parshas HaChodesh in the avodah of man is like the difference between the avodah of the tzaddikim and the avodah of teshuvah:
Parshas HaChodesh, "for the parsha of the Pesach is there," points to the avodah of the tzaddikim—and as it was the first time, that in the Pesach of Egypt there was (the beginning of) the conversion of the Jewish people, and a convert who has converted is like a newborn child—and this is like the level of tzaddikim, in whom there is no evil; and Parshas Parah, which the Torah calls a sin-offering, and which atones for the Golden Calf—is the avodah of teshuvah.

And regarding this they said, "one does not interrupt between Parah and HaChodesh," that there must be a connection between the two parshiyos, to teach that even tzaddikim must perform teshuvah.

And this is also the inner interpretation of the words of the Midrash (mentioned above in section 2): "Regarding this it is said 'This is the statute of the Pesach,' and regarding this it is said 'This is the statute of the Torah,' and you do not know which statute is greater... Which is greater? The Parah, for those who eat the Pesach need it."

The debate "which statute is greater"—Pesach or Parah—is in accordance with the two views in the Gemara, whether complete tzaddikim are greater than baalei teshuvah, or whether in the place where baalei teshuvah stand, complete tzaddikim cannot stand. And the Midrash concludes that the Parah is greater because "those who eat the Pesach need it," which means that teshuvah is greater, because even tzaddikim (those who eat the Pesach) need to perform teshuvah (Parah).

5. Based on this, one can explain the disagreement between Rabbi Levi and Rabbi Chama bar Chanina mentioned above (regarding the reason for the law that one does not interrupt between Parah and HaChodesh) according to the inner dimension of the matters:

Regarding this matter, that even tzaddikim must perform teshuvah, we find two times: a) The teshuvah of the tzaddikim in their avodah now (in this time). b) Moshiach will come to bring the tzaddikim to teshuvah, for this elevation, that tzaddikim "will merit the level of teshuvah"—"will be only after the coming of Moshiach".

And one can say that these are two modes in the teshuvah of the tzaddikim:
The teshuvah of the tzaddikim in this time is teshuvah "over the distance," for even though we are speaking of tzaddikim in whom there is no sin or iniquity (and in whom there is no evil at all), nevertheless, since they are souls in bodies, they are "distant" from Hashem relative to the closeness of their souls before they descended below, as is written in the Tanya that even "a complete tzaddik who serves Hashem with fear and great love in delights will not reach the level of his attachment to Him... before his descent into this material world, not in its entirety nor in part, and there is no value or comparison between them at all... for the body cannot bear it, etc."

And more than this, he wrote in the Tanya that even "the soul of... a complete tzaddik who serves Hashem with fear and love in delights, nevertheless is not nullified in existence entirely... but is a thing in itself, fearing Hashem and loving Him" (and in his words elsewhere, "there is one who loves").

And this is the matter of the teshuvah of the tzaddik, that his distance from Hashem is a matter that troubles and distresses him greatly. For even though his being in this state is not his fault at all, for that is how he was created [and one can say that this very fact, that he was created in a manner of "one who loves," is for his benefit, for specifically through this there is in his avodah the level of free choice, for being an existence, "a thing in itself," he chooses Hashem and His Torah, etc.], nevertheless, due to the greatness of his thirst to be attached to Hashem in perfection, he wants his soul to return to its previous status and position, "and the spirit shall return to the G-d who gave it", in order "

## 211

to attach to Him, may He be blessed, in a wondrous union, just as it was united with Him, may He be blessed, in absolute union" before its descent below.

But this, that Moshiach will come to bring the tzaddikim to teshuvah, is not teshuvah "over the distance"—for then the glory of Hashem will be revealed below as it is above, in a manner that there will be no distance from Hashem (even while man is below as a soul in a body)—rather, the intent in "bringing the tzaddikim to teshuvah" is that they will reach the level of teshuvah that comes with great love for Hashem that is above measurement and limitation ("with all your might"):

In this time, the primary perfection of love "with all your might" is in the baal teshuvah, for since the thirst of their souls to attach to Hashem comes after "his soul was in a dry and desolate land... and distant from the light of Hashem's countenance in the extreme, (and) for this his soul thirsted with greater intensity than the thirst of the souls of the tzaddikim" [and as is known the parable for this from one who is fleeing from death, that this flight is with his entire essence and above all measurements and limitations, and this matter does not apply (as much) to tzaddikim];

But after the coming of Moshiach, when His essence and being, may He be blessed, will be in revelation below, the greatness and intensity of the revelation will effect in man an unlimited elevation, in a manner that even tzaddikim will then reach the perfection of love "with all your might".

6. Now, one of the differences between the two modes mentioned above in the teshuvah of the tzaddikim is:
In the first mode, the teshuvah of the tzaddikim is only a detail in the level of their avodah as tzaddikim, which is the avodah in a state of light and holiness in which there is no evil, for even the teshuvah of the tzaddikim is in order to reach the perfection of this avodah—for since the very fact of his being a soul in a body in this material world is a state of "distance" as above, and is still lacking in the state of absolute holiness, and consequently his avodah of the tzaddik is not in perfection, therefore he is aroused to teshuvah to return his soul to its root and source [and therefore this teshuvah is only before the coming of Moshiach, unlike afterwards, when "I will remove the spirit of impurity from the earth", there is no need for this teshuvah].

This is not the case in the second mode—the level of teshuvah of tzaddikim after the coming of Moshiach (unlimited elevation)—for the avodah of the tzaddikim and the avodah of teshuvah are two different avodos,

## 212

in each of which there is an advantage over the other, and each has what the other lacks: the advantage in the avodah of the tzaddikim is in the matter of drawing down the light of holiness from above (through their avodah in fulfilling the Torah and mitzvos), and the advantage in the avodah of teshuvah is in the (refinement and) elevation of the lower realm.

And this is the explanation of the two views regarding the reason that one does not interrupt between Parah and HaChodesh: according to the view that this is because the reading of Parshas Parah is like a detail in the reading of Parshas HaChodesh, this is a parallel to the first mode in the teshuvah of the tzaddikim, the teshuvah of the tzaddikim in this time, which is teshuvah over the distance, which is a detail in the avodah of the tzaddikim as above; this is not the case according to the view of Rabbi Levi that they are two different readings, only that one should not interrupt between them, which is a parallel to the second mode in the teshuvah of the tzaddikim (as it will be at the coming of Moshiach, Moshiach will come to bring the tzaddikim to teshuvah), where they are two different advantages, only that "one does not interrupt between them," to teach that then even tzaddikim will merit the level of teshuvah.

7. Based on this, one can also explain the hint in the words of Rabbi Levi, who provided a mnemonic for this from the law of the four cups, that "between the third and fourth, one shall not drink":

The difference between the first half of the order of the Pesach Haggadah (in which one drinks the first two cups) and the second half (in which one drinks the last two cups) is: the first half speaks (primarily) of the redemption from Egypt, whereas the second half is related (also) to the redemption that is to come; for it goes without saying that the part of the Haggadah that we say over the fourth cup—which begins from "Pour out Your wrath upon the nations" and continues in the final chapters of Hallel, etc.—is openly related to the redemption to come, but even the meal that we conclude by eating the Afikoman, which is a remembrance of the Pesach (and is called "Tzafun"), is related to the redemption to come.

And this is the inner reason why one does not drink between the third cup and the fourth cup: the third cup, over which one recites Birchas Hamazon, hints at the meal that the Holy One, blessed be He, will make for the tzaddikim in the time to come, and the fourth cup, over which one completes the Hallel and recites the blessing of the song, which are only songs and praises, entirely spiritual, hints at the level of the World to Come (the world of resurrection), in which there is no eating and drinking, but tzaddikim sit and enjoy the radiance of the Shechinah.

And one can say that this corresponds to the two advantages

## 213

in the two modes of avodah (of teshuvah and the avodah of the tzaddikim) in the time to come: the meal that the Holy One, blessed be He, makes for the tzaddikim in the time to come is related to the level of teshuvah, the elevation of the lower realm (as above); and the fact that they sit and enjoy the radiance of the Shechinah and nothing more is the level of the avodah of the tzaddikim, the drawing down and revelation of the light of the Shechinah (in a world that is entirely holiness).

And in the time to come, there will be the connection of the two advantages of the avodah of teshuvah and the avodah of the tzaddikim, Moshiach will come to bring the tzaddikim to teshuvah, and therefore between the third and fourth cup one shall not (interrupt and) drink.

And this is the inner explanation of the mnemonic of Rabbi Levi, that the fact that one does not interrupt between Parah and HaChodesh (which points to the teshuvah of the tzaddikim) is like that which "between the third and fourth, one shall not drink"—to teach that here we are not speaking of the teshuvah of the tzaddikim in this time, but of the connection of the avodah of teshuvah and the avodah of the tzaddikim in the time to come (as above at the end of section 6), Moshiach will come to bring the tzaddikim to teshuvah.


## שמות

## 217

### Shemos

...in the table of the letter Vav. It is impossible that Moshe stood before Pharaoh in the year 2448, for in that very year they offered the Korban Pesach, since according to the opinion of the Seder Olam, the plagues between the two aforementioned events lasted twelve months. The error was introduced due to a lack of clarity, as mentioned in section 3. Therefore, it must be that Moshe’s standing before Pharaoh occurred at the beginning of his eightieth year—that is, in the year 2447.
Ibid. It is impossible that the "cutting off of the three shepherds"—Miriam, Aharon, and Moshe—occurred in the same year of the creation, since Rosh Hashanah fell in the middle...
(From a letter dated 28 Menachem Av, 5717)

By the Grace of Hashem, 2nd day of Rosh Chodesh Adar, 5723
Brooklyn, N.Y.
To the annual dinner of the Beis Sefer L'nearos "Beis Rivkah" in Montreal,
May they live,
Peace and Blessing!

I received with pleasure the notification that the annual banquet of "Beis Rivkah" will take place, G-d willing, on the seventh day of Adar.

7 Adar is, as is well known, the birthday of Moshe Rabbeinu.
